Master thesis
Optimization of Route Planning with
Drone-equipped Vehicles
July 1, 2021
By
Kristine Børsting (s153299)
Kevin Jakobsen (s154240)
==PAGE:2==
Abstract
With the technological advances in recent years, unmanned aerial vehicles, also known as drones, are
receiving an increasing interest for last mile deliveries. The drones have potential to be less polluting
as well as faster to navigate in urban areas than classical delivery trucks. However, their usefulness
is limited by their battery endurance and carrying capacity. These downsides can be addressed by
combining the two types of vehicles in tandems such that the drone can service customers for which it
would be more time consuming or expensive to use the truck while the truck oﬀers a mobile re-fuelling
station for the drone where it can regain battery and pick up parcels. The problem of optimising such
routes with a single truck-drone tandem was ﬁrst introduced by [Murray and Chu, 2015] and is often
referred to as the TSPD (travelling salesman problem with drone) or in the case of multiple tandems
the VRPD (vehicle routing problem with drone).
As the TSPD and VRPD are generalisations of the well studied TSP and VRP they are both NP-hard
problems to solve. As of today, most larger instances of the VRPD have been solved using heuristic
methods. Solving the compact formulations with commercial MIP solvers have not been possible for
instances with more than 12 customers so it is clear that more sophisticated techniques are necessary.
The largest TSPD instances to be solved had 39 customers and were solved with Branch-and-Price in
[Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020]. In our project we transfer the ideas used for solving the TSPD presented
in that paper to the VRPD instead. We have proposed a new compact formulation for the VRPD
which can be solved faster than previous models using a commercial solver. However, larger instances
still cannot be solved within reasonable time. We therefore additionally introduce the ﬁrst Branch-
and-Price procedure for the VRPD in which the Pricing Problem is solved using a labelling algorithm
and improvements such as ng-route relaxation and bi-directional labelling. With this procedure we
manage to solve some instances to optimality with up to 20 customers.

==PAGE:6==
1
Introduction to the VRPD
1.1
Motivation - why use drones for deliveries
In recent years there has been a growing interest in utilising unmanned aerial vehicles, also known
as drones in a more commercial sense. See for instance [Otto et al., 2018] for an overview of more
than 200 articles related to the usage of drones. Larger companies such as Amazon[Wohlsen, 2014],
FedEx[Bermingham, Finbarr, 2014] and DHL[Bonn, 2014] have turned their attention to the use of
unmanned aerial vehicles, for last mile deliveries.
The advantages of using drones as opposed to
ground vehicles include[Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020, Wohlsen, 2014]:
• Drones do not require a human driver
• Drones are not limited by the existing road network and thus urban congestion
• Drones have a smaller transportation cost pr kilometre.
• Drones generally have a higher average speed than ground vehicles.
However, the drones are limited by the smaller carrying capacity and battery endurance which is why
several research projects have gone into examining the beneﬁts of combining both trucks and drones
for deliveries.
[Poikonen et al., 2017] and [Wang et al., 2017] show that there are theoretical beneﬁts to employing
drones for last-mile delivery, by deriving worst case bounds on the potential improvements compared
to the truck-only scenario. Simulations by [Ferrandez et al., 2016] found that a truck-drone tandem
could improve the total delivery time under their assumptions and that the drone was more energy
eﬃcient. Meanwhile [Kitjacharoenchai et al., 2019] also concluded based on a case study and numerical
experiments that the inclusion of drones in commercial logistics reduced the delivery time. Finally,
[Wang et al., 2021] concludes that there is potential for a substantial reduction in CO2 emissions by
employing drones.
Motivated by these positive prospects for truck-drone tandem delivery several research papers have
been published in the last decade which attempt to solve the newly proposed problem of making
deliveries with trucks and drones in tandem. In section 2 we give an overview of the progress made so
far within this ﬁeld.
1.2
Our contributions
In this project we have presented a new compact MIP formulation for the cost minimising vehicle
routing problem with drone (VRPD) inspired by the compact formulation for the time minimising
TSPD presented in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020]. The MIP uses the notion of a linked ﬂow of trucks
and drones instead of sortie excursions to model the movements of the drone as we will discuss in
section 3. Solving the same instances as [Sacramento et al., 2019] on similar hardware we see that our
proposed MIP can be solved in less time than their current model for cost minimising VRPD.
Additionally, we implemented a Branch-and-Price procedure which makes use of up to to 3 levels of
branching. The Pricing Problem is solved using a labelling algorithm with recursions adapted from
[Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] and either ng-route relaxation or bi-directional labelling as improve-
ments to reduce solution times. We also propose heuristic label removal as a way to obtain good but
not guaranteed optimal solutions to the Pricing Problem during column generation and include dual
stabilisation on the Restricted Master Problem to reduce ﬂuctuations. With this Branch-and-Price
procedure we are able to solve some instances with 20 customers which are among the largest instances
of VRPD solved to optimality.
1
==PAGE:7==
2
Literature Review
The problem of route-planning involving drones in tandem with trucks was ﬁrst introduced by
[Murray and Chu, 2015], in which the authors consider a single truck equipped with a single drone.
They initially name the problem the Flying Sidekick Travelling Salesman Problem (FSTSP) but in the
following years the problem is gradually renamed to be called the TSP with Drone(s) (TSPD).
As of the time of this writing, the largest instances of TSPD solved to exact optimality are in
[Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020].
This paper discusses the variants of TSPD explored by more than
10 other papers and how to adapt their model and solution methods to include the various common
assumptions. The presented compact MIP is based on the idea of synchronisation of the ﬂow of truck
and drone. With a commercial MIP solver they are able to solve all tried instances with 9 customers but
they further propose a Branch-and-Price procedure with which they can solve instances with up to 39
customers. In their Branch-and-Price approach the Pricing Problem is solved with dynamic program-
ming and the procedure makes use of the following advanced techniques for improving performance:
ng-route relaxation of the Pricing problem, dual stabilisation and 3-level hierarchical branching.
Moving on from TSPD, [Wang et al., 2017] extended the problem to include more trucks and possibly
more drones per truck, a problem they called the Vehicle Routing Problem with Drones (VRPD).
Notice that the well-studied VRP can be considered a special case of the VRPD if the trucks are not
equipped with any drones. As the VRP is known to be NP-hard then so is the newly proposed VRPD.
In [Wang et al., 2017] and the follow-up paper [Poikonen et al., 2017] the theoretical beneﬁts of using
drones are explored in a worst case analysis. In [Pugliese and Guerriero, 2017] a MILP for a variant
of the VRPD is solved in which each customer has a time window. They solve instances with 5 and 10
customers to optimality using a commercial solver and compare the results to solutions obtained for
the same instances with classical VRPTW.
In the papers [Schermer et al., 2019a, Schermer et al., 2019b] the VRPD is attempted solved heuristi-
cally instead. In [Schermer et al., 2019a] the authors used a hybrid of Variable Neighbourhood Search
and TABU search to solve a variant of the problem in which en route operations were allowed, meaning
that a drone may be launched, and received, not only at nodes but also along the arcs. They used this
approach to tackle instances of up to size 50, and used their MIP formulation to solve instance of up
to size 10 to optimality. In [Schermer et al., 2019b] they introduced a matheuristic to tackle instances,
of a more standard VRPD, of up to size 100, as well as employed their MILP to solve instances of up
to size 11 to optimality.
[Sacramento et al., 2019] also focused on solving the problem heuristically. Speciﬁcally Adaptive Large
Neighbourhood Search (ALNS) was used to solve instances with up to 200 customers. When testing
against a MIP-solver on small instances their implementation of ALNS proved to provide optimal
results very quickly but the MILP itself could only solve instances of up to 12 customers.
In terms of exact solutions [Wang and Sheu, 2019] presented a variation of the VRPD where drones
may only land at speciﬁed docking hubs, not at the customers as is usually assumed. In their paper,
Branch-and-Price is used to solve instances with up to 13 customers and 2 docking hubs to optimality.
They solve the Pricing Problem with an improved pulse algorithm, employ dual stabilisation and
constraint based branching.
[Kitjacharoenchai et al., 2019] denote the problem they solve the multiple travelling salesman with
drone (mTSPD) which diﬀers from the VRPD in that drones may be recovered by any truck, not just
the one that launched it, and no capacities or customer demands are considered. They manage to solve
instances of up to 10 customers using CPLEX and up to 50 customers heuristically with an Adaptive
Insertion algorithm.
The latest, as of this writing, in regards to exact solutions for the VRPD is found in
[Tamke and Buscher, 2021] in which the authors present the ﬁrst Branch-and-Cut approach aimed at
the VRPD. Using this approach they manage to solve instance with up to 30 customers to optimality
within 12 hours, the largest instances of VRPD solved to optimality that we are aware of.
2
==PAGE:8==
Overview:
In Table 1 below we provide a short overview over literature relevant to the problem that we are
focusing on namely the VRPD i.e. with multiple tandems. There are some variations from paper to
paper on what exactly the VRPD is but below we present the typical assumptions. In Table 1 we
have noted important deviations from these standard VRPD assumptions for the papers that focus on
diﬀerent variants of the problem.
An important thing to note from Table 1 is that apart from in [Tamke and Buscher, 2021] previous
VRPD literature has only managed to solve instances with up to 13 customers to optimality.
2.1
VRPD and usual assumptions in the literature
The VRPD is a graph problem similar to classical VRP where a number of routes may start from and
have to subsequently end at the special depot node. The other nodes in the graph represent customers
that all need to be served exactly once by the tandem routes each consisting of a paired truck and
drone. It is mostly assumed that each truck is equipped with only one drone. An illustration of an
instance for the VRPD and a possible solution to it is shown in Figure 1. Typical assumptions on the
inter-relations between truck and drone and optimisation goal for the VRPD are:
• Objective: Min max time i.e. the time at which the last vehicle (truck or drone) returns to the
depot.
• The drones can visit at most one customer alone in a row before returning to the truck. This
reﬂects their inability to carry more than one parcel at a time. Additionally drones can only be
launched to deliver a parcel.
• The drones’ ability to serve a customer is limited by their battery endurance, i.e. there is a limit
on how long a drone can be away from a truck. This includes waiting time if the drone waits for
the truck reﬂecting that it stays airborne while waiting.
• Neither trucks nor drones are allowed to re-visit a customer. Additionally drones are not allowed
to perform loops where they are launched from a truck at a customer and return to the same
truck still waiting at that customer.
• Trucks and drones may leave the depot only once, i.e. when a vehicle enters the end-depot its’
route ends.
• There is a time associated with servicing a customer, launching a drone into the air, and retrieving
a drone from the air, though often some of these times are considered negligible.
Figure 1: Illustration of a VRPD instance and a possible solution. The depot node is often treated as
two nodes, a "start depot", node 0, and an "end depot", node n + 1, where n is the number of
customers.

==PAGE:10==
3
Modelling the VRPD
3.1
The notion of sorties and impact on previous models
Historically the drone’s movement away from the truck has been modelled using triples of customers
⟨i|j|k⟩called sorties. They were ﬁrst introduced in the model by [Murray and Chu, 2015] and the
notation has carried over to the later models in the VRPD literature.
A sortie ⟨i|j|k⟩has been
illustrated in Figure 2 where a drone is launched from node i with its truck present, services customer
j alone and is recovered by the truck again at node k. Throughout this report we will continue the
tradition of showing dotted lines for arcs traversed by a drone and solid lines for arcs traversed by a
truck as in Figure 2.
Figure 2: Illustration of sortie ⟨i|j|k⟩where the drone travels alone from customer i to customer j as
indicated by the dotted line, and subsequently from customer j to customer k. The truck travels via
a diﬀerent path from i to k as illustrated by the broken solid line.
Using sorties to model drone excursions, previous models for the VRPD include decision variables with
at least 3 customer indices such that the number of these variables grow cubically in the instance size.
We have included some examples of these variables from the various cited papers in the table below:
Variables
From
yijk ∈{0, 1}
"Equals 1 if the UAV is launched from node i ∈N0,
travels to node j ∈C (visiting a customer without the truck),
and returns to a truck or the ending depot at node k ∈{N+} :
⟨i, j, k⟩."
[Murray and Chu, 2015]
ykd
iwj ∈{0, 1}
"Binary variables that specify whether a drone d
associated with truck k performs the operation (i, w, j)."
[Schermer et al., 2019b]
yfd
ijk ∈{0, 1}
"=1, if drone d of tandem f ∈F performs drone ﬂight (i, j, k),
and 0 otherwise."
[Tamke and Buscher, 2021]
yv
s ∈{0, 1}
"Binary variable indicating if
the sortie s ∈P is used in the route of truck v ∈V"
[Sacramento et al., 2019]
Table 2: Decision variables from previous models grow cubic in instance size because sorties that are
triples of customers are used to model drone excursions
The idea that research into a more compact model for the VRPD could be beneﬁcial was ﬁrst proposed
by [Schermer et al., 2019b]. Using the ﬂow based model for the TSPD presented by
[Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] as a starting point we aimed to develop a model for the VRPD without
the triple customer index by shifting from modelling the drone movement with sorties to instead use
constrained ﬂow.
5
==PAGE:11==
3.2
The idea of ﬂow and decision variables in our model
A VRPD instance is assumed to consist of a directed graph where each node in the graph represent
one of n customers plus two additional nodes 0, n + 1 which both represent the same physical depot
but the node 0 is referred to as the start-depot and can only be left, not entered, while the node n + 1
is referred to as the end-depot and can only be entered, not left. Each of the customer nodes has an
associated demand and the nodes are connected via directed arcs (i, j). These arcs have associated
travel times for both types of vehicles and associated travel costs for both types of vehicles.
As in the TSPD MILP in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020], the basic idea in our model is the synchroni-
sation of two ﬂows: The ﬂow of drones and the ﬂow of trucks.
We denote the decision variables in the model xT,p
ij , xD,p
ij
∈{0, 1} reﬂecting whether the truck and/or
drone from tandem p traverses arc (i, j). There are thus 2 binary variables per arc per tandem. It is
possible for an arc to have both xT,p
ij
= 1 and xD,p
ij
= 1 in which case the truck from tandem p carries
its drone across the arc. It is not, however, possible for an arc to be traversed by the truck from two
diﬀerent tandems, i.e. to have xT,p
ij
= xT,p′
ij
= 1, p ̸= p′. This is because two trucks cannot enter or
leave the same customer without over-servicing the customer, as we will get back to later.
The values of the x-variables uniquely determine a solution to the VRPD but along with them we
introduce a set of auxiliary variables to aid in modelling. For each node, i, in the graph we introduce
three variables: yT
i , yD
i , yC
i which are binary. These variables reﬂect whether a node is serviced by a
truck alone, a drone alone or a truck and drone combined, respectively. In the combined nodes the
customer is always served by the truck, but a drone is present either because it is being carried by the
truck or it is being retrieved and/or launched at the node.
In Figure 3 we have shown a solution to a VRPD instance where the solution uses one tandem and
the values of the various x and y variables have been illustrated.
Figure 3: Illustration of VRPD solution to an instance with the non-zero xD, xT variables shown and
the y-variable values shown as colour-coding of the nodes.
6
==PAGE:12==
3.3
Assumptions of our MILP
As mentioned, there are variations from paper to paper on the basic assumptions of the VRPD so in
the following sections we go into details with what assumptions we make and why. Before going into
details, we present an overview:
Our VRPD assumptions:
a) Objective: Minimise operational costs.
b) The ﬂeet size is suﬃcient and each truck is paired with one speciﬁc drone.
c) Drone can carry at most one parcel at a time and may only be launched to deliver a parcel.
d) The drone’s battery endurance is limited. If the drone waits for the truck, the battery is still
being exhausted.
e) Neither trucks nor drones are allowed to re-visit a customer. This includes drone sorties starting
and ending at the same customer.
f) A drone may not perform a sortie which begins at the start depot and ends at the end depot
g) Trucks and drones may leave the depot only once, i.e. when a vehicle enters the end-depot its
route ends.
h) There is a vehicle speciﬁc time associated with serving a customer, and a time associated with
launching a drone into the air or retrieving a drone from the air.
i) A drone ending a sortie at the end-depot can be recovered immediately without needing to wait
for its associated truck to also arrive.
j) The truck is considered un-capacitated.
k) The drone has a limited carrying capacity and may not serve customers with a demand which
exceeds this value.
l) The last vehicle must return to the depot before a certain end-of-day time.
3.3.1
Choice of reference paper and objective
In order to best assess the quality of our solution methods developed in this report we wish to solve the
same instances as previous works on VRPD with the same assumptions and objective. Unfortunately,
since the assumptions and objective vary from paper to paper then we cannot compare closely to all
previous works at the same time.
Of the VRPD papers presented in the literature review we found that the assumptions and choice of
objective in [Sacramento et al., 2019] aligned the best with what we think are reasonable for real life
applications. Additionally, the instances solved in that paper are easily accessible and the parameter
values used are clearly stated, so we decided to choose that paper as our main performance reference.
This includes choosing to minimise operational costs as objective instead of minimising the latest return
time or sum of return times as in e.g. [Tamke and Buscher, 2021]. As in [Sacramento et al., 2019] we,
instead of minimising the latest return time, add a constraint on it reﬂecting e.g. the end of a work
day (assumption k) above).
7
==PAGE:13==
3.3.2
Launch, recovery and service times
As in our performance reference paper, [Sacramento et al., 2019], we assume that there are service
times sD, sT associated with serving customers and that these times may diﬀer for the truck and
drone. We also assume that there are separate time requirements for launching and recovering a
drone. Both processes require the full attention of the truck driver who cannot simultaneously service
a customer and the drone’s battery is being exhausted during both the launching and recovering.
In the paper [Tamke and Buscher, 2021] it is assumed that there is a time associated with launching a
drone but not retrieving it and both processes are assumed autonomous such that the truck driver is
able to perform a customer service at the same time. We found this to be a questionable assumption
since it would require a fully automated machinery to be able to supply the drone with the correct
parcel and change its battery yet still ﬁt inside a truck without taking up enough space for it to be
necessary to consider truck capacity.
If a drone ﬂies to the end-depot un-accompanied we assume, like in [Sacramento et al., 2019], that
recovering of the drone can start immediately upon arrival. The drone’s battery has to last for the
recovering but it does not have to wait for the truck to arrive also before starting to be recovered.
3.3.3
Battery endurance
Like previous works on the VRPD we assume that the drone has a limited battery endurance which
constraints the amount of time allowed to pass from it is launched till it is recovered. In
[Sacramento et al., 2019] the battery endurance speciﬁcally has to be suﬃcient to last from before
launching begins and until after recovering has been performed. If a drone needs to wait for its asso-
ciated truck at a rendezvous point, battery is assumed to still be exhausted during the waiting time.
In [Tamke and Buscher, 2021] it is not assumed that the drone drains battery while waiting. Trans-
ferring this to real life applications it would mean that the drone is either able to land and wait or
is hovering in a way that only uses insigniﬁcant amounts of energy while waiting. We do not wish to
assume that the drone can always land while waiting because it seems likely that it may be vulnerable
to theft or sabotage during that time. Additionally it is not certain that there is always suﬃcient
space and even ground for the drone to land on at a customer. These concerns were also raised by
[Wang and Sheu, 2019] who, like [Sacramento et al., 2019] and us, assume waiting time to exhaust the
battery.
Model limitations
There is a limitation on the real life scenarios that our model, as well as the one from
[Sacramento et al., 2019], can properly reﬂect. Namely that it is not possible to service a rendezvous
customer in-between recovering and launching a drone at the customer. This can potentially exclude
very speciﬁc tandem routes which are only feasible if the drone can be kept at a customer without
exerting battery between ending a sortie and starting a new one.
In Appendix E we have provided an in-depth discussion of how we track time and why this issue
occurs as well as a comparison to the model in [Sacramento et al., 2019] to ensure that we constrain
the battery endurance in the same manner. In section 4.2 we present a variation of our MIP which
allows the drone to wait on the ground between being recovered and re-launched which we later test
on the same instances as our main model.
3.3.4
Forbidden loops
In [Sacramento et al., 2019] any drone loop is forbidden. Loop refers to a sortie which starts and ends
at the same node and in the case of our reference paper this also includes sorties that begin at the
start-depot and end at the end-depot. This is a common assumption for the VRPD that we also
include in our model, even though the latter loops would probably rarely be used in optimal solutions.
8
==PAGE:14==
We expect so since there exist no arcs out of the end-depot so a drone that performs one of these loops
may only service a single customer which would be a poor use of resources.
3.3.5
Truck capacity
We assume the truck capacity to be suﬃcient based on the discussion in [Sacramento et al., 2019] where
it was mentioned that with small package delivery it is more likely for the time constraints to be binding
than the capacity constraints. In the discussion a truck capacity of 1300 kg is assumed and a usual
customer demand of 2.27 kg or less, so for instances as small as the ones solved in this report it becomes
unnecessary to consider a capacity limit. [Tamke and Buscher, 2021] also omits capacity constraints
on the trucks in their model for the VRPD based on the conclusion in [Sacramento et al., 2019].
In section 4.1 we present a modiﬁcation of our model which does include truck capacity and compare
it to the main MIP.
3.3.6
Pairing of trucks and drones
The ﬂeet of trucks and drones considered in our model is assumed homogeneous but with each truck
paired to a speciﬁc drone in "tandem" pairs. This is a recurring assumption in VRPD literature along
with the assumption that any vehicle may only interact with the other vehicle it has been paired with
i.e. a drone is not allowed to be launched by one truck and then recovered by another truck.
In order to best compare our model to previous works we chose to include these assumptions in our
model as well. However, in section 4.3 we experimented with a modiﬁed model in which trucks and
drones are not paired but may only still interact with one other vehicle at a time.
3.4
Notation: Parameters and variables in the model
This section presents the notation used for parameters and variables in our model. Some of these have
already been presented during the assumptions but this is a full overview.

3.5.1
Explanation of the model
The objective in (1a) is to minimise the operational costs of using the drones and trucks for transporta-
tion. In particular the operational costs are calculated as a cost for the truck to traverse a particular
arc times whether the arc is traversed by each truck plus a cost for the drone to traverse an arc times
whether any drone traverses the arc alone. We assume that when the truck and drone traverse an arc
together the drone is not ﬂying but is carried by the truck and is thus not expending any resources.
Constraints (1b)-(1c) ensure ﬂow conservation of trucks and drones respectively.
Constraints (1d)-(1e) link the y-variables to the x-variables such that each customer is marked as either
a "truck customer", "drone customer" or "combined customer" based on what ﬂows into the node.
A consequence of these constraints combined with (1h) is that each customer can be entered by at
most one truck, at most one drone or at most one truck-drone pair. For instance it is impossible to
have xT1
ij = xT2
i′j = 1 on two arcs going into the same customer. The depots will always be marked as
"combined customers" due to constraint (1f).
Constraint (1g) makes sure that for each tandem the number of trucks that leave the start-depot, the
number of drones that leave the start-depot and the number of each that enter the end-depot should
all be equal and at most 1.
Constraint (1h) ensures that each customer is served exactly once.
Constraints (1i)-(1l) keep track of time and eliminate sub tours as follows:
If a truck travels from i ∈C0 to j ∈C+ then (1i) is binding, otherwise M is chosen large enough
for the constraint to be superﬂuous. In the case it is binding it ensures that aj is at least the time i
was left (time di) plus the truck travel time to j and the truck service time sT at j plus a potential
recovering time if a drone ﬂies into j unaccompanied.
Similarly constraint (1j) is binding only if a drone travels from i ∈C0 to j ∈C+. In that case it sets
the time aj to be di plus the drone travel time from i to j plus a potential drone service times if the
drone services customer j or plus a recovering time if the drone serviced customer i because then it
will be rendezvousing with the truck at j. In case the drone is carried by the truck from i to j the left
hand side of (1i) will be larger than (1j).
Together the two constraints (1i) + (1j) ensure that the availability time at j is always the latest time
that both vehicles are ready for a potential launch. In case a drone leaves a node i ∈C0 constraint
(1k) is binding. It adds the launch time to the departure time of i in case the leaving drone services
the next customer alone.
Finally, the constraint (1l) ensures that for all nodes, the departure time is at least the same as the
arrival time, even if no drone is launched at the node.
Constraint (1m) implements the capacity of one parcel for each drone by making sure that the drone
from tandem p can only travel between any two nodes if at least one of the two nodes is visited by the
truck from p.
Constraint (1n) forbids drone loops from the start depot to the end-depot as discussed in section 3.3.4.
Loops from a customer node to itself are forbidden as a consequence of constraint (1e) and (1h). Loops
from start-depot to start-depot or end-depot to end-depot are impossible as it requires arcs not in A.
Constraint (1o) forbids any drone from servicing customers which cannot be serviced by a drone due
e.g. to their demand exceeding the maximum carrying capacity of the drone.
Constraints (1p)-(1q) keep track of the drone’s battery endurance so it does not run out. The former,
(1p), says that if any drone travels from node i to j to k and services node j alone then the battery
has to last from the time it is launched at node i until it is at node k, node k has been serviced and
the drone has been recovered. This constraint does not apply to k = n + 1, because when a drone
arrives at the depot it does not have to wait for its truck but can be recovered straight away which is
why constraint (1q) sets this constraint separately.
Constraint (1r) sets the values of the xDalone
ij
-variables. This constraint and the binary behaviour of
the variables will be discussed in the next section.
Constraint (1s) sets the maximum duration of a work day by upper bounding the availability time of
13
==PAGE:19==
the end-depot i.e. the time at which the last vehicle returns.
The remaining four lines of the model are the variable deﬁnitions.
Notice that in the model (1), the number of decision variables and some of the constraints grow linearly
in the number of available vehicles K since p ∈P = {1, ..., K}. The model therefore beneﬁts from a
good estimate on K which is only as large as need be.
3.5.2
Binary behaviour of xDalone-variables
In constraint (1r) the xDalone
ij
-variable is forced to be at least 1 if a drone travels from node i to j
and either i or j is serviced by the drone. Note that at most one drone can travel on the same arc
due to (1e)+(1h).
Both of these conditions are met if and only if a drone traverses the arc (i, j)
unaccompanied by a truck.
Since the xDalone
ij
variables appear positively in the objective function, the solver will always want to
minimise the value of these variables, and thus when lower bounded by 1 they are simply set to 1.
The RHS of the constraints (1r) can never take a value larger than 1 since at most one of yD
i
and
yD
j
can be 1 if xD
ij = 1 as a consequence of constraint (1m). Meanwhile, the RHS will also always
be integer because of the binary behaviour of all the variables present so it cannot take values in the
interval ]0, 1[. In case at least one of the conditions are not met, i.e. if either none of the customers
are drone customers or no drones travel from i to j then the RHS will be 0 or less. In this case the
lower bound of 0 in (1u) means that xDalone
ij
is set to 0. Thus, in conclusion, the xDalone-variables will
behave as if binary if they are deﬁned on R+.
3.5.3
Big M:
The big M used in (1) can be set to M = Tmax because it is known that no availability and departure
time can be larger than this in a feasible solution1.

4
Model variations
4.1
Modelling truck capacity
In the model in (1) we did not constrain the truck capacity as it was not expected to be a binding
constraint for the small instances we expect to solve. However, if one was to solve larger instances
or instances where the customer demands are closer in size to the truck capacity then it would be
necessary to include a constraint on capacity.
Denoting the truck capacity Q it could e.g. be included in (1) by introducing the variables:
qi ≥0
∀i ∈V
These variables reﬂect the capacity used by a truck upon leaving node i so at time di. Along with
these variables the following two constraints would have to be added:
qi + demj +
X
l∈C
deml · xDalone
jl
≤qj + 2Q

1 −
X
p∈P
xTp
ij


∀(i, j) ∈A, j ̸= n + 1
qi ≤Q
∀i ∈C
The ﬁrst constraint is only active if a truck drives from node i to node j, otherwise the term 2Q makes
it trivially satisﬁed. In case xTp
ij = 1 for some tandem p (recall that it is still at most possible for one
truck to traverse the same arc) then the constraint is binding and sets qj as follows: It is equal to qi
plus the demand of customer j plus the demand of any customer that the drone serves after being
launched at customer j if a drone is launched. For this constraint we give the end depot a demand of
demn+1 = 0
The second constraint simply ensures that the truck capacity is never exceeded.
4.2
A more realistic battery
As discussed in section 3.3.3 it may be beneﬁcial to model the battery consumption of the drone in
a way that allows the drone to be recovered before it has to wait for the truck to service a customer.
Allowing this introduces some slightly more complicated constraints to the model in (1) which we will
introduce here.
First of all this modiﬁcation will make use of an extended set of variables for keeping track of time
and order, these are (replacing ai and di in (1)):
Added variables:

Constraint (2c) says that if a truck travels from node i ∈C0 to j ∈C+ then the time that the truck
arrives at j is the time it left i plus the travel time. Constraint (2d) works similarly but for the drone.
If a drone is carried from i to j by the truck then aD
j will be the time the drone could have arrived if
it had ﬂown, but this has no impact on av

Constraints (2e)-(2f) set the arrival time of all vehicles at a node i ∈C+, av

aT

0 = av
0 = d0 = 0.
Constraint (2g) sets the variable SFi to 0 if it is not possible for the truck to fully serve the customer
before a potential drone arrives and allows it to be 1 otherwise.
Constraints (2h)-(2j) set the departure time of the three types of customer nodes that exist. The big
M in these constraints can be chosen as M = Tmax like in (1). The departure time of the end-depot
is meaningless and for the start-depot it is 0 so these nodes are excluded from the constraints. More
speciﬁcally, constraint (2h) sets the departure time of all nodes that are visited by a truck alone to be
the arrival time of the truck plus the truck’s service time. Similarly constraint (2i) sets the departure
time of a customer visited by the drone alone. Finally, constraint (2j) sets the departure time of a
customer visited by both the truck and drone from a tandem to be av
i plus the service time of the
truck if the truck did not have suﬃcient time to serve the customer before the drone arrived. Added
to these are also the recover time if the drone is recovered and the launch time if the drone is launched
at the node.
The end-of day constraint (1s) should be changed to use the new arrival time instead of the old
availability time:

4.3
Un-pairing the tandems
Since we assume a homogeneous ﬂeet of drones and trucks it could be interesting to allow drones to
pick up parcels at any available truck, not necessarily a speciﬁc one each time
Therefore, we constructed a modiﬁed version of the model in (1) where each variable no longer has
a speciﬁc p-index. The decision variables in this modiﬁed problem are denoted xT
ij, xD
ij ∈{0, 1} with
no p-index and reﬂect whether any truck or any drone traverses the arc (i, j) (recall from previously
that even in (1) at most one vehicle of each type could ever traverse an arc so we would never need to
model a xT
ij or xD
ij variable larger than 1.).
Assumptions in the modiﬁed model:
We assume that the ﬂeet size for trucks and drones are both K so there are still one drone per truck in
the ﬂeet. To maintain realism we constrain the number of drones that can be present at a node to 1.
This is to ensure that the truck driver has to interact with at most one drone at a time, including any
drone that may have been carried by the truck into a node. Another constraint which we now enforce
more clearly is that a drone can only be launched with the purpose of serving a customer, i.e. it is
not allowed to launch a drone from one truck which ﬂies directly to another truck without serving a
customer in-between. In reality there is nothing wrong with allowing this type of re-allocation even if
it would probably rarely be beneﬁcial since there would be an operational cost for the drone to ﬂy and
it would not help towards the goal of serving all customers. However, this form of drone movement
does not compare well with any other VRPD models that only allow the drone to perform sorties or
be carried. It is also important for the simplicity of our modiﬁed model that it can be assumed that
any drone launched always carries a parcel to deliver to a drone customer. If a drone was allowed to
ﬂy between two combined customers unaccompanied by a truck the xDalone
ij
-variables as deﬁned by
constraint (1r) in its current form would be set to 0 and thus no operational costs for the excursion
would be noted in the objective. Not including this assumption would therefore require a larger eﬀort
to re-model how we keep track of arcs traversed by unaccompanied drones.
The concrete model changes:
Most of the changes from model (1) are self-evident and involve removing the tandem indices p and the
sums over all tandems. However constraint (1m) has been rewritten to constraint (3m) instead. The
new constraint ensures that a drone is only allowed to traverse arc (i, j) if at least one of the nodes i, j
are combined customers but it is not required that they are served by a truck from a speciﬁc tandem,
just any truck.
We have also ensured the aforementioned property that drones are only launched with the purpose of
serving a customer by adding constraint (3t). It speciﬁcally forbids a drone from travelling between
two combined customers if it is not accompanied by a truck.

A concern regarding not pairing the trucks and drones is the eﬀect it can have on robustness in real
life applications. If a drone at some point changes truck during the solution but the ﬁrst truck suﬀers
delays then the second truck will have to wait for the delayed drone. Therefore, allowing the drones
to change trucks also allows for delays in some truck routes to aﬀect other truck routes.

About the instances:
Throughout this report we will be solving some of the instances that were also solved in
[Sacramento et al., 2019] and with the same parameter values (see Table 3).
The instances are named using three identiﬁers ”n.2d.x” where the ﬁrst identiﬁer n states the number of
customer nodes, 2d represents the size of the grid where the customers have been randomly placed upon
generating the instance by drawing a 1st and 2nd coordinate from a uniform distribution U]−d, d[. The
last identiﬁer just says which of the 4 instances generated with the same n and d-value this instance is,
so x = 1, 2, 3, 4 as there are 4 instances per "category". The customers have been assigned a demand
each by ﬁrst drawing a random number, P, from U[0, 1[ and then depending on the value of P their
demand has been drawn from one of two uniform distributions:
demi ∈
(
U[0, 2.27]
P < 0.86
U[2.27, 68]
otherwise
In [Sacramento et al., 2019] the authors deﬁne a set of customers, C′, which can be served by the drone
as their demand does not exceed the drone carrying capacity. In our MILP we instead consider the
complementary set of customers C
D who cannot be served by the drone using the same criteria, i.e.
that the customer demand exceeds the drone carrying capacity.
Value of K:
The value of K in (1) should be chosen large enough to not aﬀect the optimal solution but as we
already noted the computational complexity of the model also increases with K so it should not be
chosen unnecessarily large. In the following experiments we set K = 3 and if for any instance the
optimal solution found uses all 3 tandems we plan to re-solve it with a higher K estimate. However
none of the instances we have solved used more than 2 tandems, which we think makes sense because
the instances are fairly small. To put the sizes into perspective consider the following relationships:
With a grid size of 2d = 20 miles (the largest grid size considered) it will take the truck 20
3560 ≈34
minutes to travel from one side of the grid to the other. This is about one 14th of the full day’s length.
20
==PAGE:26==
Meanwhile, the maximum length that the drone can ﬂy on a full battery (reserving some of the battery
for serving, launching and recovering) is:
(30 −1 −1 −1) · 50
60 = 22.5 miles
Experiments:
We solved the model (1) on these instances experimenting with diﬀerent variations of the MILP. First
experimenting with the variable domains and then with the problem variations presented in section 4.
In our reference paper, [Sacramento et al., 2019], the instances are solved on a Huawei XH620 V3
computer with Intel Xeon Processor 2660v3 (2.60 GHz) using CPLEX 12.7. We used the same type of
hardware (using all cores) on the DTU HPC cluster when solving the instances with our MILP using
CPLEX 12.10.
5.2
Experimenting with variable domains
We have previously discussed how the y- and xDalone-variables do not have to be deﬁned as binary
variables but will behave binary as a consequence of the binary x-variables even if deﬁned on R+.
In this ﬁrst set of experiments we solved the small instances from [Sacramento et al., 2019] with dif-
ferent variable domains for the y- and xDalone-variables.
The results of the experiments are shown in Table 4 where for each instance we have reported the
objective value of the optimal solution found and the average solution time averaged over 5 attempts
per instance. The ﬁrst two columns are the results reported in [Sacramento et al., 2019] for comparison.
The rows labelled "total" and "subtotal" include the aggregated run times for all instances and all
instances with the same number of customers. We imposed a time limit of 1 hour on each instance.
Only instance "12.20.2" was not solved within the time limit for all versions so it has been omitted
from the subtotals and total run times as indicated by (-).

==PAGE:28==
From Table 4 we see that the optimal solution values to the instances from [Sacramento et al., 2019]
obtained when solving our model in (1) for all combinations of variable deﬁnitions agree with the
known optimal solutions from our reference paper. Additionally, we manage to solve all instances in a
10th of the time indicating that the ﬂow based model with fewer variables is in fact computationally
advantageous. For the instances of size 6 only, our model is generally slower to reach an optimum than
[Sacramento et al., 2019] but the scalability of our model is clearly better as it is already much faster
in general on the instances of size 10.
A surprising observation from Table 4 is that it improves the run time considerably to deﬁne the y-
variables as binary instead of exploiting that they can be deﬁned as continuous. This is surprising to
us because we would expect more binary variables to lead to a larger branch and bound tree in the
worst case. By deﬁning them as continuous variables instead we would have expected fewer branch
and bound iterations to be necessary and thus achieve a faster termination. However, this is not what
we see in reality.
We can think of two possible explanations for this unexpected behaviour:
1) By allowing the solver to branch on the y-variables instead of just the x-variables it may be possible
to make better branching decisions. From constraint (1h) we can see that setting one y-variable to
1 also sets two other y-variables to 0 such that more y-variables become integer. Additionally, from
constraints (1d)-(1e) we can see that setting either yD
i
= 1 or yT
i = 1 for some i ∈C forces all x-
variables of the opposite vehicle type into node i to be 0. Therefore branching on a single y-variable
can aﬀect a multitude of x-variables to also become integer.
2) Informing the solver that the y-variables are binary may allow it to exploit certain cuts it knows,
to improve the LP-bound and reach faster termination.
From Table 4 we also note that redeﬁning the xDalone-variables to integer, leads to a slight decrease in
performance both if y ∈R+ and if y ∈{0, 1}.
These variables do not interact with each other in as simple a manner as the y-variables do in constraint
(1h) so setting one of them to an integer value does not automatically guarantee integrality of other
xDalone-variables. Their interaction with the x-decision variables is also less impactful. Branching on
one variable, xDalone
ij
, forces the RHS of one constraint (1r) to be either 0 or 1 but this still allows
non-integer values for the x- and y-variables on the RHS.
5.3
Model variations
In these experiments we implemented the model variations from section 4 and solved the same in-
stances as previously in order to compare with the results obtained using the model in (1). In all
model variations the y-variables have been deﬁned as binary and the xDalone-variables as non-negative
continuous based on the insight from the previous experiments.

Table 5: Comparison of completion times when solving diﬀerent variations of our MILP model. Run
times shown in seconds and as average over 5 attempts per instance. For instance 10.10.3 (marked in
cyan) a diﬀerent optimal solution is found for the last variation.

==PAGE:30==
5.3.1
Truck capacity
The second column in Table 5 contains the results obtained with the modiﬁcations from section 4.1
where the truck is capacitated with a capacity of Q = 1300 kg as in [Sacramento et al., 2019]. As
discussed already, the truck capacity is not expected to have an impact on the solution to the instances
we solve currently that have small customer demands and few nodes. This is also what we observe from
Table 5 since none of the optimal solution values have been aﬀected by the modiﬁcation. In Appendix
D we have reported the summed customer demand for all customers in each instance solved and they
are all well below Q, none of them even exceeding 200.
Looking at the solution times, the modiﬁed MILP manages to solve all instances in almost 5 minutes
less than before. There may be no good explanation for this behaviour but our best guess is that
the capacity constraints are more easily recognised as sub tour elimination constraints than the time
constraints which involve two variables pr customer instead of 1. It may be the case that the solver is
then able to recognise the structure of these added constraints in a way that helps it to exploit some
tricks speciﬁc for routing problems or similar.
5.3.2
A more realistic battery
The model solved in the third column in Table 5 contains the modiﬁcations from section 4.2. This is
where the constraints relating to battery endurance were changed to reﬂect that the drone could be
recovered at a customer before the truck serves the customer and not exhaust the battery during this
service.
The optimal objective values obtained are the same as before the modiﬁcation. Thus, the modiﬁcation
has not lead to better solutions in any of the instances solved. Trying to make sense of this observation
we notice that the only case where the modiﬁcation can lead to a better optimal solution is if this
better optimal solution involves a drone sortie which expends almost the full battery. More speciﬁcally,
the sortie needs to end at a customer node at which the truck does not arrive early enough to serve
the customer before the drone’s arrival. Additionally, the time that the drone spends away from the
truck must be suﬃciently long such that if the drone has to wait for the truck to serve the customer at
the rendezvous before being recovered it runs out of battery, but it does not run out if the truck ﬁrst
recovers the drone and then serves the customer.
Recall from previous calculations that the drone can travel 22.5 miles on a full battery and the largest
grid size considered spans 20 miles from one side to the other.
Knowing that the inter-customer
distances in our instances are not very large compared to the battery endurance we think it makes
sense that the 2 minutes extra battery endurance, that could be utilised if the drone does not have to
wait for truck service, is insuﬃcient to obtain better solutions on these instances.
While the modiﬁcations from section 4.2 did not allow any better solutions, it did make an impact on
the solution times. As can be seen in Table 5 the collective solver time to solve all instances with the
modiﬁcation has increased by 60 %.
5.3.3
Un-pairing the tandems
The last column of Table 5 contains the results obtained with the modiﬁcations described in section
4.3, namely where we have removed the tandem index from the trucks and drones.
We expect this model to be faster to solve than the model (1) since it has fewer x-decision variables
and it is expected to give solutions that are at least as good as model (1) since it is a "relaxation"
where the requirement that drones need to be recovered/launched by a speciﬁc truck has been relaxed.
This is also exactly what we see in Table 5. The modiﬁed model is solved nearly 3 times faster, and
there is in fact a single instance in which it yields a better solution, namely instance "10.10.3". We
have illustrated the optimal solution to the instance for the original model (1) and for the modiﬁed
25
==PAGE:31==
model in Figures 5 and 6 respectively. The original solution has an objective value of 2.553 whereas
the solution to the modiﬁed model has an objective value of 2.542. In the latter solution, one of the
drones is recovered and launched by both trucks throughout its route.
Figure 5: The optimal solution to instance 10.10.3 using model (1). The truck-drone tandems have
been colour-coded such that one tandem has solid black lines for the truck and dotted grey lines for
the drone and the other has dark/light blue.
Figure 6: Solution to instance 10.10.3 with the modiﬁed model in which trucks and drones are not
paired. Arcs traversed by a truck shown as black solid lines and arcs traversed by a drone shown as
dotted grey. Notice that the drone in the lower right corner interacts with both the truck performing
tour depot →1 →depot as well as the truck performing tour depot →4 →9 →5 →depot.
26
==PAGE:32==
The fact that we only see one instance in which the relaxation gives a better solution is somewhat
understandable. In order to exploit the relaxation, it needs to be beneﬁcial for a drone to change
which truck it interacts with. In instance 10.10.3 we see that this happens because the truck route
depot →1 →depot only allows for an associated drone to visit 2 customers alone but there are 3
customers in the lower right corner so it is beneﬁcial for the other truck to recover and launch the
drone one more time such that it can visit all 3 customers.
Additionally, it needs to be feasible for the drone to change truck which requires some quite speciﬁc
conditions since each truck may only interact with one drone at a time. Say we label one truck a and
one truck b. Similarly the drone that initially interacts with truck a we label drone a and the other
drone b. For drone a to interact with truck b it needs to be the case that drone b is performing a
suﬃciently long sortie such that truck b can visit an additional customer before the rendezvous with
drone b. This customer that truck b is visiting alone then needs to be suﬃciently close to drone a for
it to be able to reach the truck at the end of its sortie and use it as recharging station before heading
out on a new sortie.
6
Column generation approach
In previous literature on VRPD related problems it has become clear that solving the compact MIP
model with a commercial solver leads to small solvable instances and that more sophisticated exact
techniques are necessary to solve larger instances [Wang and Sheu, 2019, Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020,
Tamke and Buscher, 2021]. Therefore, we now present a column generation approach based on a set
partitioning (SP) formulation of the VRPD instead. The VRPD can be formulated as a set partitioning
problem as follows:
Set Partitioning (SP) formulation:
min

Where r ∈Ωis the set of all feasible truck-drone tandem routes with associated costs cr and parameters
air equal to 1 if route r services customer i and 0 if not. The decision variables in the SP are the
binary variables ψr reﬂecting whether a certain tandem route is chosen in the optimal solution. The
objective is to minimise operational costs and the constraints are to use at most K tandems as well as
to service each customer exactly once. The associated dual variables for these constraints have been
denoted λ0 and λi,
i ∈C as shown with magenta.
Since the number of feasible routes in Ωmay be vast, only a subset of routes will be included and these
will be added iteratively using column generation to solve the LP relaxation of (4) also known as the
Master Problem (MP):

Master Problem (MP) - LP relaxation of SP:

Notice that as we relax the binary variables ψr from the domain ψr ∈{0, 1} to the continuous domain
0 ≤ψr ≤1 the upper bound of 1 is redundant as a consequence of constraint (5c) which already
ensures that ψr ≤1 ∀r ∈Ω.
In a given column generation iteration the MP with a restricted set of columns, R ⊆Ω, is solved to
LP optimality. This problem is called the Restricted Master Problem (RMP) and since it only
has access to a subset of all columns the solution obtained gives a primal bound (upper bound in our
case) on the optimal solution to the MP.
The purpose of column generation is to add columns to the RMP until the optimal solution to the
RMP is also an optimal solution to the MP. Primal optimality of an RMP solution w.r.t. the MP is
monitored through dual feasibility w.r.t. the DMP (Dual Master Problem). Using LP theory the dual
problem of the non-restricted MP is:
Dual Master Problem (DMP):

Columns missing from Ωmeans that the associated dual constraints (6b) are missing from the DMP.
If the current RMP solution is not primal optimal w.r.t. the MP then the dual solution will violate at
least one of these constraints. The goal of column generation is to iteratively identify such violated dual
constraints and add the associated primal variables/columns to the RMP until reaching an optimal
MP solution.
In practice how these violated dual constraints are found is using the Sub-Problem, also known as
Pricing Problem:

6.1
Column generation framework:
To summarise how the MP is solved by iteratively solving the RMP and PP we present the column
generation framework in its simplest form:
Algorithm 1: Simple column generation framework
1 Generate an initial set of columns such that the RMP has feasible solutions.
2 while Improving columns exist do
3
Solve RMP to obtain dual solution λ
4
Solve PP with dual solution λ to ﬁnd columns with negative reduced cost
5
if Columns were found then
6
Add them to the RMP
7
else
8
Break: No more improving columns exist.
9 Solve RMP to obtain primal solution, this solution is also the optimal MP solution.
We will later present how we have modiﬁed this simple framework to improve the procedure.
6.1.1
Initial columns:
The initial set of columns needed for starting column generation could be generated with, for instance,
a construction heuristic. However, as the focus of this project has been on the exact methods used to
solve the VRPD we have not invested time in developing a specialised heuristic for generating initial
columns. Instead we iteratively call the pricing problem with a constructed dual solution λ which
motivates visiting as many customers as possible but without re-visiting customers already served by
other routes. More speciﬁc details on this process is included in Appendix B.
6.1.2
Number of columns added
In the framework in Algorithm 1 we mention columns in plural from the PP. Solving the PP from
(7) with a MIP solver will only return one column of minimal reduced cost unless special settings are
made. However, as we progress to solve the PP with dynamic programming we enable the Pricing
Problem to return multiple improving columns instead of only the best.
6.2
Dual stabilisation
It is a well known issue with column generation that the dual solution λ may ﬂuctuate signiﬁcantly from
iteration to iteration which can lead to columns of poor quality being generated based on extreme dual
values. The consequences of this is a slow convergence where many columns are generated but upon
achieving optimality mostly columns generated in the last few iterations are used. This undesirable
behaviour may be triggered by a degenerate solution space or a large number of feasible combinations.
For instance, for the CVRP it has shown to result in slow convergence if each vehicle has to service
a large number of customers[Pessoa et al., 2008]. As the CVRP is closely related to the VRPD we
expect a similar behaviour.
To combat the issue of ﬂuctuating dual solutions, dual stabilisation may be added to the master
problem. In our framework we have experimented with BoxPen dual stabilisation which, in short, tries
to force the dual variables to take values inside a speciﬁed box and penalises the objective proportional
to the distance to the box if they do not stay inside it[Du Merle et al., 1999].
30
==PAGE:36==
This stabilisation has only been applied to the dual variables λi associated with constraints (4c). The
dual variable λ0 associated with constraint (4b) can be considered as a shadow price reﬂecting how
much is to gain if K was to be increased. However, we always choose K to be "large enough" as we
have assumed the ﬂeet size to be suﬃcient and not a limiting factor so this constraint is not expected
to be binding and thus the dual variable is expected to be 0 always.
Comments on over-serving customers
In [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] the sign in what corresponds to our constraint (4c) is changed from
” = ” to ” ≥” to halve the domain of the associated dual variables λi. For the VRPD with multiple
truck-drone tandems we cannot make use of this trick as we will illustrate through the following
example: Consider the instance depicted in Figure 7. Solving our MIP from (1) with the parameter
values presented in Table 3 gives the following optimal solution and corresponding objective value:
Truck visits
Drone visits
0 →1 →5 →6
0 →2 →1 →4 →5 →3 →6
Objective value: 3.680
Nodes 0 and 6 are the both the depot, each customer has to be visited exactly once and the bold
customers indicate that the corresponding vehicle is serving that customer. The solution is also shown
in Figure 8a.
Now allowing over-serving customers in the restricted master problem and solving the LP relaxation
of the SP formulation using column generation we obtain the following optimal solution and objective
value. No changes have been made to the Pricing Problem so cycle-elimination is still present here to
forbid the same route to over-serve a customer. Notice that the new solution obtained is integer and
thus not only optimal for the LP relaxation but for the SP itself:
Weight of route in solution
Truck visits
Drone visits
ψ = 1
0 →1 →6
0 →2 →1 →5 →6
ψ = 1
0 →1 →6
0 →3 →1 →4 →6
Objective value: 1.618
The above solution is illustrated in Figure 8b and is signiﬁcantly better than the previous. The decrease
in operational costs stems from the fact that it is favourable to visit customer 1 twice since this allows
two separate trucks to launch their drones from this customer thus avoiding the higher operational
costs of needing to serve the further oﬀcustomers with a truck. As it is not just a solution to the MP
but also to the SP it should satisfy all the basic assumptions but customer 1 is served twice which
contradicts the problem deﬁnition that each customer should be served exactly once (see section 2.1).
With this example in mind we conclude that it is not possible to relax the equality in constraints
(4c) in the VRPD as [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] does for the TSPD. The diﬀerence that makes it
possible for the TSPD is that cycle elimination in the PP is enough to ensure that no over-serving can
take place in the MP for any integer solution because only one route may be chosen. For the VRPD
multiple routes may be chosen in an integer solution so customers can be served multiple times by
diﬀerent routes even though each route at most serves each customer once.
31
==PAGE:37==
Figure 7: An instance for the VRPD with the depot shown as a green box at (0,0) and the customers
1-5 shown with purple circles. Notice that the scaling diﬀer on the two axes.
(a) Solved using a MIP-solver
(b) Solved with CG allowing over-visiting
Figure 8: The instance from Figure 7 solved to (a) IP optimally using the MIP in (1) and (b) LP and
by coincidence also IP optimality with column generation after allowing over-visits in the RMP
Half or full box, BoxPen dual stabilisation:
Since we cannot halve the domain of the dual variables λi, i ∈C as in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020],
these can take values from the entire real line. However, the goal in the PP is to ﬁnd tandem routes
with negative reduced costs and the only terms that can become negative are when the dual variables
associated with the customers visited are positive since they are subtracted in the reduced cost.
If a dual variable λi is negative, then no mater if it is -0.001 or -100 it is highly unlikely for the
associated customer to be visited in a good solution to the PP. This is because, even if the route does
not need to make a detour to visit the customer, servicing the customer makes the reduced cost worse.
Because of this we suspect that keeping the negative dual variables above a certain threshold does not
have much impact on the PP, since the fact that they are negative at all makes them superﬂuous to
consider.
32
==PAGE:38==
Therefore, we initially experimented with only forcing the dual variables to be contained in a "half
box", i.e. we attempt to upper bound them as follows:
"Half box":
λi ≤λi,
λi ∈R+
∀i ∈C
To ensure that our intuition is right we did also compare to the impact of a full box as in typical
BoxPen dual stabilisation.
"Full box":
λi−≤λi ≤λi+,
λi−∈R−, λi+ ∈R+
∀i ∈C
More details on both are found in the following sections.
6.2.1
Half box dual stabilisation
In the stabilised dual master problem we attempt to keep the dual variables below upper bounds λi for
each i ∈C by introducing variables ωi, that measure how much the dual solution exceeds the upper
bounds λi and then penalising these values by an amount, εi, in the objective as shown below:
Stabilised Dual Master Problem
Changes, as compared to (6), marked in cyan:
max
Kλ0 +
X
i∈C
λi −
X
i∈C
εiωi
(8a)

The column generation framework from Algorithm 1 is also aﬀected by the dual stabilisation. This is
because column generation cannot be terminated unless the PP fails to produce an improving column
when given the dual solution to the non-stabilised RMP. To ensure that a solution from the stabilised
RMP is also the solution to the non-stabilised RMP it needs to either lie in the interior of the dual
boxes or the penalties must be 0, such that the dual solution is unaﬀected by stabilisation.
33
==PAGE:39==
The framework we implemented was based on the framework presented in the course 42136 (see Ap-
pendix C) which is further based on [Du Merle et al., 1999]. It can be summarised as follows:
Algorithm 2: Initial Half-Box Framework
1 Generate an initial set of columns such that the RMP has feasible solutions.
2 while Improving columns exist and no stop criteria has been met do
3
Solve the stabilised RMP to get dual solution λ
4
Solve the PP to ﬁnd improving columns
5
if Improving columns were found then
6
Add these columns to the restricted master problem and increase penalties εi
7
else
8
Check stop criteria
9
if At least one stop criteria has been met then
10
Stop
11
else
12
Increase box size λi and decrease penalties εi
13 Solve the RMP to obtain primal solution which is also the optimal solution to the MP.
In particular, the box sizes, λi, are updated as:
λi = λi · αλi
and the penalties are updated as follows:
Increased:
εi · αεi
Decreased:
εi = εi
αεi
where αλi, αεi > 1 are parameters.
If, for some i ∈C, εi is decreased below a threshold, that we initially set to 10−7, it will instead be set
to εi = 0
Finally the stop criteria are:
1. εi = 0 ∀i ∈C
2. λi < λi ∀i ∈C
These stop criteria reﬂect the previously mentioned conditions for when the dual solution to the
stabilised RMP is unaﬀected by stabilisation so that it is also a dual solution to the RMP without
stabilisation.

6.2.3
Modiﬁed dual stabilisation frameworks
When solving the PP speciﬁcally with dynamic programming we introduce a modiﬁed dual stabilisation
framework to replace the framework in Algorithm 2 for half-box and full-box stabilisation, however,
the modiﬁed framework makes use of the notation introduced in the following sections so it is presented
later in section 7.4.
6.2.4
Parameter values and initialising box sizes
The starting values for the penalties εi, ε+
i , ε−
i as well as the "cooling" parameters αεi, αε+
i , αε−
i ,
αλi, αλi+, and αλi−are all parameter values that need to be tuned to obtain full performance.
35
==PAGE:41==
Additionally, initial values of λi, λi+ and λi−need to be chosen based on the instance data in a sensible
way. The values λi will in some way reﬂect how diﬃcult it is to serve a certain customer with the
current columns in the RMP such that a large value of λi will reﬂect that customer i ∈C is currently
hard to serve and the PP will thus likely return a column that serves this customer because it will be
beneﬁcial for the reduced cost of the column to do so.
From LP theory it is known that the optimal primal and dual solutions have the same objective value
i.e. for an optimal solution to the MP it is the case that:
X
r∈Ω
crψr = Kλ0 +
X
i∈C
λi
Recalling that λ0 = 0 when the ﬂeet size is not constraining then this tells us that the sum of the λi
variables should in some way correspond to the collective operational cost of the routes used. This could
be exploited to try and estimate the addition to operational cost associated with inserting customers
in sensible routes or similar.
We will return to how we in practice initialised the values of λi, λi+ and λi−in the results section 10.2.
36
==PAGE:42==
7
Solving the PP with dynamic programming
Instead of ﬁnding improving columns, i.e. truck-drone tandem routes, by solving the MIP in (7) we
implement a labelling algorithm using dynamic programming to do it. With dynamic programming we
build several truck-drone tandems from scratch at the same time, while keeping track of their reduced
costs. Each of these unﬁnished routes or partial solutions will have an associated label that is recursively
propagated to new labels/partial solutions. The recursions are based on [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020]
adapted to a cost minimising objective and tandem routes that do not necessarily visit all customers.
7.1
Generating labels:
7.1.1
Label notation:
To a partial solution we associate the label (S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f), where:
S ⊆C
is the set of customers that this partial solution has visited so far.
iT
is the last node that the truck has visited.
iD
is the last node where the truck and the drone were together
(i.e. where the drone currently is)
τ
is the amount of time passed since the truck and drone were last together.
θ
is the total amount of time spent since the vehicles left the start depot.
f
is the reduced cost of the partial solution.
7.1.2
Propagating labels:
Labels are propagated in generations starting from the initial label (∅, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0) reﬂecting that both
truck and drone are waiting at the start depot at the beginning of their route. Inside each generation
all labels will have visited the same number of customers which we will refer to as the "length" of the
label, |S|. In each generation of propagations all labels of the same length are propagated using the 3
possible propagations presented below. Each of these propagations visit one additional customer such
that the label entry S2 of a newly generated label will contain one more customer than S1 from the
label propagated on: S2 = S1 ∪{j}, j ∈C, j /∈S1. This is why propagating all labels of some length
|S| will result in new labels each of which have length |S| + 1.
For tandem routes where the truck and drone split up and serve customers simultaneously the truck
is always moved ﬁrst and the drone second. While the truck is moving, the time spent is recorded by
the label entry τ instead of θ and then θ is updated once the drone has been moved to catch up with
the truck in time.
Given a partial solution (S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f) with |S| < n the three possible ways to propagate it are:
1. Always possible: Add a truck arc
2. If iD = iT : Add a combined arc
3. If iD ̸= iT : Add a drone sortie starting in iD and ending in iT .
These have been illustrated on Figure 9 and will be discussed in more detail below.
A partial solution will be propagated in all the ways possible, including e.g. adding multiple diﬀerent
truck arcs and multiple sorties/combined arcs, such that one partial solution visiting |S| customers
may be propagated into several partial solutions visiting |S| + 1 customers.
In [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] option 3 above is referred to as a drone leg but most other VRPD
literature uses the notion of drone sorties instead so we will stay with that convention.
37
==PAGE:43==
Figure 9: Illustrations of how partial solutions can be propagated in the three ways with either a
truck arc, combined arc or drone sortie.
Truck arcs:
It is always possible to attempt to add a truck arc. When adding a truck arc there are two possible
starting scenarios: Either truck and drone are currently together in which case iT = iD and τ = 0
or they are not in which case iT ̸= iD and τ has a positive value. In either case the propagation
process is the same: For all nodes j /∈S a truck arc (iT , j) is added resulting in a set of new partial
solutions. Denote the original label (S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f) then it is propagated to all solutions of the form
(S ∪{j}, j, iD, τ + tT
iT ,j + sT , θ, f + cT
iT ,j −λj). Here λj is the value of the dual variable from the
Restricted Master Problem. A partial solution for which θ + τ + tT
it,j + sT + tT
j,n+1 > Tmax should not
be made since it is impossible to make a feasible ﬁnalised tandem route from it, under the assumption
that the time matrices satisfy the triangle inequality.
Additionally, if iD = 0 we do not propagate with a truck arc if that would make τ > e. This is
because we know that the drone has to rendezvous with the truck at least once before it can ﬂy to the
end-depot, so such a partial solution cannot lead to a feasible ﬁnalised solution either.
In summary the recursion for propagating with a truck arc is:
(S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
truck arc

∀j /∈S
(S ∪{j}, j, iD, τ + tT

Checks:

If iD = 0 : τ ≤e

Combined arcs:
In the case that iT = iD it is possible to add a combined arc from iT to j /∈S where the truck carries
the drone. The recursion for this type of propagation is shown in the grey box below. Note that one
can repeat the same check regarding Tmax, as for the truck arc to identify if a partial solution cannot
lead to any feasible completed solutions.
(S, iT , iT , 0, θ, f)
combined arc
========⇒
∀j /∈S
(S ∪{j}, j, j, 0, θ + tT

Drone sorties:
In the cases where iT ̸= iD it is possible to add a drone sortie to the route where the drone is launched
from iD and recovered at iT . This propagation is more heavily restricted than the others and there
may be customers who are not in S, but which it is infeasible to visit with any drone sortie because
they are in C
D.
Thus a partial solution is propagated to all labels of the form (S∪{j}, iT , iT , 0, θ+θ′, f+cD
iD,j+cD

the sortie is infeasible due to the drone’s limited battery endurance. It therefore does not make sense
to propagate from a partial solution with L + τ + R > e as the drone will always run out of battery
before the truck is available for recovery.
In summary:
(S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
