7.1.3
Storing labels
When propagating labels one generation at a time we need to store the newest labels in a way where
we can access them to propagate from them and make the next generation. However, we also need to
store the old labels that have already been propagated on. The reason we have to store these as well
is that we cannot predict which length the optimal solution to the PP has, since it may not visit all
customers.
We experimented with two formats for storing labels:
List format:
All labels made in the same generation of propagations are stored in the same list. These lists are then
kept in another list such that the ﬁrst entry is generation 1 and the last entry is the generation that
needs to be propagated further.
The advantages of this format is that all labels are kept together and new labels are easily added to
the existing labels by appending the list containing all the new labels.
Matrix format:
Labels are stored in (n + 1) × (n + 1) matrices where each entry (x, y), x, y ∈C0 contains labels with
iT = x and iD = y.
When new labels are generated, all labels from the new generation are stored in one such (n+1)×(n+1)
matrix. This matrix is then used as input for propagating these labels to achieve a new (n+1)×(n+1)
39
==PAGE:45==
matrix with the labels from the next generation. The labels that were just propagated on are now
added entry-wise to a ﬁnal (n + 1) × (n + 1) matrix which contain all labels from previous generations
which will not be propagated further on.
Writing to this format becomes more cumbersome since labels need to be placed in diﬀerent matrix
entries depending on the current location of truck and drone. Oppositely, information about a labels
iT , iD is available without needing to read the label so extracting all labels with a speciﬁc iT , iD is
easier. As we will see in section 7.3.1 this allows us to more easily compare labels that have a chance
of dominating each other.
7.1.4
Finalising routes:
Once we have propagated n generations (where n is the number of customers) we stop propagating.
Propagations may also end early if a generation of partial solutions, have been attempted propagated,
but no feasible partial solutions visiting an additional customer exist.
At this point a large number of labels exist corresponding to partial solutions. None of these partial
solutions, however, return to the end-depot yet. To generate actual routes from these partial solutions 2
types of ﬁnalising propagations can be performed. Which types are applicable depends on the location
of truck and drone in the partial solution considered:
1. If iD = iT : Add a combined arc ending in n + 1
2. If iD = iT : Add a truck arc and a drone sortie both ending in n + 1.
3. If iD ̸= iT : Add a truck arc and a drone sortie both ending in n + 1.
These are also illustrated on Figure 10.
Option 1 can be treated exactly as adding any other combined arc where in this case j = n + 1
and the associated dual variable to subtract is λ0. The only feasibility check to perform is to ensure
θ + tT
iT ,n+1 ≤Tmax.
Option 2 and 3 can both lead to feasible solutions only if |S| ≤n −1 i.e. if the partial solution does
not visit all customers. To ﬁnalise such partial solutions two consecutive propagations are necessary.
First a truck arc (iT , n + 1) is added:
(S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
truck arc
======⇒
(S ∪{n + 1}, n + 1, iD, τ + tT
iT ,n+1, θ, f + cT
iT ,n+1 −λ0)
Here it should be ensured that θ + L + τ + tT
iT ,n+1 ≤Tmax, otherwise the truck returns to the depot
too late.
Afterwards, a drone sortie is added going from iD to n + 1 via an un-visited customer, j /∈S ∪C
D,
that the drone is allowed to service. The result of this propagation is a ﬁnal label of the form:
drone sortie
=======⇒
(S ∪{n + 1, j}, n + 1, n + 1, 0, θ + θ′, f + cT
iT ,n+1 −λ0 + cD
iD,j + cD
j,n+1 −λj)
With:
θ′ = L + tD
iD,j + tD
j,n+1 + sD + R
The reason that this calculation of θ′ does not include the time that the truck has spent away from the
drone is that the drone can be recovered immediately upon arriving at the depot whether the truck has
arrived or not. This also means that the time θ + θ′ does not reﬂect the time at which both vehicles
return to the depot, that time would have to be calculated as:
Tback = θ + max{θ′, L + τ + tT
iT ,n+1}
40
==PAGE:46==
However, to ensure that the end-of-day constraint is satisﬁed it is suﬃcient to check θ + θ′ ≤Tmax
since the other term has already been considered. It should still be ensured that the battery endurance
is not exceeded i.e.: θ′ ≤e.
Finally, if iD = 0 then the partial solution cannot be feasibly ﬁnalised at all, since sorties starting in
the start-depot and ending in the end-depot are not allowed.
Figure 10: Illustrations of how partial solutions can be ﬁnalised to form complete routes depending
on iT , iD.
Procedure
Instead of ﬁnalising every partial solution we in practice only attempt to ﬁnalise solutions which have
potential to give a column with reduced cost low enough to be among the ones we return from the
PP. We do this by going through the labels and calculating the following value which says how good
a solution we could at most achieve by ﬁnalising the label:
fest = min{f + cT
iT ,n+1, f + cT
iT ,n+1 + cD
iD,n+1 −λ∗}
Where λ∗= maxi∈C{λi} and cD
iD,n+1 is the minimum operational cost of any sortie between iD and
the end-depot due to to the triangle inequality.
A label is only considered for ﬁnalisation if fest shows it has potential to produce a new column
returned.
Each label may be ﬁnalised in several ways. If iD = iT it may be ﬁnalised either with a combined arc
or a truck arc and sortie and in either case there may be more than one possible sortie to make. In
our implementation we only ﬁnalise each label once, in the way that results in the smallest reduced
cost. We deem that it will be most useful for the RMP to receive columns that are diﬀerent instead of
columns representing routes which only diﬀer in the last part. However, one could have also decided
to ﬁnalise each label in multiple ways and potentially return diﬀerent columns made from the same
label.
41
==PAGE:47==
7.2
Correctness of dynamic programming recursions
In this section we will discuss why the propagations and ﬁnalisations presented above correspond to
solving the PP in (7).
First we will give an overview of the properties that an optimal solution to the PP needs to have and
reference the constraints from the MIP in (7) that ensure these properties are kept. The MIP has
constraints not included as part of this list whose purpose is to make the inner functions of the model
work, such as linking the various variables to model the intended properties etc.
1. The objective value should be the smallest reduced cost, calculated as operational costs minus
the dual variables λi of all customers visited by the route and λ0. This is exactly how it is
calculated in (7a).
2. One drone and one truck should leave the start-depot and re-enter the end-depot as ensured by
(7g) in the MIP. Additionally no vehicle is allowed to leave the end-depot after arriving at it
which is ensured in the MIP by the non-existence of arcs out of the end-depot.
3. Each customer should be served at most once as ensured by (7h).
4. The drone may only be launched to serve a customer and has to be recovered at a rendezvous
with the truck or at the end-depot next. This is ensured by (7m).
5. There is a vehicle speciﬁc time required to serve a customer and launching or recovering the
drone. These are handled by the MIP in constraints (7i)-(7l) where the variables ai and di are
set.
6. The drone’s battery endurance must not be exceeded when away from the truck, even if waiting
for the truck at a rendezvous. This is ensured by (7p) and (7q) which also ensure that the drone
does not have to wait for the arrival of the truck at the end-depot.
7. Sorties starting at the start-depot and ending at the end-depot are speciﬁcally forbidden by (7n).
8. The drone may not serve customers with a demand exceeding its carrying capacity.
This is
ensured by (7o).
9. The last vehicle has to return to the end-depot before the end of day time as ensured by constraint
(7s).
Next we will argue for why all of these 9 properties are also ensured for the optimal route made with
dynamic programming:
1. The reduced cost of a route is calculated by updating f each time either the truck or drone moves
and the calculations correspond to (7a) since operational costs of traversing arcs are added and
dual variables of visited customers are subtracted as well as λ0 which is subtracted once pr route
during ﬁnalisation.
2. Both truck and drone will have left the depot since we do not ﬁnalise solutions with iD = 0 and
the drone cannot leave the depot without leaving either with or after the truck so the only label
with iT = 0 also has iD = 0 and thus it will not be ﬁnalised. Additionally, the truck and drone
could never leave the end-depot because they only enter it when the label is being ﬁnalised, which
is the last thing to happen.
3. Each customer is served at most once either by a truck alone, drone alone or the truck with the
drone present as we only propagate towards customers not in S i.e. who have not already been
propagated to with a sortie or a truck arc/combined arc.
42
==PAGE:48==
4. The only time the drone is moved is when propagating with a combined arc or a drone sortie so
the drone could never be launched to do anything other than perform a sortie. The sortie will
end at a customer if made during propagations or at the end depot if made during ﬁnalisation.
5. Time is kept track of using θ and τ where θ can be somewhat compared to the times ai from the
MIP. When propagating with a truck arc or combined arc the truck service time is added to τ
or θ respectively. The reason that τ is necessary is to keep track of the time spent by the truck
away from the drone such that if they rendezvous it can be compared to the time spent by the
drone. During this comparison the launch, recover and service times for the drone are considered
in the calculation. These are also included if the sortie is made during ﬁnalisation in which case
the drone ﬂies to the depot instead of rendezvousing with the truck at a customer.
6. The drone’s battery endurance is considered when performing any sortie by calculating θ′. If the
sortie is made during propagations θ′ is the total time the drone has been away from the truck
including potential waiting time. Meanwhile, if the sortie is made during ﬁnalisation so it ends at
the depot then θ′ only considers the time until the drone is recovered at the depot independent
of the truck’s arrival time. If θ′ exceeds the battery endurance the sortie is not made.
7. These speciﬁc sorties are forbidden by the fact that we do not ﬁnalise labels with iD = 0.
8. The drone carrying capacity is taken into account by only propagating with a drone sortie to
customers j /∈S who also are not in C
D both during propagations and ﬁnalisation.
9. It is continuously monitored whether Tmax is exceeded and if so, the partial solution or ﬁnalised
solution that would have been made is discarded.
7.3
Removing labels
During propagations not all labels generated are equally promising so some of them are removed at
the end of the generation of propagations that they were produced in. This is to save memory as well
as time since they would have otherwise been further propagated and ﬁnalised.
We experimented with 3 diﬀerent "levels" of label removal, where each level is less aggressive than
the previous one. The third level, dominance or exact label removal, only removes labels which are
strictly worse than other labels. This guarantees that the optimal solution to the Pricing Problem
can be made no matter what it looks like. With the lower levels of label removal this property is not
guaranteed because labels are removed based on their current form without taking into account how
they could or could not be further propagated and ﬁnalised.
When solving the PP with the lower levels of label removal it can be considered as solving it with
a heuristic approach because decisions on what labels to keep are made based on their here-and-now
value in a way that seeks to generate good solutions but without being able to guarantee optimality
since a limited number of all labels are considered.
The framework from Algorithm 1 modiﬁed such that the PP can be solved with the 3 diﬀerent levels
of label removal is presented below, using red to show where the diﬀerent levels are called. While
solving the PP with the ﬁrst two heuristic levels of label removal may provide good negative reduced
cost columns, it cannot be guaranteed that no improving columns exist until the PP has been solved
with exact label removal and failed to ﬁnd one.
If either the ﬁrst or second level heuristic is skipped the number of nested if-statements is decreased
accordingly. We will go into details with the 3 separate levels of label removal in the following sections.
43
==PAGE:49==
Algorithm 3: Column generation framework with multiple levels of label removal
1 Generate an initial set of columns such that the RMP has feasible solutions.
2 while Improving columns exist do
3
Solve RMP to obtain dual solution λ
4
Solve PP with lvl 1 heuristic label removal to ﬁnd improving columns.
5
if At least one improving column was found then
6
Add column(s) to the RMP
7
else
8
Solve PP with lvl 2 heuristic label removal to ﬁnd improving columns
9
if At least one improving column was found then
10
Add column(s) to the RMP
11
else
12
Solve PP with exact label removal to ﬁnd improving columns.
13
if At least one improving column was found then
14
Add column(s) to the RMP
15
else
16
Break: No more improving columns exist.
17 Solve RMP to obtain primal solution, this solution is also the optimal MP solution.
7.3.1
Exact label removal - Removal by dominance
When solving the Pricing Problem with exact label removal only labels that are dominated by other
labels are removed.
For two labels, (S1, iT
1 , iD
1 , τ1, θ1, f1),(S2, iT
2 , iD
2 , τ2, θ2, f2), where the truck and drone are together (iT =
iD) then label 1 dominates label 2 if all the following conditions are satisﬁed:
iT
1 = iT
2 = iT
(dom1 a)
iD
1 = iD
2 = iD
(dom1 b)
S1 ⊆S2
(dom1 c)
iD = iT
(dom1 d)
θ1 ≤θ2
(dom1 e)
f1 ≤f2
(dom1 f)
These rules ensure that any propagation or ﬁnalisation that would be feasible for label 2 is also feasible
for label 1 and the resulting label/route would have at least as good an objective value if made from
label 1 instead of 2 and have used at most as much time.
Similarly for two labels where the truck and drone are not together (i.e. iD ̸= iT ) we have that label
1 dominates label 2 if:
iT
1 = iT
2 = iT
(dom2 a)
iD
1 = iD
2 = iD
(dom2 b)
S1 ⊆S2
(dom2 c)
iD ̸= iT
(dom2 d)
τ1 ≤τ2
(dom2 e)
θ1 + τ1 ≤θ2 + τ2
(dom2 f)
f1 ≤f2
(dom2 g)
44
==PAGE:50==
Notice that for iD ̸= iT there are two conditions that need to be satisﬁed for the time keeping label
entries τ, θ. It is necessary for label 1 to have the property that the total time spent since the truck
left the depot is less than for label 2 (equation (dom2 f)) or the end of day constraint could make
some propagations of label 1 infeasible that are feasible for label 2. Additionally it is also necessary to
ensure that τ1 ≤τ2 or propagating label 1 with some truck arcs could make it infeasible for the drone
to rendezvous with the truck again despite that being an option for label 2. When truck and drone
were together no conditions were necessary on τ because then τ1 = τ2 = 0.
In practice we check whether S1 ⊆S2 using the intersection:
S1 ⊆S2
⇔
S1 ∩S2 = S1
From the experiments in Appendix H.1 we learned that a speed-up could be achieved by checking
conditions (dom1 c) and (dom2 c) last, since these checks are the computationally hardest.
7.3.2
Level 1 heuristic removal
When the Pricing Problem is solved using the ﬁrst layer of label removal, all labels of the form
(S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f), just generated in the newest generation of propagations are given an estimated value
of what the reduced cost of the resulting route would be if ﬁnalised now:
fest = f + cT
iT , n+1
The 5 labels for which this number fest is the lowest are kept, all other labels from this generation are
discarded.
The number "5" has later been tuned since it is a parameter value that could be changed to increase
performance.
7.3.3
Level 2 heuristic removal
We experimented with two versions of a level 2 label remover:
Level 1 inspired:
Same principle as level 1 but where more than 5 labels are kept per generation.
Dominance inspired (dom*):
Removes labels that are dominated by other labels in accordance to the dominance rules in section
7.3.1 above but where the time consuming check S1 ⊆S2 from equations (dom1 c) and (dom2 c) is
omitted. Thus resource-use in the form of time and cost are both considered but not which customers
have been visited apart from iT and iD.
7.4
Modiﬁed dual stabilisation
With the multiple levels of label removal introduced above the PP can be solved with varying com-
plexity. In the stabilised column generation framework in Algorithm 2 line 4 where the PP is solved it
would make sense to follow the procedure from Algorithm 3 and attempt to solve it level for level until
either improving columns are found or it can be concluded that none exist. However, in the stabilised
framework in Algorithm 2 it is not guaranteed that column generation can stop if an exact iteration
of the PP returns no improving columns. It may be the case that none of the stop criteria have been
met in which case we would solve a hard problem, namely the exact PP, only to update the box sizes
λi (or λi+ and λi−) and start over with another stabilised RMP solution.
To avoid spending unnecessary time on the hard exact PP iterations when column generation can not
be terminated we implemented a modiﬁed dual stabilisation framework for both half box and full box
45
==PAGE:51==
stabilisation. In this modiﬁed framework the PP is only solved to optimality (with exact label removal)
if one of the stop criteria is satisﬁed as seen in Algorithm 4.
We have also modiﬁed the way in which we update the box sizes, such that the current dual solution
is guaranteed to satisfy the stop criteria λi < λi ∀i ∈C (or λi−< λi < λi+ ∀i ∈C for full box
stabilisation) if it is found again when the stabilised RMP is solved. This is to avoid situations where
the PP and stabilised RMP are solved multiple times back and forth without adding any improving
columns but just to increase the box size suﬃciently for the stop criteria to be satisﬁed.
Speciﬁcally they are updated as:
Modiﬁed half-box:
∀i ∈C | λi ≥λi :
λi
= λi · αλi
Modiﬁed full-box:
∀i ∈C | λi ≥λi+ :
λi+ = λi · αλi+
∀i ∈C | λi ≤λi−:
λi−= λi · αλi−
Here αλi, αλi+, αλi−> 1 are all still parameters.
Algorithm 4: Modiﬁed Dual stabilisation Framework
1 Generate an initial set of columns such that the RMP has feasible solutions.
2 while Improving columns exist and no stop criteria has been met do
3
Solve the stabilised RMP to get dual solution λ
4
Solve PP with heuristic label removal
5
if Improving columns exist then
6
Add column(s) to the Restricted Master Problem and increase penalties εi
7
else
8
Check stop criteria
9
if At least one stop criteria has been met then
10
Solve PP to exact optimality
11
if At least one improving column is found then
12
Add column(s) to the Restricted Master Problem and increase penalties εi
13
else
14
Stop
15
else
16
Increase box size λi and decrease the penalties εi
17 Solve the RMP to obtain primal solution which is also the optimal solution to the MP.
How the penalties εi (or ε+
i , ε−
i
for full-box stabilisation) are updated is unchanged from how they
were presented in section 6.2.

8
Improving dynamic programming
8.1
ng-route relaxation
In order to improve dominance by being able to compare more labels to each other we apply ng-route
relaxation in which the routes have a limited "memory" and can therefore forget that they have visited
certain customers earlier in the route. This make the conditions (dom1 c),(dom2 c) less restrictive.
ng-route relaxation was introduced in [Baldacci et al., 2011] for CVRP and VRPTW and is a relaxation
of the cycle-constraints, as it allows certain cycles to occur. Speciﬁcally, one deﬁnes a neighbourhood
Ni for each customer i which consists of a speciﬁc number of customers that are the closest to i by
some measure. For our problem we use the truck’s travel cost, cT
ij, as distance measure. A route, which
visits the same customer i ∈C twice, of the form ..., i, ..., j, ..., i, ... is then allowed if and only if there
exists a j, which is visited in the cycle in between the two visits to i, such that i /∈Nj. This means that
the allowed cycles will generally be expected to be of poor quality as re-visits happen across N-sets
and thus involve long travel distances.
The size of the sets Ni controls the impact of the relaxation. The smaller the Ni sets are the less
restrictive the conditions (dom1 c),(dom2 c) are, which makes dominance more eﬀective at removing
labels. On the other hand, cycles are also more likely to occur which can yield LP solutions with worse
bounds if they are used in the solution to the RMP.
Choosing the size of the Ni sets therefore represents a trade-oﬀbetween good LP bounds and reduction
in the number of labels kept and thus computational complexity of the PP.
In regards to the propagation of labels we implement a slight variation of what is presented in
[Baldacci et al., 2011] as we have two vehicles which may not be at the same customer. The label
entry S from our previous labelling algorithm is replaced by Sng which contains the customers visited
that have not been forgotten. When propagating a partial solution we then update Sng as follows:
(Sng, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
truck arc
======⇒
∀j /∈Sng
((Sng ∩Nj) ∪{iD} ∪{j}, ...)
(Sng, iT , iT , τ, θ, f)
combined arc
========⇒
∀j /∈Sng
((Sng ∩Nj) ∪{j}, ...)
(Sng, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
drone sortie
========⇒
∀j /∈Sng∪C
D
((Sng ∩NiT ) ∪{j}, ...)
Notice that during the update of Sng, when inserting a truck arc both iD and j are ensured to be in
the resulting set. iD was already part of Sng but as it is intersected with Nj it would be possible for
the truck to "forget" the customer in which the drone is currently situated, if iD is not part of Nj. In
such cases the drone could then re-visit the customer it is already at without any travel expenses for
the ﬁrst part of the sortie.
When inserting a drone sortie the drone will ultimately end in node iT even though it visits customer
j on the way so Sng is intersected with NiT allowing iD to be forgotten but it is noted that the drone
visited customer j on the way even if j /∈NiT . An illustration of how Sng is updated during propagation
where this becomes relevant is shown in Figure 11.
This is diﬀerent from how it is done in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020], who, in the case of a drone
sortie, updates Sng as: (Sng ∪{j}) ∩NiT .
In Appendix H.3 we discuss how this diﬀerence is expected to aﬀect column generation and we test
two versions of column generation side by side where we have implemented both options for updating
the Sng sets.
We experimented with diﬀerent Julia speciﬁc data types for Sng and the Ni sets but since these
experiments are implementation speciﬁc we have placed them in Appendix H.2.
47
==PAGE:53==
Figure 11: Updating the set Sng during propagations. Notice in iteration 3 when inserting the truck
arc 2 →3 the customer iD = 1 is kept in the set Sng even though 1 /∈N3 to avoid solutions where the
same customer is visited twice in a row. In iteration 4 the customer served by the sortie, 4, is also
kept in Sng even though 4 /∈N3. This diﬀers from how ng-route relaxation was implemented for the
TSPD in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020].
8.2
Bi-directional labelling
Bi-directional labelling or forwards-backwards labelling has historically shown to be beneﬁcial for a
range of graph problems such as the shortest path problem and the capacitated VRP [Righini and Salani, 2006,
Desaulniers, 2017]. We believe it to be beneﬁcial for the VRPD as well because the problem is almost
symmetric. With symmetric cost and time consumption for all arcs the only thing to keep in mind
when reversing a full route is whether the route makes use of the one non-symmetric exception: That
the drone may be away from the truck for longer than the battery endurance in the speciﬁc case where
it ﬂies into the depot and is recovered within the time limit of the battery.
When solving the PP with bi-directional labelling we ﬁnd the tandem routes with lowest reduced cost
via a two step process:
1. First, generate all non-dominated half-routes
2. Merge half-routes into full routes by interpreting one of them as a "forwards label" and the other
as a "backwards label" and continue one of them into the other.
Initially we only implemented bi-directional labelling for the iterations where the PP is solved with
exact label removal but as we will present in section 10.6 we also experimented with using it for the
second level heuristic iterations.
8.2.1
Generating half-routes
Starting from the empty solution with associated label (S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f) = (∅, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0) we propagate,
as previously, out until a half-point is reached (see below). During the propagations, the way in which
combined arcs and drone sorties are made is unchanged. However, when propagating with a truck arc
if iD = 0 (i.e. the drone has not yet left the depot) it is checked whether τ > e. If, for a label just
generated, this is the case but the sortie length itself is below e then the label is stored in a separate
structure from all the other labels. This separate structure contains the labels that are only feasible
when interpreted backwards, i.e. when the labels are interpreted as going into the end-depot even
48
==PAGE:54==
though during propagations they were propagated as if leaving the start-depot. When propagating
one generation at a time, labels from both structures are propagated. Additionally, when removing
labels with exact label remover (dominance) the backwards labels may be dominated by forwards labels
because all forwards labels are feasible when interpreted in both directions. This does not hold the
other way so the forwards labels may only be dominated by other forwards labels.
Choice of "half-point":
Because the instances we solve currently are small then we set the point for which we stop propagating
a label further as the time when it has visited ⌈n
2 ⌉customers. For larger instances it may be worthwhile
to propagate out to Tmax
2
instead, but currently this is not a binding constraint.
When the half-point is reached, propagations cease and we have two separate structures containing
labels. One which contain all non-dominated labels that are feasible in both directions and one which
contain the special non-dominated labels that are only feasible in the backwards direction. From these
we make two new structure:
We keep the structure containing all the labels that are feasible in both directions and denote these
the "forwards half-routes".
Additionally we make a copy of this where we append all the labels that were only feasible in the
backwards direction, so that we have structure containing all labels that are feasible in the backwards
direction. We call these the "backwards half-routes".
If launch and recover times diﬀer:
In case the launch time, L, and recover time, R, are not equal the problem becomes a little less
symmetric than what we presented above:
Consider a label in which the drone performs a sortie out of the start-depot to rendezvous with the
truck. When we reverse such a route and consider the sortie as going into the end-depot, instead of
out of the start-depot, the recover time has been added to the time spent by the truck, instead of
the launch time, even though the event that takes place now that the half-route is reversed is a drone
launch, not recovery. The feasibility check for the drone’s endurance is unaﬀected because both R from
when it was considered recovered by the truck and L from when it was considered launched at the
depot are part of the sum to calculate the time spent. Thus it makes no mathematical diﬀerence for
the feasibility check of the sortie if the two times are swapped. This also goes for any sortie between
customers, thus any other sortie that could be made in the half-route label or as part of merging. Here
both a recover and a launch time will be added in the calculations for the time spent by truck and
drone so it has no impact if the two times are added in the wrong order or not.
Thus the only impact it could have on feasibility is if the diﬀerence between L and R makes the
diﬀerence between whether or not a tandem route exceeds the end-of-day time Tmax. To remedy this,
it would be necessary to ensure feasibility of a tandem route made from merging a forwards half-route
with a backwards half-route where the drone ﬂies to the end-depot that has a speciﬁc time spent which
is either in the interval ]Tmax, Tmax + R −L] in case R > L or in ]Tmax + R −L, Tmax] in case L > R.
There may exist a way to do this which does not involve re-calculating the time spent for the full route
by starting with the label keeping entries from the forwards half-route, but for our instances we assume
R = L and additionally we have observed that the end-of-day constraint is nowhere near binding for
the small instance that we are solving. Nonetheless, it is a concern to keep in mind if solving instances
where R ̸= L.
8.2.2
Merging:
When ﬁnding the best solution(s) to the Pricing Problem the "half-routes" have to be merged to form
full tandem routes. Here we present 3 types of "merges". All 3 are necessary because it needs to be
ensured that any tandem route can be made by a merge of two half-routes and while some tandem
routes can be made by merging multiple diﬀerent half-routes in multiple diﬀerent ways, some tandem
routes can only be made by merging two speciﬁc half-routes in a speciﬁc way.

==PAGE:55==

8.2.3
Correctness of merges
In the following two paragraphs we will discuss why we need all 3 types of merges and why some of
them include conditions on the half-routes being fully propagated:
Ensuring that all tandem routes that visit < n customers can be generated:
Consider any tandem route visiting fewer than all customers. For this route we will deﬁne two special
customers "A" and "B" as follows:
Starting from the end-depot, go backwards through the customers visited by the route and count them.
As customers are counted, move along truck arcs and combined arcs ﬁrst and only count a customer
52
==PAGE:58==
visited by a sortie after both end-points of the sortie have been counted. The customer who is counted
as customer number ⌈n
2 ⌉is labelled "A" unless the customer is served by a drone alone in the tandem
route in which case we instead label the customer ⌈n
2 ⌉−1 as "A". Note that in the latter case "A"
will then be the customer at the end-point of the sortie serving customer ⌈n
2 ⌉.
Similarly for "B", we count the customers forwards in the route beginning from the start-depot and
moving along truck arcs and combined arcs ﬁrst, as before, until we reach either customer number ⌈n
2 ⌉,
or customer number ⌈n
2 ⌉−1 in case the former customer was served by the drone. Then we label this
customer "B". For an example of a tandem route where the customers "A" and "B" are labelled see
Figure 15.
In case a route visits at most ⌈n
2 ⌉customers, "A" and "B" are simply the ﬁrst and last customers served
by the truck, respectively. For these especially small routes, glue merging will always be suﬃcient to
reproduce them because drone sorties from the start-depot to the end-depot is prohibited. Thus there
will exist at least one customer, "C", visited during the route at which the truck and drone are both
present. If we split the route at this customer in the way described below it will result in two half-
routes, each of which visit less than ⌈n
2 ⌉customers and which can be glue merged to form the full
tandem route again. Moving on, we thus consider routes that visit more than ⌈n
2 ⌉customers though
not all customers.
With "A" and "B" deﬁned as above it is possible to split the tandem route at "A", "B" or any
customer served by the truck between serving "A" and "B", and we will denote such a customer
"C". It is possible to choose "C"="A" or "C"="B" since these are always served by the truck as a
consequence of how we deﬁned them. What we mean by splitting the tandem route at "C", is that
we create the following two half-routes: One half route is created by copying the part of the tandem
route that serves the customers who would be counted up to and including "C" following our counting
convention above, out of the start-depot. The other half-route is made doing the same in the opposite
direction so counting customers up to and including "C" by going backwards from the end-depot.
Because of the way we deﬁned "A" and "B", then each of these half-routes will at most serve ⌈n
2 ⌉
customers.
Additionally, because the full tandem route visits less than all n customers, "A" will
always be at least as early in the route as "B" though with the possibility that they are the same
customer.
If all sorties are fully contained on either side of "C" i.e. if for each sortie both the end-points of
the sortie, as well as the mid-point, are customers served by the same half-route, then when split this
way the two half-routes will together have served all the customers that the full tandem route served
and traversed all arcs that were traversed in the full route. Therefore the full tandem route could be
reproduced by merging the two half-routes with a glue merge at "C". However, it is also possible that
there exists a sortie which starts at a customer to one side of "C" and ends to the other side of "C"
in the tandem route such that each half-route does not contain both end-points of the sortie and thus
none of them include the sortie.
A very speciﬁc property need to hold for a tandem route for it to be impossible to choose any "C"
between "A" and "B" such that splitting at "C" results in half-routes that can be glue merged to
reproduce the full route. A route with this property has been illustrated in Figure 15
This very speciﬁc property is:
The drone is not on the ground at any customer between "A" and "B" in the tandem route, i.e. a
drone is launched at the latest at customer "A" and recovered at the earliest at "B". In such a speciﬁc
case no customer "C" can be chosen such that the tandem route can be reproduced by glue merging
two half-routes made from splitting at customer "C" and where each half route serves at most ⌈n
2 ⌉
customers.
To reproduce such a tandem it will however be possible to choose a customer "C" between "A" and
"B" (both included) served by the truck and then split the tandem at this customer, like described
above, to obtain two half-routes that can then be sortie merged at "C" to form the full tandem because
the only part of the full route not accounted for by any of the half-routes is the problematic sortie.
53
==PAGE:59==
One of these customers, "C", that we can always split at, is customer "A" in which case the backwards
half-route will have visited ⌈n
2 ⌉customers. This is because customer "A" is either the customer visited
as number ⌈n
2 ⌉counted backwards from the end depot. The reason it cannot be customer ⌈n
2 ⌉−1,
is that that requires a sortie, fully contained in the backwards half-route, which starts in "A". This
contradicts the condition that there is a sortie starting in "A" that cannot be contained in either
half-route, which is what made sortie merges necessary in the ﬁrst place. Thus this half-route will
always have visited ⌈n
2 ⌉customers.
In conclusion we have found that glue merging is suﬃcient to reproduce almost all tandem-routes
visiting < n customer. The only exceptions are tandem routes with the speciﬁc property that they
contain a sortie that cannot be fully contained in one half-route, in which case sortie merging is
necessary. When sortie merging is necessary it will always be possible to sortie merge at the customer
we would label "A" with the above convention and therefore it is suﬃcient to consider sortie merging
for pairs of half-routes where the backwards half-route is fully propagated (i.e. visit ⌈n
2 ⌉customers)
54
==PAGE:60==
Figure 15: Illustration of the counting notation and labelling of customers "A" and "B" in the proof.
Here applied to a tandem route which cannot be reproduced by splitting at a customer "C" and then
glue merging. We have shown the forwards and backwards half-routes arising from splitting at
"A"="C" because this choice will always result in a backwards half route that is fully propagated.
The two half-routes could be sortie-merged to reproduce the full tandem route.
Ensuring that all tandem routes visiting all n customers can be produced:
Now consider a tandem route which visits all n customers, and deﬁne "A" and "B" as before. If n is
odd then ⌈n
2 ⌉· 2 = n + 1 which means that customer "B", when deﬁned as above, will be customer
number either ⌈n
2 ⌉−1 or ⌈n
2 ⌉when counting customer backwards from the end-depot to ﬁnd "A".
The ﬁrst will be the case if customer number ⌈n
2 ⌉when counting forwards was not served by a drone
alone. From this we can see that for n odd, we have the same property as when considering shorter
tandem routes, that is:
Customer "A" will always be at least as early in the full tandem route as customer "B". This means
that we have all the same conditions for choosing a customer "C", to split the tandem route at, as we
had before. Thus, if n is odd, any tandem route visiting all n customers can be reproduced by glue
55
==PAGE:61==
merging and sortie merging.
If, however, n is even, then we are no longer guaranteed this property. This is because, one way to
cover every customer, is for both half-routes to visit n
2 distinct customers, with no overlap, which is
the maximum length of a half-route. In this case the customer visited as number n
2 counted going
backwards from the depot is later in the route than the customer n
2 counted when going forwards form
the start-depot and thus "B" would be before "A" in the route. The previous methods for merging
are no longer suﬃcient in this case since we cannot choose the customer "C" to split at, as in the ways
described previously. A route constructed as such is shown in Figure 16
If the full tandem route is constructed such that this is the case, then it is necessary to split the route
at both "A" and "B", by which we mean that the following two half-routes are created: The forwards
half-route is made by copying the part of the tandem route that serves customers up to, and including,
customer "B", and the backwards half-route is created by doing similarly, in the opposite direction for
the customers served up to, and including, customer "A".
Note that, in any feasible tandem route that visits all customers and can be split in this way it is
known that both "A" and "B" are combined customers. This is because if they were not, then it would
be necessary to insert a sortie at some point, but since all customers are already covered there is no
available mid-point for this sortie.
It might not be possible to split a tandem route into two half-routes with no overlap, if there is a
sortie that cannot be fully contained in either half-route, as described earlier.
In that case, since
there is overlap in which customers the two half-routes visit, deﬁning "A" and "B" as before leads to
"A"="B". Additionally it means that there is a customer that is not contained in either half-route.
Speciﬁcally that customer is the mid-point of the problematic sortie. If this is the case then the full
route can be reproduced by splitting it at "C"="A"="B", and sortie merging the two half-routes at
"C" as described above.
In conclusion, any tandem route visiting all n customers can be re-created using glue merges and sortie
merges as previous if n is odd. If n is even instead it is necessary to introduce combined arc merges
as well because the two half-routes that visit all customers together cannot overlap in a customer to
make glue merging possible.
56
==PAGE:62==
Figure 16: Illustration of the counting notation and labelling of customers "A" and "B" applied to a
tandem route which cannot be reproduced by splitting at a customer "C" and then glue merging or
sortie merging. We have shown the forwards and backwards half-routes arising from splitting in the
way described for when "B" appears earlier in the route than "A". The two half-routes can be
combined arc merged to reproduce the full tandem route.
8.2.4
Merging in practice:
In the section above we learned that glue merging half-routes is for the most part suﬃcient to generate
all imaginable feasible tandem routes. The only exceptions were that sortie merging may be necessary
in some cases but then the backwards half-routes can always be chosen among the fully propagated
half-routes. Additionally if n is even the tandem routes that visit all customers can only be generated
by combined arc merges or sortie merges between fully propagated half-routes.
In total we therefore need up to 6 types of merges to be able to recreate any tandem route. We have
summarised these in the table below where we ﬁrst write conditions on of how far the half-routes have
been propagated, here "F" means that a half-route has been fully propagated i.e. has |S| = ⌈n
2 ⌉and
"NF" means that a half-route is not fully propagated i.e. has |S| < ⌈n
2 ⌉. The last two columns indicate
what type of merge is possible and the conditions on the half-route labels for the merge to be possible
at all.

With the matrix structure for storing labels presented in 7.1.3 all labels that share all the following
properties can be accessed together:
• Whether the half-route is a forwards hal f-route (Fhr) or backwards half-route (Bhr)
• A speciﬁc iT

In experiments enclosed in Appendix I.3 we found that a small speed-up could be achieved by sorting
the labels with a speciﬁc (Fhr/Bhr, iT , iDF/NF)-tuple such that the half-routes with the lowest
58

reduced costs are ﬁrst. This makes it possible to know when to stop exploring further merges between
half-routes a speciﬁc pair of tuples.
Staying out of coding speciﬁcs, the basic idea in the merging framework is then:
Algorithm 5: Sketch of merge framework
1 Start with dummy best routes that all have reduced cost ∞.
2 for Each of the 6 types of merges from Table 6 do
3

4

5

6

7
Estimate reduced cost, bcest, for resulting merged route
8
if bcest lower than one of the current best tandem routes then
9
Check feasibility for the merge including that Sfw ∩Sbw is as in Table 6
10
if Feasible and not identical to one of the best routes then
11
Include it in the best routes and remove the worst of them
12
else
13
Break, move on to next compatible tuple (Bhr, iT , iDF/NF).
14 Return The best routes
The merges from Table 6 are done in a speciﬁc order and since we only attempt to merge two routes
for which the merge has potential to give a new best route, then the earlier good routes are found, the
better. In Appendix I.1 we performed experiments to see which order seemed most beneﬁcial. The
results showed that the order only had a minor impact on the collective run times but the best order
seemed to be:
1. Combined arc merge, F-F
2. Glue merge, F-F
3. Glue merge F-NF
4. Glue merge NF-NF
5. Sortie merge F-F
6. Sortie merge F-NF
Note that in line 10 in Algorithm 5 it is ensured that the route found which is better than at least
one of the current best tandem routes is not exactly identical to any of the best tandem routes. As we
have illustrated in Figure 17 it is sometimes possible to produce the same tandem route by merging
diﬀerent half-routes and therefore this check is necessary to obtain a number of diﬀerent best routes
and not accidentally the same route multiple times.
Additionally note that when we sortie merge two half-routes we only keep the tandem route which was
best even though it may be possible to merge the two half-routes with sorties serving various diﬀerent
customers j. One could also produce all resulting tandem routes in case more than one of these could
be among the best routes made, however, as discussed already when we made a similar choice for how
we ﬁnalised labels, we deem it to be more useful for the RMP to receive routes that diﬀer in more ways
than one sortie.
59
==PAGE:65==
Figure 17: Tandem route and how it can be split in multiple ways (here 2) into two half-routes which
when glue merged reproduces the original tandem route.
8.2.5
The potential of combining ng-route relaxation and bi-directional labelling
Combining ng-route relaxation and bi-directional labelling is not something we have managed to do
within the scope of this project. It may be possible to combine them both, in which case it would
probably improve column generation signiﬁcantly, by allowing the PP to be solved even faster. However,
we are concerned for whether the more aggressive dominance that comes with ng-route relaxation can,
in some way, make it impossible to produce certain half-routes, that are not in themselves optimal ng
half-routes, but which are necessary to produce the optimal full tandem route.
If more time had been available we would have liked to investigate this further.
60
==PAGE:66==
9
Branch and Price
In our branching strategy we add the branching constraints to the pricing problem of our column
generation framework.
We choose to add the branching constraints to the PP instead of the MP
for the following reason: We know that solving the PP, speciﬁcally the exact iterations, is the most
time-consuming part of our column generation frameworks, see for instance the results in Table 11
or the proﬁling results in section 10.5.1. Therefore, we want to ensure that any column produced
from the PP does not violate the branching constraints, but can actually be used by the RMP and
additionally adding the branching constraints to the PP is expected to make it easier to solve due to a
narrowed solution space. One could also have experimented with adding branching constraints to the
MP, however that is outside the scope of this report.
We have experimented with branching on both customers and arcs as described in the following sections
inspired by the variables in our MIP in (1).
9.1
Branching on arcs
Recall that in our MILP formulation we had the decisions variables xDp
ij , xTp
ij ∈{0, 1}, ∀(i, j) ∈A, p ∈P
representing whether the directed arc (i, j) was traversed by the drone/truck respectively from tandem
p. During column generation, when solving the RMP, each tandem route r ∈R generated by the
PP, is assigned a weight ψr, which when compared to the MILP reﬂects that all xDp
ij , xTp
ij ∀(i, j) ∈A,
belonging to the same tandem p = r, is assigned the weight 0 ≤ψr ≤1. In order to obtain an integer
solution without solving the SP (the MP prior to LP-relaxation), it is therefore necessary for all these
weights ψr to be integer. We achieve this by controlling the cumulative vehicle ﬂows, where "vehicle"
here refers to either truck or drone:
On each arc (i, j) ∈A we consider the summed weights P ψr for all routes r ∈R where the speciﬁc
vehicle type traverses the arc (i, j). In the case of cumulative drone ﬂow, ψr only contributes if the
drone is ﬂying, i.e. if the drone is carried by its truck across arc (i, j) (if (i, j) is a combined arc) in
route r then ψr is considered part of the cumulative truck ﬂow on (i, j) but not the cumulative drone
ﬂow on (i, j).
By branching 0 and 1 on arcs with a non-integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow, as will be described below,
we ensure that the MP solution eventually has integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow on all arcs. The only
way for a Corner-Point Feasible (CPF) solution to the MP to have this property is if all the routes
chosen by the MP have been chosen with integer weights ψi ∈{0, 1}∀r ∈R, which means that we have
a solution to the SP as well.
A more formal proof of this statement is enclosed in Appendix F, but the short argument is that ﬂow
conservation and the fact that each customer has to be visited exactly once, means that tandem routes
which coincided to give an integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow on a certain arc (i, j) ∈A, cannot deviate
from each other at any point and thus have to be identical, in which case one of them would be chosen
with weight 1 and the other(s) weight 0.
9.1.1
Branching on a truck arc:
When branching on an arc (a, b) which had a fractional cumulative truck ﬂow in the current MP
solution we make two child nodes:
In the 0-child we require that if the truck ever ﬁnds itself in customer a then it does not travel to b
immediately after. A route can still visit both a and b, but no truck is allowed to visit b immediately
after a. This guarantees that the arc (a, b) will have a cumulative truck ﬂow of 0 in the next MP
solution.
In the 1-child we enforce that each tandem route either does not visit customers a and b at all, or
serves b with the truck immediately after serving a with the truck. This ensures that all routes which
61
==PAGE:67==
contribute to serving customers a and b, traverse the arc (a, b) with a truck, such that the cumulative
truck ﬂow is guaranteed to be 1 on the arc in the optimal MP solution.
Note that it cannot be strictly enforced that all routes in the 1-child traverse arc (a, b) with the truck,
since the optimal SP solution may consist of multiple routes, which would then all visit both a and b.
9.1.2
Branching on a drone arc:
When branching on an arc (a, b) with fractional cumulative drone ﬂow, the restrictions on the child
nodes are:
In the 0-child it is forbidden for any drone to ﬂy from customer a to b. This does not include combined
arcs so it is allowed for the drone to be carried from customer a to customer b in which case it will not
service any of them or contribute to the cumulative drone ﬂow on the arc which is guaranteed to be 0
in the next MP solution.
In the 1-child it is enforced that both vehicles from tandem p are only allowed to serve any of the
customers a or b if the drone from tandem p ﬂies from a to b. This can be achieved by the drone
performing a sortie that either starts in a or ends in b:
Option 1:
(a = iD) 99K b 99K iT
Option 2:
iD 99K a 99K (b = iT )
If the drone is carried by the truck it is not considered ﬂying so a combined arc from a to b is not
allowed. This means that all tandem routes either do not visit a and b at all, or visits both of them in
which case arc (a, b) is traversed by a ﬂying drone. This guarantees that the cumulative drone ﬂow of
is 1 on the arc in the MP solution.
A possible other variant:
One could consider another type of drone arc branching. In this variant we would distinguish between
drone arcs that are the start of a sortie and those that the end of a sortie. This would make enforcing
the branching constraints easier because we do not have to allow both options above. A concern with
this variant, however, is the size of the Branch and Bound tree, since it would be necessary to create
three child nodes when branching on an arc (a, b). One in which no drones are allowed to ﬂy from a
to b, one where drones have to be launched in a and ﬂy to b, and one where they have to ﬂy from a to
b and be recovered in b. It is also possible that one would have two separate types of drone arcs and
then go back to branching 0 and 1 on the two speciﬁc types. Both these versions result in a potentially
larger Branch and Bound tree.
Testing this variant is, however, outside the scope of this report.
9.1.3
Branching on arcs incident to the depots:
In Appendix A we proved that it is never necessary to branch on arcs that leave or enter the depots
since in any case where such an arc has non-integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow, there also exists an arc
between customers with a non-integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow so branching can be done purely on
inter-customer arcs instead. We have not investigated whether it is more or less beneﬁcial, in terms of
run-time, to exploit this property, but in our case it made certain implementation speciﬁcs easier.
9.2
Branching on customers
In our MILP in (1) we had a set of auxiliary variables for the customers whose values could be uniquely
determined from the values of the x-variables. These were denoted yT
i , yD
i , yC
i ∈{0, 1} and reﬂected
whether customer i was served by a truck alone (yT = 1), drone alone (yD = 1) or a truck where the
drone was also present (yC = 1).
62
==PAGE:68==
We saw that, while these variables could, in theory, be deﬁned as continuous and still take binary
values as a consequence of the x-variables being binary, setting these variables to be binary allowed for
faster solution times of the MILP in section 5. As mentioned previously, it might be that informing
the solver that the y-variables are binary allowed the solver to exploit a set of useful cuts, however we
also considered whether it might be more beneﬁcial to be able to branch on these variables. As we
discussed, branching on a single y-variable can aﬀect other y-variables and a multitude of x-variables.
Looking at the y-variables we see that for the way we have implemented dynamic programming yT
i = 1
and yC
i = 1 reﬂect two versions of the same event, namely that the customer i ∈C is served by a truck
with or without a drone being present as well. As opposed to the opposite event, yD
i = 1, where the
customer is served by a drone and no truck visits the customer. In order to branch on customers in
a non-integer solution to the MP we therefore consider the total in-going truck ﬂow into a customer
i, and if this is fractional then it means that the customer is served by a combination of trucks and
drones. This can never be the case in an integer solution so we branch on the customer by generating
the following two child nodes:
In the 0-child we enforce that this customer may only be served by drones so it is forbidden for any
truck to visit the customer.
In the 1-child we enforce that this customer may only be served by trucks so it is forbidden for any
drone to perform a sortie where this customer is the mid-point.
In both the 0-child and the 1-child it is legal to not visit the customer with a route at all.
We do not expect customer branching alone to be enough to guarantee integer solutions so we will
experiment with which order to branch on the various 3 options: Customers, truck arcs and drone
arcs. These experiments are presented later in section 10.8.
9.3
Eﬀects of branching on the feasible propagations
As we branch on customers, and truck- or drone-arcs, the feasible solution space is narrowed and the
propagations in the labelling algorithm (section 7.1.2) needs to take these restrictions into account,
such that the tandem routes generated in the pricing problem satisfy the branching constraints.
In this section we present the additional considerations that need to be made before propagating a
label in the three possible ways presented previously. When ﬁnalising a label either with a combined
arc, or a truck arc accompanied by a drone sortie, these ﬁnalisations also need to satisfy the below
considerations. In our implementation, arcs going into the depot are never branched on, as discussed
in section 9.1.3, so some of the complications can always be ignored speciﬁcally when ﬁnalising.
Note that for some of the considerations, a violation does not always immediately lead to infeasibility.
This is because we have prioritised not generating labels that could never be feasibly ﬁnalised. The
reason we prioritise this, is to avoid spending resources on propagating such labels and comparing them
with other labels during label removal even though they can never be used. We will continuously use
the notation a and b to denote the nodes, which are branched on, or which are both incident to the
arc being branched on. With the way we have implemented arc branching as discussed in section 9.1.3
we only consider branching on arcs (a, b) if a ∈C and b ∈C, so for this section a and b is always used
to denote customers and never a depot.
9.3.1
Propagating with a truck arc
When looking to propagate a label (S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f) with a truck arc from iT to an un-visited customer
j then this is illegal due to branching, if any of the following statements are true. We have sorted the
statements by the type of branching that has led to the infeasibility. Two of the statements have been
coloured red because they do not depend on j, so if either of those are true the label (S, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
cannot be propagated with any truck arc.
63
==PAGE:69==
Customer branching:
• j is a drone customer (we have branched 0 on j)
Truck arc branching:
• The truck arc iT →j is set to 0
• ∃a ̸= iT such that the truck arc a →j is set to 1
Drone arc branching:
• The drone arc iD 99K j is set to 1
• ∃a such that the drone arc a 99K iT is set to 1 and iD ̸= iT
• ∃b such that the drone arc iT 99K b is set to 1 and iD ̸= iT
• ∃a ̸= iT , iD such that the drone arc a 99K j is set to one AND the sortie iD 99K a 99K j is illegal.
Regarding the ﬁnal point there are several ways that the sortie could be illegal:
- There has been branched 0 on the drone arc iD 99K a
- There has been branched 1 on a drone arc k 99K a where k ̸= iD
- The sortie takes longer time to perform than the drone has battery endurance for
- The customer a is in C
D
- a is a truck customer (we have branched 1 on a).
An illustration of all the branching constraints that can make it illegal to perform the propagation
iT →j is shown in Figure 18.

respectively on the arc/customer.

Truck branching:
• The truck arc iT →j is set to 0
• ∃b ̸= j such that the truck arc iT →b is set to 1
• ∃a ̸= j such that the truck arc a →j is set to 1
Drone branching:
• ∃b such that the drone arc iD 99K b is set to 1
• ∃a such that the drone arc a 99K j is set to 1

9.4
Solving each B&P node with previous dynamic programming improvements
9.4.1
Bi-directional labelling
Branching on directed truck and drone arcs poses a challenge for bi-directional labelling. Before adding
any branching constraints we discussed that all labels feasible in the forwards direction are also always
feasible in the backwards direction. Meanwhile, the only labels that are feasible in the backwards
direction but not the forwards direction are labels where the drone exploits that it does not have to
wait for the truck to be recovered at the end-depot. This property was important because we could
then produce all half routes, both in the forwards and backwards direction, by only propagating in
the forwards direction and keeping track of a few additional backwards feasible labels. Once we start
branching on arcs this property falls apart:
Consider for instance the situation depicted in Figure 21 where we have branched 1 on the drone arc
(a, b). During propagations we have generated a half-route where the truck drives from the start-depot
to b and the drone performs the sortie 0 99K a 99K b. Interpreted in the forwards direction this label
is feasible with respect to the branching constraint since the drone traverses the arc (a, b). However,
when interpreted in the backwards direction this half-route is not feasible since customer a and b are
both visited but no drone traverses the directed arc (a, b). Oppositely, if we had branched on the arc
(b, a) instead, the label would be feasible in the backwards direction but not the forwards.
66
==PAGE:72==
Figure 21: Example of how arc-branching breaks the symmetry that we exploited in bi-directional
labelling. Here the label interpreted forwards is feasible since the drone traverses arc (a, b) but it is
not feasible when interpreted backwards since a and b are visited but the directed arc (a, b) is not
traversed by a drone.
This break of symmetry would require us to diﬀerentiate more between forwards feasible and backwards
feasible labels which is surely doable but which may threaten the usefulness of bi-directional labelling,
since it means we need to propagate twice, once in each direction. Instead we notice that branching
on customers does not break the symmetry. This is because forbidding a customer, j to be the middle
of a sortie does not diﬀerentiate between whether we perform sortie i 99K j 99K k or k 99K j 99K i
and forbidding the truck from serving a customer can be achieved by either forbidding to enter or
leave the customer which due to ﬂow conservation will automatically prohibit both. For as long as no
branching has been done on arcs, Branch and Bound nodes can therefore be solved with bi-directional
labelling in the pricing problem, adapting the propagations to obey the branching constraints but
without re-thinking the way that forwards and backwards routes are generated.
Based on this observation we implemented a Branch and Price framework where nodes of the tree that
include only customer branching are solved with column generation using bi-directional labelling in
the pricing problem and nodes that involve arc branching are solved with the previously used plain
version of column generation. These results are presented later in section 10.10.
9.5
Improving the Branch and Price procedure
In this last section we will discuss ways to improve the Branch and Price procedure other than changing
the way in which each node is solved.
9.5.1
Keeping columns
Starting column generation in a child node with a set of columns from the parent node which satisfy the
added branching constraint can potentially reduce the number of times that the PP is solved. This is
because the columns kept may have otherwise needed to be generated anew during column generation
in order to reach termination. This does however introduce some overhead to the B&P procedure since
it has to be investigated which of the columns generated upon solving a node that can be used in the
child-nodes.
ng-relaxation and concerns when keeping columns
ng-relaxation can be applied to the PP in the Branch & Price framework without extensive modiﬁ-
cations. One thing to keep in mind however, is that the columns generated by the PP may not be
elementary. This becomes important if some columns are kept from when a parent node was solved
with column generation to when the child is solved.
67
==PAGE:73==
Keeping columns without ng-route relaxation:
Without ng-relaxation any column feasible in the parent node will be feasible in at least one child
node. If we branched on an arc (a, b) then any column from the parent node where the speciﬁc vehicle
type traverses the arc (a, b) is feasible in the 1-child. Meanwhile, any column from the parent node
where the arc (a, b) is not traversed by the speciﬁc vehicle type is feasible in the 0-child, and sometimes
feasible in the 1-child as well if neither a nor b are visited. Similarly if we branched on a customer, a,
then any column where a is served by the truck is feasible in the 1-child, any column where a is served
by the drone is feasible in the 0-child and if a is not visited at all then the column is feasible in both
children.
Keeping columns with ng-route relaxation:
When the routes are not elementary it becomes possible for a column generated when solving the
parent node to not be feasible in either child-node. Consider an example as shown on Figure 22a
where the truck traverses arc (a, b) early in the route. Since the truck traverses the arc (a, b) then this
tandem route is not feasible in the 0-child when branching on (a, b) for the truck. Later in the route
the truck returns to customer b which is allowed by ng-relaxation if it has visited a customer in the
meantime who does not have b in their Ni set. This means that b has been visited twice, where one
time the arc (a, b) was not traversed by a truck. If we allow such routes in the 1-child then we can no
longer guarantee that the cumulative truck ﬂow on arc (a, b) in the optimal MP solution of the 1-child
is 1. Another way to argue for why it should not be allowed in either of the children, is that the route
could not be generated in either of the children when obeying the propagation rules from section 9.3
above. Speciﬁcally, the truck arc a →b early in the route is disallowed in the 0-child and the truck
arc i →b later in the route is disallowed in the 1-child.
For another example where we instead consider customer branching see Figure 22b. Here the same
tandem serves customer a twice, once with the drone and once with the truck meaning that this route
is not feasible in either children if we branch on customer a.
(a) Branching on the truck arc (a, b) would make
this route infeasible in both child nodes. The drone
has been omitted for simplicity since the truck’s
movement alone is enough to cause the infeasibility.
(b) Branching on the customer a would make this
route infeasible in both child nodes. Only the part of
the drone’s movement that is involved in the
infeasibility has been shown for simplicity.
Figure 22: Examples of tandem routes which are neither feasible in the 0- nor 1-child after branching.
The slightly more speciﬁc requirements for non-elementary tandem routes to be feasible in the 0- and
1- child nodes are:
When we have branched on customer a ∈C:
• 0-child: If the total ﬂow of trucks into a is 0.
• 1-child: If the total ﬂow of trucks into a equals the number of times a is served in total.
When we have branched on a truck arc (a, b):
• 0-child: If the truck never traverses the arc (a, b).
68
==PAGE:74==
• 1-child: If the number of times the arc (a, b) is traversed by the truck is equal to the number of
times a is served and the number of times b is served.
When we have branched on a drone arc (a, b):
• 0-child: If the drone never traverses the arc (a, b) alone (ﬂies).
• 1-child: If the number of times the drone traverses the arc (a, b) alone (ﬂies) is equal to the
number of times a is served and the number of times b is served.
9.5.2
Solving RMP to IP-optimality
Another possible improvement which makes use of the columns generated during column generation
could be to attempt to solve the current RMP to integer optimality if the optimal RMP solution was
not already integer. The hopes of this is to be able to ﬁnd good integer solutions early in the tree.
However, solving the RMP to integer optimality is expected to be more time consuming than solving
it to LP optimality even though it is a somewhat simple set partitioning problem. Additionally, it is
not guaranteed to have an integer solution at all or, if it does, there is no guarantee that it is good.
It could make sense to attempt to solve the root node RMP to integer optimality in order to quickly
reduce the objective value gap on the SP solution. How many nodes it makes sense to attempt this
at, depends on how time consuming it turns out to be to solve the RMPs to integer optimality, and
how often they lead to any good solutions.
9.5.3
Search strategy in the tree
As a ﬁnal improvement we experimented with diﬀerent tree-search strategies for the Branch and Bound
procedure. Among typically used options ([Wolsey, 1998]) are:
• Best First in which the next B&B node to be explored is always the one with the best dual
bound (lower bound in our case).
• Breadth First in which the next B&B node is chosen from among the oldest open nodes.
• Depth First in which the next B&B node is chosen from among the newest open nodes.
What we have implemented are Best First and Breadth First searches. When branching on arcs we
expect the search tree to be unbalanced because it will be much more restrictive to branch 1 on an
arc than to branch 0 on it. We also expect it to be more likely that integer solutions are found in the
1-children while it may be necessary to branch many more times in a 0-branch in order to obtain an
integer solution. With this in mind we expect a Breadth First search strategy that always explores the
1-child before the 0-child to be eﬃcient for ﬁnding integer solutions early in the search.
This is valuable because ﬁnding an early integer solution helps to prune as many nodes as possible.
On the other hand, we fear that this search strategy may be ill-suited for reducing the root node gap
because the deep 0-branches may have very small lower bounds that only slightly increase for each
layer we go deeper. This is a concern if we do not expect to be able to close the whole tree within the
time limit.
On the other hand, the Best First strategy will aim to increase the lower bound on the root node early
and once an integer solution is found it is expected to be good, allowing us to prune more aggressively.
However, it may take many iterations before the ﬁrst integer solution is found.
We do not expect branching on customers to attribute to an unbalanced search tree as much because
the constraints in both child-nodes are similarly restrictive.
Results of experiments with these improvements can be found in section 10.12.
69
==PAGE:75==
10
Results and empirical analysis
All experiments are, unless otherwise noted, carried out on a Intel Xeon Processor 2650v4 with 12
cores, 2.20GHz. There were 8GB of RAM available although the memory consumption is generally
less than 2 GB. As MIP solver we used CPLEX 12.10 and gave it access to 4 cores while the rest of
the code was only granted access to 1.
The time-limit for experiments are, unless otherwise noted, always 1 hour. As mentioned in section 5 we
use the instance from [Sacramento et al., 2019] since they are easily available and it makes comparison
easy. However in that set there are no instance between sizes 12 and 20. Thus the instances of size 14
and 16 that are mentioned in the following sections are made by us, following the procedure described
in [Sacramento et al., 2019], and are named following the same convention.

10.2
Improving dual stabilisation
Initial values for box sizes:
In section 6.2.4 we discussed the intuition behind how the box sizes could be initialised.
Based on how λi and λi+ in some way reﬂect the diﬃculty of serving customer i we chose to initialise
them as:
λi = cT
0,i
∀i ∈C
λi+ = cT
0,i
∀i ∈C
λi−= −cT
0,i
∀i ∈C
We have no good estimates for λi−which is why we decided to simply make the full-box symmetric
to begin with. If full-box dual stabilisation should turn out to be beneﬁcial then a more thoughtful
initialisation of these values would be ideal.
The initial values of λi and λi+ may be an overestimate because subsequently visited customers in a
route are typically closer to each other than to the depot, so their addition to the route cost may be
less than cT
0,i. However, from the results in Table 8 we learn that it is still necessary to increase these
values multiple times before satisfying the stop criteria so they are small enough to have an impact.
An alternative initialisation that would have been interesting to try if more time had been available
would be to use the value of the initial solution generated before column generation starts as an
estimate for the primal objective value of the MP and then split this number between the customers
in a way that reﬂects their distance to the depot.
10.2.1
Frameworks and Box type
In section 6.2 we introduced half-box and full-box dual stabilisation and the changes to the column
generation framework when applying either of them. Later, in section 7.4 we introduced a number of
modiﬁcations for when the PP is speciﬁcally solved with dynamic programming and varying levels of
label removal.
What we went on to implement were three diﬀerent column generation frameworks:
1. A half-box dual stabilisation framework as presented in section 6.2 where the PP is solved with
dynamic programming and if it does not return any improving columns is immediately solved
again with a higher level label remover without considering the stop criteria ﬁrst.
2. A half-box dual stabilisation framework in the modiﬁed version presented in section 7.4 where
exact label removal is only used when the stop criteria are satisﬁed.
3. A full-box dual stabilisation framework also in the modiﬁed version from section 7.4.
In Table 8 we have solved all the instances with up to 12 customers and for each instance and framework
reported:
Header
Explanation
Obj
The objective value
It
The number of times the PP is solved with level 1 heuristic label removal
Exact
The number of times the PP is solved with exact label removal.
Time
Time taken to solve the instance
72
==PAGE:78==
For the full-box dual stabilisation framework, Table 8 further contains the number of times a λi+ or
λi−variable is updated during the column generation procedure. These are found under the headers
"+ " and "-" respectively. The reason we count the number of updates is to gain more insight into
how important the lower bound is.
In the version of the code used to solve the PP in these experiments, the second level heuristic label
remover had not yet been developed.
The values of the not-yet-tuned parameters were set to the same in all the three frameworks:
ε = ε+ = ε−= 0.1,
αε = αε+ = αε−= 1.1,
αλ = αλ+ = αλ−= 1.5
The initial box sizes λi, λi+, λi−were calculated for each instance as explained above.
The purpose of these initial results were only to compare the 3 frameworks which is why none of the
parameters have been properly tuned yet.

Table 8: Comparing our original and modiﬁed framework structure as well as half- and full-box
stabilisation.
Comparing the ﬁrst two columns in Table 8 we see that the modiﬁed half-box framework uses less than
half of the time in total used by the original framework to solve all instances. Additionally we note
that the number of iterations performed with exact label removal has been reduced which was also the
goal with the modiﬁed framework.
Comparing the two modiﬁed frameworks next we see that the framework with full-box stabilisation
is slower in total. Additionally, the number of updates to λi−is less than a 6th of the number of
updates to λi+. It therefore seems that the actual dual variables seldom take values that are low
74
==PAGE:80==
enough for the current bound to have an impact, and that the added overhead from keeping track of
the negative bounds and their possible violations is large enough that it outweighs any beneﬁt gained
from restricting the variables.
All in all we therefore conclude that the modiﬁed half-box dual stabilisation framework is an improve-
ment compared to the original stabilised framework in section 6.2 and that lower bounding the dual
variables as in the full box framework does not seem to be beneﬁcial compared to only upper bounding
them.
For results, where larger instances are solved with and without dual stabilisation after tuning the
parameters, see section 10.7.
10.2.2
Tuning dual stabilisation
The modiﬁed half-box dual stabilisation framework, which was found to be best in the test above,
contains several parameters that need to be assigned a value.
Apart from the initial values of λi these are: αεi and αλi as well as the initial values of εi.
While we have presented the parameters with a customer index i, what we try is to have one value for
all i ∈C. To reﬂect this choice the subscript i will be omitted from the rest of this section.
Our very ﬁrst attempt of tuning these values is reported in Appendix G.1. The reason we do not show
it here is that we decided to repeat the experiments because the instances solved initially were only
the 12 instances of size 12 which made the diﬀerences in cumulative run times so small that it was
hard to make clear conclusions based on the data.
Below we present two batches of results: First we tuned the parameters only on column generation
with ng-relaxation. Later we repeated the tuning experiments with an improved framework and tuned
the values both with and without ng-relaxation.
Tuning dual stabilisation with ng-route relaxation and 2 levels of label removal:
Our intuition is that avoiding extreme dual solutions is especially important when the PP is ng-relaxed
as it will otherwise seem very beneﬁcial to produce routes that over-serve customers with associated
large dual variables λi. While these routes have low reduced costs their actual costs may be quite large
and thus not of interest to the RMP when choosing columns for an optimal solution.
Based on this we decided to tune the dual stabilisation parameters ε, αε and αλ on a version of column
generation with modiﬁed half-box dual stabilisation and ng-relaxation of the PP with Ni set size 5. In
this version only two levels of label removal had been implemented, namely the level 1 heuristic and
exact label removal.
The values we tried for the three parameters were all 64 combinations of:
ε = [0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7]
αε = [1.0, 1.1, 1.2, 1.3]
αλ = [1.1, 1.5, 3.0, 5.0]
With each triple of values (ε, αε, αλ) we solved all 24 instances of size 12 and 14 and recorded the
cumulative time spent to solve all instances.
This produces a 4 dimensional dataset so what we
present here is:
• Table 9 reports the 10 triples with smallest accumulated run time out of all triples tried.
• Figure 23 contains heat maps of the accumulated run time for each triple where one of the values
in the best triple (ε, αε, αλ) = (0.5, 1.3, 1.1) has been locked and the other two are varied on
the axes.

(0.1, 1.2, 1.1)
