Table 9: 10 best triples for the parameter values (ε, αε, α¯λ) when solving instances of size 12 and 14
with ng-relaxation.
76
==PAGE:82==
Figure 23: Heat maps over the accumulated run times when solving all instances of size 12 and 14
with various triples. Each heat map shows a "slice" of data where the ﬁeld with a green star
corresponds to the best triple (ε, αε, α¯λ) = (0.5, 1.3, 1.1) and for each plot one of the three
parameter values is set to the one from the best triple.
77
==PAGE:83==
Looking at the two bottom plots in Figure 23 as well as the triples in Table 9 it is very clear that the
value of αλ has a minimal impact on performance. The ﬁrst 4 triples have (ε, αε) = (0.5, 1.3) while
taking all 4 values of αλ and the diﬀerence in cumulative run time is only a few seconds. The next 4
triples then take values (ε, αε) = (0.7, 1.2) and all four values of αλ and the diﬀerence in accumulated
run time between triple 5 and 8 is considerably less then between triple 4 and 5 so the other two
parameter values seem to have a larger impact.
Additionally it seems that the best values of ε and αε are related in some way. Note that the best value
for these two according to Table 9 are (ε, αε) = (0.5, 1.3) and the next best are (ε, αε) = (0.7, 1.2)
instead of something like (ε, αε) = (0.5, 1.2) or (ε, αε) = (0.7, 1.3). This may also explain the results
in the top heat map of Figure 23. It seems that the best values for (ε, αε) are found in the top right
corner where both parameters take high values but there is not a monotonic decrease when leaving
this corner in either direction.
Out of the parameter values tested the triple (0.5, 1.3, 1.1) gave the best results but here the value
of αε = 1.3 is a corner point of the values tested so ideally we would try higher values. However, we
also see from the colour-bar in the heat maps that the impact of changing from the current optimal
parameter values to nearby triples of parameter values is relatively small. This is why we have used the
values (ε, αε, α¯λ) = (0.5, 1.3, 1.1) for most of the following experiments without returning to tuning
until late in the project after exploring other ways of improving performance.
Tuning dual stabilisation with and without ng-relaxation with improved CG
After adding the second level heuristic label remover we performed another dual stabilisation tuning
experiment. Since we found previously that the value of αλ seemed to have almost no impact on
performance we only tuned the values of ε and αε in these experiments. The instances solved were
again the instances of size 12 and 14 but this time we used column generation both with and without
ng-relaxation.
The heat maps showing the accumulated run times for the various pairs of parameter values are shown
in Figures 24-25. The white triangles with pink border indicate that one or more instances could not
be solved within the time limit of 1 hour for this pair of parameter values so the accumulated run time
depicted is misleading. The green star indicates the ﬁeld with values (ε, αε) = (0.5, 1.3) which were
found to be best in the previous tuning experiment.
78
==PAGE:84==
Figure 24: Tuning of the parameters ε and αε using stabilised column generation without
ng-relaxation. The white triangles with pink border show ﬁelds where column generation did not
ﬁnish within the time limit of 1 hour for at least one instance.
Figure 25: Tuning of the parameters ε and αε with ng-relaxation. The white triangles with pink
border show ﬁelds where column generation did not ﬁnish within the time limit of 1 hour for at least
one instance.
From Figures 24 and 25 we see that which values of ε and αε produce the best results diﬀer from when
ng-relaxation is used or not. The pairs that gave the best results in these experiments were:
Plain CG:
ε = 0.4
αε = 1.2
CG with ng-relaxation:
ε = 0.6
αε = 1.4
79
==PAGE:85==
Based on these it seems that ng-relaxation beneﬁts from a more aggressive early dual stabilisation
penalty. However, a surprising observation from these experiments is that the impact of changing the
values of (ε, αε) is greatest on the version of column generation without ng-relaxation even though we
expected it to be opposite.
Going from (ε, αε) = (0.5, 1.3) which were the values found to be best previously to instead use
(ε, αε) = (0.4, 1.2) for the version of CG without dual stabilisation reduces the total run time by more
than 500 seconds. The only experiments involving dual stabilisation performed after learning that
performance could be improved with these values of (ε, αε) are the ones found in Table 25.
10.3
Multiple layers of heuristic label removal
In section 7.3 we introduced 3 levels of label removal, a level 1 heuristic, two level 2 heuristics, and the
exact label removal (dominance). In these experiments we compare performance when using diﬀerent
combinations of label removers. The column generation framework used as basis in these experiments
is the plain version without dual stabilisation, ng-relaxation or bi-directional labelling.
As mentioned in section 7.3 the goal with the multiple layers of label removal is to make it possible to
achieve good, but not necessarily optimal, solutions to the PP faster than what would be possible if it
was solved to exact optimality.
The ﬁrst level heuristic is a meant to be very aggressive in removing labels, in order to produce columns
as fast as possible. The second level heuristic constitutes a compromise between the ﬁrst and last levels
since it is expected to keep more labels per generation of propagation, making it less likely to miss
improving columns that exist while also making it slower to solve than the ﬁrst level heuristic.
Notation:
In these experiments we solved all the instances of size up to 12 to LP optimality using column
generation with the following 5 versions of the pricing problem:
Header
Explanation
Exact only
PP is solved to exact optimality always
1lvl(5)
PP solved with the level 1 heuristic and only when no improving columns
are found is it solved to exact optimality.
2lvl(5,50)
PP is solved with level 1 heuristic. When no improving columns are found
it is solved again with the level 1 heuristic except 50 labels are kept per
generation instead of 5 (The ﬁrst level 2 heuristic in section 7.3).
Exact iterations are only performed when neither of the previous levels
return any improving columns.
2lvl(5,20)
Same as above except 20 labels are kept per generation in level 2.
2lvl(5,dom*)
Same as previous except as second level heuristic, labels are removed
according to the dominance rules omitting the requirement S1 ⊆S2
(The second level heuristic labelled dom* in section 7.3)
1lvl(dom*)
PP is solved with the level 2 heuristic dom* for as long as improving columns are
found, before exact iterations are performed, skipping the level 1 heuristic entirely.
Results:
The run times and number of times the PP was solved with a certain level label remover is shown for
each instance in Table 10.
80
==PAGE:86==
Reading through the results from left to right we see that introducing the ﬁrst level heuristic vastly
reduced the number of exact iterations. The collective run time taken to solve all instances is less than
20% of what it was when the PP was only solved with exact label removal.
Moving on, the 3 experiments with 2 level heuristics all manage to reduce the total number of exact
iterations but only "2 lvl(5,dom*)" manages to reduce the total run time below that of "1lvl(5)".
Our initial thought was that the level 2 heuristic iterations in "2lvl(5,20)" and "2lvl(5,50)" were in
themselves too time consuming so even though they manage to reduce the number of exact iterations
they add too much spent time themselves.
In Table 11 however, we see that this is not the case. Here we have solved the instances of size 12 again
with "lvl2(5,dom*), "lvl2(5,20)",and "lvl2(5,50)" and kept track of how much time was spent on solving
the PP with the various levels of label removal as well as the time taken to solve the RMP. We see
here that the level 2 iterations in "lvl2(5,dom*)" take 0.222 seconds on average while for "lvl2(5,20)"
they only take 0.008 seconds and contribute much less to the total run time. The reason that that
"lvl2(5,dom*)" outperform the other two must be because of the at least 17 fewer exact iterations
performed which could indicate that dom* is better at ﬁnding improving columns since fewer exact
iterations are necessary.
Recall that whenever we solve the PP with a level label remover then the PP has previously been
attempted solved with all lower level label removers as well. It therefore leads to "double work" each
time a higher level iteration is performed because it means solving the same Pricing Problem one
more time. For "lvl2(5,dom*)" we see that only 123 exact iterations are needed in total because the
lower level heuristics are suﬃcient to produce a lot of good columns to the RMP. To decrease the time
spent on re-solving the PP we experimented with using dom* as the ﬁrst and only level heuristic label
remover since we know that it is apparently very good at producing columns such that the exact PP
is solved less often.
These results are found under the header "1lvl(dom*)" and reveal that the total number of iterations
have been decreased at the expense of only a single extra exact iteration. However, the total run-time
is worsened from when the level 1 heuristic was included. Referring again to table 11 this may not be
so surprising since a ﬁrst level iteration is on the order of 100 times faster than a dom* iteration.
A ﬁnal observation from looking at Table 11 is that an overwhelming majority of the time taken to
solve an instance is spent on solving the PP to exact optimality. Thus reducing the number of exact
iterations is the greatest single impact we can make to reduce run-time.

10.4
Number of columns from the PP
From Table 11 above, we learned that the time spent solving the RMP is less than a tenth of the
time spent solving the PP. In order to try and reduce the number of times that the PP is solved we
experiment with adding several columns at once. The potential draw-back from doing this is that the
RMP can become unnecessarily complicated to solve, with several columns of too poor quality to be
used.
The ﬁrst experiments where we tried to tune this number are presented in Appendix J and concluded
that 4 columns gave the best performance out of the values 3-6. However, in these experiments we
only changed the number of improving columns that the PP could return at a time, but we kept
the number of labels kept by the level 1 heuristic label remover at 5. As we saw in Table 10 the
majority of times that the PP is solved it is solved with the level 1 heuristic label remover and since
this only keeps 5 labels per generation then choosing more than 5 labels between them becomes almost
meaningless, since at least some of these labels would be "related". By related, we mean that they
have been propagated from the same label or are previous states of the same label. We do not expect
such related labels to be of much use to the RMP since they will be similar in structure and therefore
cannot be combined in an integer solution.
In the series of experiments we present here, we therefore instead tuned both the number of columns
returned by the PP and the number of labels kept per generation in the level 1 heuristic, trying only
combinations where at least as many labels are kept as columns returned. It may have been interesting
to experiment with varying the number of columns returned for the diﬀerent levels of label removal in
the PP but we unfortunately did not have suﬃcient time to try this.
Figure 26 contains a heat-map of the cumulative time taken to solve all instances of size 12, using
plain lvl2(5,dom*) column generation framework, with the various settings for the number of columns
returned and labels kept. The dark blue area are the combinations which we did not try as the number
of columns is greater than the number of labels.
83
==PAGE:89==
Figure 26: Heat-map of run-times when testing out increasing number of labels kept by the level 1
heuristic as well as the number of columns returned by the PP. Combinations with # labels > #
columns where not tested and are marked with blue on the heat-map.
We see that the best settings involve both keeping a lot of labels as well as returning a lot of columns. In
fact the optimal value corresponds to keeping 200 labels and returning 200 columns. This experiment
was performed late in the project so for most other experiments 4 columns are returned by the PP and
5 labels are kept. The only later experiments where we changed to keeping 200 columns and labels are
the ones in Table 16 and 25.
10.5
ng-relaxation: Ni-set size
In section 8.1 we introduced ng-route relaxation as a means to make the PP easier to solve but at the
possible cost of the quality of the bound returned since the RMP is granted access to non-elementary
routes.
In [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] it is stated that the usual size used for the Ni sets are 8-10 but for
the TSPD, the paper itself uses |Ni| = 5. Using our non-stabilised column generation framework where
ng-relaxation is used to solve the PP, we solved all instances up to size 12. For these experiments we
used 3 sizes for the Ni-sets: 8 and 5 inspired by [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] as well as |Ni| = 12.
Because the largest instances solved have 12 customers then with |Ni| = 12 the ng-relaxation will
have no actual impact on the PP. The reason we included this experiment is to be able to evaluate
the impact on computational performance that the modiﬁcations to the code related to ng-relaxation
have.
Table 12 contains the results of these experiments. For each instance we report the objective value,
number of iterations, run time and the total number of labels kept when solving the Pricing Problem
in the last iteration of column generation.

Table 12: Results when solving instances without ng-relaxation as well as with ng-relaxation with
diﬀerent Ni set sizes. The number of columns returned from the PP in all experiments were 4.
Comparison: No ng vs |Ni| = 5, |Ni| = 8
From Table 12 it is clear that ng-relaxation is unfavourable for the instances of size 6. For the instances
of size 10 ng-relaxation is on average slower for all Ni-set sizes however for |Ni| = 5 the total number of
labels kept is smaller than without ng-relaxation. Moving on to the instances of size 12 both |Ni| = 8
and |Ni| = 5 reduce the average number of labels kept compared to without ng-relaxation but they are
still slower in total. For |Ni| = 5 we notice that there are some instances with small grid size where the
instance is solved faster with ng-relaxation than without it. Our previous experience is that a smaller
grid size results in a harder problem, possibly because drone endurance becomes less relevant leading
to more feasible solutions.
Looking at the impact of adding the two extra customers from the instances with 10 to the instances
with 12 customers we see that the total number of labels and run-time increases as:
85

Based on this we conclude that while it is currently not favourable to use ng-relaxation for the instance
sizes tested, this may not be the case for larger instance. For |Ni| = 5 we see that it scales better than
without ng-relaxation and with 12 customers we are already seeing that |Ni| = 5 gives better results
in terms of computational time on certain of the harder instances.
For a comparison between using and not using ng-relaxation on larger instances than solved here, see
section 10.7.
Comments on |Ni| = 12:
As expected ng-relaxation with set size |Ni| = 12 has no direct impact on the column generation
procedure. We see that the objective value, number of iterations and number of labels in the last
iteration is unaﬀected for all instances. However, the impact on run time is concerning. The total
accumulated run time has more than doubled from without ng-relaxation to with |Ni| = 12. Our best
suggestion for this behaviour is the increased number of intersections made both during propagations
and during exact label removal:
From the experiments enclosed in Appendix H.1 we learned that the dominance check, S1 ⊆S2,
in (dom1 c) and (dom2 c) is apparently quite time consuming in practice. In those experiments we
managed to achieve a signiﬁcant speed-up by re-arranging the order in which we check the various
dominance conditions such that it is the last thing to be considered.
In practice how dominance has been implemented is, that we check whether S1 ⊆S2 by actually
checking S1 = S2 and S1 ⊂S2 separately. This is because we ﬁrst dominate new labels with the other
new labels generated, in which case we know that they all have |S1| = |S2|, so we can simply check if
S1 = S2. Subsequently we attempt to dominate the newly generated labels with older labels in which
case |S1| < |S2| so we have to check whether S1 ⊂S2 by checking if S1 = S1 ∩S2.
With ng-relaxation two labels from the same generation of propagations are not guaranteed to have
|S1| = |S2| so instead of checking whether S1 = S2 we have to check S1 ⊆S2 which involves ﬁnding
the intersection S1 ∩S2. Since all labels with the same (iT , iD)-pair are checked for dominance against
each other this adds an enormous amount of intersections which were not there previously.
Additionally, recall from section 8.1 that when propagating a label with a truck arc, combined arc or
drone sortie, Sng is not just updated to include the new customer visited, j, but it is also intersected
with Nj. This, too, adds thousands, if not millions, of times where intersections are calculated which
were not there in the version where the PP was solved without ng-relaxation.
Below we investigate, more in-depth, the eﬀect of these added intersections:
10.5.1
Proﬁling the procedure to solve instance 12.10.2
To obtain a better understanding of why ng−route relaxation with Ni sets of size 12 increases run
time we proﬁled the column generation procedure when solving instance 12.10.2. This instance was
chosen based on having the largest amount of labels so that if the intersections discussed above are
the problem then it should be clear to see.
Tables 13 and 14 both show the percentage of call backs in the various parts of the column generation
procedure based on 31719 and 45715 total call backs for the plain column generation and the ng-relaxed
column generation respectively.

From Tables 13-14 we see that the ng-relaxed version spends a much greater percentage of time solving
the Pricing Problem, in particular it spends much more time checking the dominance conditions.
Going into code speciﬁcs, 81.6% of all call backs are to 4 lines in the dominance function. These
are the 4 lines in which we check conditions (dom1 c) and (dom2 c).
Based on this we conclude
that the added intersections during propagation of labels, are not the greatest time-sink. The added
intersections needed for dominance are, however detrimental to performance.
10.6

where we compare 3 diﬀerent versions of column generation which make increasing use of bi-directional
labelling. Speciﬁcally we compare the following three variants:

Table 15: Comparing plain column generation, column generation with bi-directional labelling in
exact iterations and bi-directional labelling in both exact iterations and level 2 heuristic iterations.
Instance 14.5.3. was not solved within the time-limit for the plain version so it has been omitted from
all sub totals and totals as indicated by "(-)".
From Table 15 we see that bi-directional labelling is beneﬁcial when used in the exact iterations, but
almost becomes a hindrance when used in the level 2 heuristic iterations as well. Especially make note
of the almost doubling of the total number of exact iterations when using bi-directional labelling in the
level 2 heuristic iterations. Additionally, only 17 times out of the 353 times that the level 2 heuristic
is used to solve the PP does it succeed, the rest of the time it is followed immediately by an exact
iteration.
To explain the behaviour observed in Table 15 we notice the following "vulnerability" of bi-directional
labelling: Some tandem routes can only be generated by merging two speciﬁc half-routes as discussed
in section 8.2. This means, that if an otherwise good solution to the PP consists of two half-routes
where one of them is, in and of itself, not a good half-route, except that it ﬁts well with the other good
half-route, then that ﬁrst half-route may easily be discarded by the level 2 heuristic label remover.
An example could be a half-route which serves a far oﬀcustomer making it expensive but also ensuring
that all customers can be visited by the resulting full route when merged with another half-route. Any
other half-route with the same iD, iT but smaller resource consumption who has for instance not served
the far oﬀcustomer can result in the ﬁrst half-route label being discarded because the label-entry S is
not considered in the level 2 heuristic label remover.
This makes it harder for the level 2 heuristic iterations to produce the columns with lowest reduced
cost and therefore it may be more common for the level 2 heuristic to fail at ﬁnding improving columns
even if they exist, when solved with bi-directional labelling as we see in Table 15.
88
==PAGE:94==
With the exact label removal, a half-route label can only dominate another half-route label if it can
"take its place" in merging, since the S-entry is also considered, so this discussed vulnerability is not
a problem for exact iterations.
The reason that we see some variations in the number of iterations between the ﬁrst two columns in
Table 15 is probably because our choice to only produce one tandem route out of any two half-routes
when merging compared to only producing one full route per label when ﬁnalising can lead to some
variations in the columns returned from the PP when we return more than 1 at a time. This can have
an impact on the RMP solutions and thus the column generation procedure.
In the end we see that bi-directional labelling is a large advantage for the exact iterations but it is also
most beneﬁcial to only use it in the exact iterations.
10.7
The Big Column Generation Comparison
In this experiment we solved instances with 12 to 16 customers to LP-optimality with 6 diﬀerent varia-
tions our the column generation framework, the results are shown in Table 16 where the abbreviations
used in the headers mean:
• plain - Pricing problem solved with dynamic programming and no "tricks"
• fwbw - Pricing problem solved with bi-directional labelling in exact iterations.
• NG - Pricing problem solved with ng route relaxation where |Ni| = 5 based on the results from
section 10.5.
• Stab - The column generation framework uses modiﬁed half-box dual stabilisation with εi =
0.5, αεi = 1.3, αλi = 1.1, ∀i ∈C
In all cases the number of improving columns returned by the PP per iteration is up to 200 and the
number of labels kept by the level 1 heuristic label remover per generation of propagations is also 200.
Takeaways from these results:
For the instances of size 12 and 14 the total run time decreases monotonically when going right in the
table, meaning that solving the Pricing Problem with bi-directional labelling has sped up the run time
of column generation compared to the "plain" version. Additionally ng-route relaxation reduces this
run time even more, but at the cost of worse LP bounds. Under the header "Int" we have reported
whether the solution obtained to the MP is integer (Y) or not (N) and we can see that considerably
fewer instances are solved to integer optimality when using ng-relaxation. Thus when implementing
Branch and Bound it is not trivial to tell whether using bi-directional labelling for the Pricing Problem
or ng-route relaxation is better. The addition of dual stabilisation has generally, for instances of size
12 and 14, improved the total run times of all 3 versions slightly. For the instances of size 16 there
is almost no eﬀect though it is slightly worse to solve the instances with stabilisation compared to
without.
For the instances of size 16 several of them cannot be solved within the run time limit by plain column
generation which is why no subtotal has been calculated for these. ng-route relaxation still leads to
faster solutions than bi-directional labelling, but at the aforementioned cost that fewer instances are
solved to integer optimality.

To put into perspective how well the labelling algorithm/dynamic programming is performing we have
included Table 17 where we test two other column generation frameworks. In one the PP is only solved
by solving the MIP from (7). In the other we use the plain column generation framework with 3 levels
of label removal except for the iterations where the PP would be solved with dynamic programming
and exact label removal where we instead solve it with the MIP.
For the former we report the number of column generation iterations, as well as the time spent. For
the latter we report the number of diﬀerent kinds of iterations used, as well as the time spent.
We did not experiment with extracting multiple solutions to the MIP at once when solving it with
CPLEX, so when solving the PP by solving the MIP in (7) we only return one improving column at a
time.

Table 17: Comparing two variants of using the MIP to solve the PP. Again (-) indicates that the

Comparing these results to the ones from Table 16, we see that the column generation frameworks
using only dynamic programming to solve the PP vastly outperform both these two other versions.
It is of course an unfair advantage that the frameworks using dynamic programming can return 200
columns at a time compared to the 1 that the other two can return but the run times are hardly even
comparable as is. Solving most of the instances of size 6 with the MIP-only framework takes twice the
time that "plain" takes to solve all instances of size 12 for instance.
92
==PAGE:98==
The version of column generation which only uses the MIP for the exact iterations is about 6 times
faster but still vastly slower in comparison to the frameworks tested in Table 16.
10.8
Diﬀerent branching options - arcs and customers
In sections 9.1-9.2 we introduced three ways to branch: On customers, truck arcs or drone arcs. In
order to investigate if there is a more beneﬁcial order to prioritise these we experimented with several
branch and price frameworks where we changed the order in which we attempt to branch. When
investigating a speciﬁc type of branching whether it be customers, truck arcs or drone arcs we always
branch on one of the candidates with the most fractional value in the current MP solution.
The column generation framework used in these experiments is plain version with no dual stabilisation
of the master problem and the pricing problem is solved without ng-relaxation or bi-directional labelling
returning up to 4 columns at a time.
The results are shown in Table 18 and includes a header with the branching order. Here "T" means
truck arc and "D" means drone arc. Such that "T-Customer-D" refers to a Branch and Price frame
where we branch on truck arcs for as long as there exist truck arcs with fractional cumulative truck
ﬂow and then move on to branch on customers etc.
For each version of the Branch and Price framework we have reported the number of times it was
necessary to branch on a customer, truck arc and drone arc before terminating. For most instances
the Branch and Price framework manages to ﬁnd the optimal integer solution within the time-limit
of one hour. In the cases where this was not the case we have reported the interval between the best
upper and lower bound on the integer solution at the time where the framework was stopped.
93

2781.702
Table 18: Results for instances of size up to 14, using our Branch and Price procedure. In the left
column we branched on arcs only, while in the remaining 4 we tried various orders for all the types of
branching introduced. The symbol "(-)" indicates that values are excluded from the subtotals and
total because one or more variant(s) did not solve the instance within the time limit.
Our take-aways from these experiments
From Table 18 we see that branching on customer type ﬁrst as in column 2 and 3 leads to considerably
fewer times branched before termination. Thinking back to the MIP and our observation that making
the y-variables binary lead to faster solutions then this makes sense with our expectations. We also
note that when going from the ﬁrst to second column the run-times are decreased considerably by
being allowed to branch on customers ﬁrst.
Comparing the last two columns we see that branching on drone arcs ﬁrst instead of truck arcs leads
to a slower run-time and a higher number of times branched.
We think this observation makes intuitive sense because when we branch on a drone arc we do not
guarantee that the arc is traversed by drones who all perform the same or similar sorties. Consider for
instance the arc (a, b) in Figure 27. All three sorties depicted have a drone traverse arc (a, b) so they
could each be chosen with weight 1
3 and then the cumulative drone ﬂow on the arc (a, b) would be 1
but the cumulative drone ﬂow on many of the nearby arcs would be fractional. The point being that it
may require branching on a great many drone arcs before all drone arcs have integer cumulative ﬂow.
94
==PAGE:100==
It may especially be an issue that the arc (a, b) can be traversed by drones both starting and ending
a sortie as we discussed in section 9.1.2.
Figure 27: If we have branched 1 on the drone arc a 99K b then the solution to the MP for this node
may choose several tandem routes which perform varies sorties where the arc (a, b) is either the start
or the end of a sortie. These tandem routes would be chosen with fractional weights and lead to a
large number of other arcs with fractional cumulative drone ﬂow in the vicinity of (a, b).
Based on these observations the best branching order seems to be:
1. Customers
2. Truck arcs
3. Drone arcs
Comparing to [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] they implemented the same 3-level branching with cus-
tomers, truck arcs and drone arcs. It is interesting that we see for the instances solved that customer
branching alone is suﬃcient to reach integrality. We do not, however, believe this to be guaranteed for
all instances.
10.9
Keeping columns between B&P nodes
As discussed previously, it may be beneﬁcial to keep columns generated when solving a parent node
for when the child node is solved if the columns are feasible w.r.t. the added branching constraint in
the child-node. This adds an overhead for checking which columns are feasible in what child-nodes but
may in turn reduce the number of times the PP needs to generate columns because it does not have
to generate them anew.
In this series of experiments the instances up to size 14 have been solved with two B&P frameworks.
One in which column generation in each node starts only with the initial set of columns needed to
ensure a feasible RMP solution exists. In the other, column generation starts with all the columns
generated in the parent node which are feasible w.r.t. the new branching constraint. If the RMP has
no solution with these columns, additional initial columns are added unless the problem is actually
infeasible.
The results are shown in Table 19 and reported are:
95
==PAGE:101==
Header
Explanation
Obj
Objective value
Branched
Number of times branched before terminating.
Cols Kept
Total number of columns that B&B nodes, which were solved, had saved from their
parent node.
(Does not include columns saved for child-nodes that were pruned by bounds)
CG It
Total number of column generation iterations across all solved B&B nodes.
BBTime
Time taken to solve instance to IP optimality with Branch & Price.
CGTime
Total time spent across all B&B nodes solved to obtain LP optimality
with column generation.
Overhead
Diﬀerence between the previous two, i.e. the amount of time spent during
Branch & Price which is not spent on solving the separate nodes. This time involves
ﬁnding what to branch on, choosing next node and ﬁnding out which columns to keep
in the two child-nodes, updating the best known IP solution etc.
The column generation framework used is the plain version returning up to 4 columns from the PP at
a time. In both Branch & Price frameworks the branch order is the one found to be best before: 1)
Customer, 2) truck arc, 3) drone arc.

Table 19: Comparing Branch & Price where columns are kept or not kept from parent node to child
node.
Our take-aways from this experiment:
First we note that for most instances no branching is necessary because the solution found in the root
node is integer which is why many of them have 0 in "Cols Kept". For the instances where branching
is necessary, keeping columns has managed to reduce the total number of column generation iterations
by more than 2000 in total. This is a decrease of ∼35% in the total number of column generation
iterations across all instances including those where no branching occurs. Additionally the total time
97
==PAGE:103==
to solve all instances has decreased noticeably. The total overhead time is smaller but within the range
of what could be explained by variance. All in all we conclude that keeping columns between parent
nodes and child nodes is beneﬁcial.
10.10
Combining B&P with dynamic programming improvements
In section 9.4 we discussed how all or some of the nodes in the Branch & Bound tree could be solved to
LP optimality with the modiﬁed column generation frameworks that use ng-relaxation or bi-directional
labelling.
In these experiments we compare 4 variants of B&P:
1. B&P where the nodes are solved with plain column generation
2. B&P where the nodes with only customer branching are solved with column generation using
bi-directional labelling and nodes with any arc branching are solved with plain column generation
3. B&P where the nodes are solved with column generation where the PP is ng-relaxed with Ni-set
size 5
4. Similar but with Ni-set size 8
In all versions, columns are kept between parent and child node if they are feasible w.r.t. the branching
constraints, the branch order is customer, truck arc, drone arc and the PP may return up to 4 improving
columns at a time.
The results of these experiments are shown in Table 20 and reported are:

fwbw
Number of B&B nodes solved with bi-directional labelling

Table 20: Comparison of 4 diﬀerent versions of Branch & Price. Plain CG refers to column
generation where the pricing problem is solved without extensions. Bi-directional+plain CG refers to
column generation where bi-directional labelling is used for the pricing problem as long as no arcs
have been branched on, in which case the plain version is used.
Focusing on the ﬁrst two columns of Table 20 ﬁrst we see that for the instances solved, all B&B nodes
can be solved with bi-directional labelling because none of them involve arc branching. Doing this has
reduced the total time taken to solve all instances by about 30%.
Going further in the table we see that the total number of B&B nodes solved drastically increases
when using ng-relaxation with set size |Ni| = 8 and it further increases for |Ni| = 5. This makes
sense since ng-relaxation makes the PP easier to solve at the cost of the bound returned as we also
observed in Table 16. With worse bounds it then makes sense that more branching is necessary to
obtain integrality. Comparing to the plain variant, the total time taken by Branch & Price is almost
99
==PAGE:105==
the same as with |Ni| = 8 despite the more than doubled number of B&B nodes solved which shows
how much faster each separate node can be solved.
With the instance sizes solved here the variant "Bi-directional CG" is a clear winner. However we
should keep in mind how we saw previously that with ng-relaxation the run times scaled much better
10.11
Adding dual stabilisation
The later tuning of only the two dual stabilisation parameters ε and αε in section 10.2.2 showed that
the best values diﬀered depending on whether the PP was ng-relaxed or not and that for plain column
generation it made a large impact on the solution time to change the values. Therefore, we experimented
with dual stabilisation one more time, adding dual stabilisation to the column generation frameworks
for the 4 variants of Branch & Price tested above. For "Plain CG" and "Bi-directional+plain CG" we
set:
αλi = 1.1
εi = 0.4
αεi = 1.2
∀i ∈C
For "ng |Ni| = 5" and "ng |Ni| = 8" we set:
αλi = 1.1
εi = 0.6
αεi = 1.4
∀i ∈C
All other settings are unchanged from before. The results are shown in Table 21 and in Table 22 we
compare the total number of BB nodes solved and total run times across the two experiments with
and without dual stabilisation.

Table 22: The total time taken to solve all instances and the total number of B&B nodes solved across

From Table 22 we see that the conclusions are similar with stabilisation as they were without: Bi-
directional labelling is a clear winner but "ng |Ni| = 8" could still be relevant for larger instances.
Comparing the 4 variants with and without dual stabilisation in Table 22 we see that "Bidirec-
tional+plain CG" seemingly beneﬁts from dual stabilisation while "Plain CG" does not.
This is
surprising because the parameter values were tuned on plain column generation and their process
should be the same. Some of this diﬀerence in behaviour may be explained by variance in run time
since all instances were only solved once, however the diﬀerence seems to be signiﬁcant.
For the two versions with ng-relaxation we see that "ng |Ni| = 5" no longer times out on any instances
in Table 21 and that the run-time and number of nodes solved for "ng |Ni| = 8" are reduced. As we
have already discussed previously we think it makes sense that with ng-relaxation avoiding extreme
dual solutions is important.
10.12
B&P further improvements
In these experiments we have tried to implement some of the improvements discussed in section 9.5.
As a basis for the experiments we are using the Branch & Price framework "Bi-directional + plain
CG" from before without stabilisation. This framework is referred to as "Reference".
To this framework we have then experimented with adding the following modiﬁcations:
Header
Explanation
One IP
Upon termination of column generation in the root node, the RMP is
attempted solved to integer optimality
More IP
Every node for which the (R)MP solution is non-integer is subsequently
attempted solved to IP optimality
Best First
Breadth First search strategy is replaced by a Best First strategy as
described in section 9.5.3
Best First More IP
Best First search strategy is combined with attempting to solve the RMP
to integer optimality in each node.
The goal with "One IP" and "More IP" is to obtain a ﬁrst integer solution early in the search so
that some nodes can be pruned before looked at. The reason that we experiment with both solving
the RMP for only the root node and for all nodes is that we cannot predict whether it will be too
time-consuming to solve the RMP to integer optimality compared to how much it aids the search.
The goal with "Best First" is, as we discussed already, to reduce the root node gap faster and to ﬁnd
an integer solution of good quality which is important for pruning. We tried to combine the Fest First
strategy with "More IP" in order to both ﬁnd an integer solution early but also hopefully ﬁnd an
integer solution of good quality.
The results are shown in Table 23. "BBNodes" indicates the number of B&B-nodes looked at while
"Solved" contains how many of them were solved, i.e. if a node was pruned by bounds (has a lower
bound worse than the current best integer solution) then it is counted in "BBNodes" but not in
"Solved".

6629.752
Table 23: Experimenting with modifying the Branch and Price search procedure to achieve good
integer solutions earlier. "BBNodes" refers to the number of B&B nodes looked at during the search
wheres "Solved" refer to how many were actually solved. A B&B node which is pruned by bound is
counted in "BBNodes" but not in "Solved".
From Table 23 we see that the impact of the modiﬁcations are only minimal. In all versions including
the one without modiﬁcations the total number of B&B nodes solved is 122 so the modiﬁcations have
not managed to decrease this number by pruning more nodes. The accumulated run times for the 5
versions are all within ±7% of the reference version and it is therefore hard to say if any speed-up have
been achieved or if the diﬀerence is due to variance.
Solving the instances of size 14 one more time we recorded the collective time spent on solving the RMP
to integer optimality, in the nodes where it was attempted, in the two versions "One IP" and "More
IP". These results are shown below in Table 24. The same column generation framework as above
was used where the PP may return up to 4 columns and 5 labels are kept by the ﬁrst level heuristic
label remover. Additionally we repeated the experiment with these numbers set to 200 instead. The
reason we perform this experiment is to see how much time it would add to column generation to solve
the RMP to integer optimality in selected nodes. The results we have presented above did not show
that any impressive beneﬁt came with it, but we suspect that there might be beneﬁts to it for other
instances where more branching is necessary. Thus we want to investigate if it can be added to the
103
==PAGE:109==
framework without adding a substantial overhead even when the RMP may contain many columns
because the PP can return up to 200 at a time.

1.308
Table 24: Statistics for how much time is spent solving the RMP to IP optimality in total across all
instances of size 14 compared to the time spent by Branch and Price in total
From Table 24 we see that even when the PP returns up to 200 columns at a time the time taken to solve
the RMP to integer optimality is negligible compared to the overall time spent by Branch-and-Price
so we decide to use the improvements from "Best First More IP" as we believe these improvements to
have potential, even if they did not lead to noticeable improvements in these speciﬁc experiments.
10.13
Solving the VRPD with Branch and Price
In previous experiments we have continuously solved the same instances multiple times and made
decisions on how to improve our Branch-and-Price frameworks based on solving them. A concern with
this is that we may over-ﬁt our parameters and choices to the speciﬁc instances we keep solving. For
this reason we want to test our ﬁnal Branch-and-Price frameworks fairly, on instances that have not
been solved previously. We do this by generating all new instances of sizes 14 and 16 following the
same recipe as previous. We also produce a batch of instances with 18 customers as well as attempt
to solve the instances from [Sacramento et al., 2019] with 20 customers.
The frameworks we test in these experiments include all the improvements we have found during the
previous experiments, including keeping 200 labels and columns and the improvements described above
for "Best First More IP".
The time limit given to our Branch-and-Price framework is 12 hours per instance and the memory
limit is set to 40 GB.
For each framework we report the total run-time as well as the number of B&B nodes solved. For the
frameworks with bi-directional labelling we additionally report how many of those B&B nodes were
solved using column generation with bi-directional labelling.
In case Branch-and-Price runs out of time the current gap on the SP solution is returned. If the time-
limit is reached before the root node has been solved to LP optimality the current RMP is attempted
solved to integer optimality to obtain an integer solution to the SP, in order to obtain an upper bound
on the value of the optimal solution.

Table 25: Comparison of run times and # B&B nodes solved when solving the VRPD with Branch
and Price, using diﬀerent improvements to solve the PP. Only the instances that were solved within
the time-limit by all versions are included in the subtotals.
105
==PAGE:111==
For some instances in Table 25 no objective value or gap is stated, these are instances that we did not
manage to solve because the jobs were not completed successfully due to e.g. running out of memory.
With the way we implemented our labelling algorithm we make all possible labels in each generation
of propagations and then subsequently remove labels from all the labels generated. It may have been
more memory eﬃcient to remove labels during propagations instead of after, and possibly even to
consider the ﬁnalisation of a label immediately after its creation, instead of only ﬁnalising labels upon
termination of label generation. However we did not experiment with these options.
From Table 25 we observe that the framework using bi-directional labelling and no dual stabilisation
outperforms the other frameworks on the instances of size 14, but already from size 16 it starts being
the framework with ng-route relaxation and dual stabilisation that produce the best results. This
framework manages to solve the most instances to optimality within the time-limit and is the framework
to solve most of the larger instances to either optimality or to be able to provide a gap.
Especially notable is it that "ng 8 stab" outperforms bi-directional labelling on the instances of size
16 and 18 despite solving more than 6 times as many Branch & Bound nodes, truly highlighting how
much faster each Branch & Bound node can be solved when employing ng-route relaxation.
The superior scale-ability of ng-relaxation makes sense, since in a way what bi-directional labelling
attempts to do, is to half the eﬀective instance size while ng-route relaxation in its own way reduces
the eﬀective instance size to a speciﬁc number.
When we solved only the LP relaxation we were concerned for whether the improved solution times for
column generation with ng-relaxation would be out-weighed by the worse bounds and thus larger need
for branching, but in these ﬁnal results what we see is actually that ng-relaxation and dual stabilisation
ends up providing the best performing framework, out of the ones implemented, for solving larger
instances.
Adding dual stabilisation to the frameworks with bi-directional labelling and ng-relaxation showed
to be most impactful for the framework with ng-relaxation, becoming more impactful the larger the
instances are.
In this project we only considered Ni sets sizes of 5 or 8 but a proper tuning would have been preferred.
Out of the instances with 20 customers from [Sacramento et al., 2019] we managed to solve 4 of them to
proven optimality. To our knowledge we are the ﬁrst to do so and comparing to [Sacramento et al., 2019]
we are able to conﬁrm that the solution they obtain with ALNS to instances 20.5.1 and 20.5.3 are in
fact optimal, but the solutions they get to instances 20.5.2 and 20.20.1 are not.
Finally we note that the MIP from (1) spends 4319.275 seconds solving all instance of size 12, which is
more than double the time that it takes the framework employing bi-directional labelling to solve all
the instances of size 14.
106
==PAGE:112==
11
Conclusions and Further work
In this project we have presented a compact formulation for the cost minimising VRPD, inspired
by the formulation presented in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] for time minimising TSPD. Comparing
solution times to the MIP solved in [Sacramento et al., 2019], we saw that our presented MIP could
be solved in shorter time on the same instances.
We also presented how 3 variations from our assumptions could be handled by the compact formulation
and how this aﬀected the solution times as well as solution quality.
In order to solve larger instances we additionally implemented and continuously improved the ﬁrst
Branch-and-Price framework for the VRPD variant we consider. During column generation, the Pricing
Problem was solved using a labelling algorithm, with recursions inspired by the recursions presented
in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020]. We experimented with multiple branching decisions, and techniques
such as ng-route relaxation, bi-directional labelling, dual stabilisation, and heuristically solving the
Pricing Problem, in order to improve performance. Some of the frameworks implemented were able to
solve instances of the VRPD with up to 20 customers, though not consistently.
As we improved the implemented Branch-and-Price framework we observed that the majority of the
time taken to solve an instance, was spent on solving the Pricing Problem to optimality and there-
fore future improvements would be most impactful if focused on improving this part. Alternatively,
improvements could also be made by ﬁnding ways to reduce the number of times that the Pricing
Problem needs to be solved to optimality. In our project we managed to do this by introducing label
removers to our labelling algorithm, that constitute a heuristic way to solve the Pricing Problem.
Further work to improve Branch-and-Price for the VRPD could involve combining ng-relaxation and bi-
directional labelling as well as improvements to the initialisation of the box sizes for dual stabilisation.
Considering the impressive results of Branch-and-Cut in [Tamke and Buscher, 2021], a potentially very
impactful improvement could be the addition of cuts, i.e. a Branch & Price & Cut approach, especially
in combination with ng-relaxation to compensate for the fact that it worsens the LP bounds. One type
of cuts to consider could be subset-row cuts as introduced for the VRPTW in [Jepsen et al., 2008].

A
Proof that branching is never necessary on arcs incident to depot
The following proof is by contradiction and considers arcs leaving the depot. The entire proof can
be repeated for arcs entering the depot by reversing the direction of all arcs and is therefore a trivial
extension of what we show.
Assumption:
We assume that there exists at least one arc leaving the depot which has cumulative vehicle ﬂow that
is non-integer but that all inter-customer arcs have integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow such that we cannot
branch on them. We will diﬀerentiate between three cases for the arc leaving the depot:
I) The cumulative truck ﬂow is non-integer and the cumulative drone ﬂow is not.
II) The cumulative drone ﬂow is non-integer and the cumulative truck ﬂow is not.
III) Both the cumulative drone ﬂow and truck ﬂow are non-integer.
In each case we denote the cumulative vehicle ﬂow of the relevant type r where 0 < r < 1. Additionally
we choose to denote the customer incident to the arc, i so the arc can be referred to as (0, i).
To aid the reading process of this proof we have included illustrations of the various cases and sub-cases
considered and referred to them at the start of each paragraph which considers a new sub-case.
Case I)
In this case we assume that all routes, in which the arc (0, i) is traversed by the truck alone or the
truck carrying the drone, have been chosen in the ﬁnal solution with cumulative weight 0 < r < 1.
For the solution to be feasible, customer i has to be serviced with weight exactly 1. Note that even
though some of the cumulative truck ﬂow going into i from the depot may come from trucks carrying
drones then these drones cannot service customer i, only be launched here. Therefore there has to be
a cumulative ﬂow of 1 −r which also go into node i.
All of this cumulative ﬂow must be on inter-customer arcs entering customer i, i.e. at least some of
the ﬂow must be on an arc (j, i) where j is some other customer. The reason that none of the ﬂow can
come from the depot is:
Figure 28a: It cannot be accounted for by truck ﬂow from the depot or the cumulative truck ﬂow on
arc (0, i) would be greater than r. If it is accounted for by drones ﬂying from the depot into i, then
this is not considered Case I but rather Case III since it would mean that there is a cumulative drone
ﬂow on the arc (0, i) with a non-integer value in the interval ]0, r −1].
Figure 28b: Consider the scenario in which some of the missing ﬂow into i is accounted for by truck
ﬂow into i from a customer j such that the cumulative truck ﬂow on arc (i, j) is q > 0. In order for
customer i to not be over-serviced then we know that q ≤1 −r which since 0 < r < 1 means that q is
non-integer and thus this contradicts our assumption that no non-integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow exist
on inter-customer arcs.
Figure 28c: From the above we learn that the remaining ﬂow into i has to be accounted for by drones
ﬂying into i from another customer. If the only drone ﬂow into customer i stems from drones serving
customer i then the above argument can be repeated: There will exist an arc (j, i) with cumulative
drone ﬂow 0 < q ≤1 −r < 1 which contradicts our assumption.
Figure 28d: However, there could also be a ﬂow of drones into i which are recovered by their trucks in
i and therefore do not service this customer. In this case, assume that there exists an arc (j, i) with a
ﬂow of q drones that service customer i and a ﬂow of 1 −q drones that do not. Then the cumulative
drone ﬂow on arc (j, i) is integer. Looking at customer j then this means that a total drone ﬂow of q
needs to be launched here and a total drone ﬂow of 1 −q visit customer j as the midpoint of a sortie
and thus serve this customer but not customer i. The remaining service of customer j is accounted for
110
==PAGE:116==
by a truck ﬂow of q which has to enter customer j in order to launch the ﬂow of q drones.
To summarise: at this point it is known that a ﬂow of q trucks with their drones and 1 −q drones that
ﬂew to customer j must have come from somewhere. Looking at the drones ﬁrst then because they are
retrieved by their trucks in customer i and the only truck ﬂow into customer i came from the depot
then the drones can only have been launched at the depot and hence came from there prior to serving
j. Meanwhile, if the ﬂow of the q trucks have come from any customer k then the cumulative truck
ﬂow on arc (k, j) would be non-integer so they must have come from the depot also. This means that
the arc (0, j) has a ﬂow of q trucks and 1−q ﬂying drones and therefore this would be considered Case
III and not Case I.
(a) If some of the missing ﬂow into i comes from a
non-zero drone ﬂow from the depot then there is a
non-integer cumulative drone ﬂow out of the depot
which is considered in Case III instead of Case I.
(b) If some of the missing ﬂow into i comes from
truck ﬂow into i then there exists an inter-customer
arc (j, i) with non-integer cumulative truck ﬂow.
(c) If all of the missing ﬂow into i comes from drones
and the only drone ﬂow into i is from drones that
service i then this leads to the same contradiction as
in Figure 28b.
(d) If all of the missing ﬂow into i comes from drones
then the only way to not reach immediate
contradiction is if there exists an arc out of the
depot with non-integer cumulative truck and drone
ﬂow which is Case III
Figure 28: Illustrations for the proof of Case I.
Case II)
The considerations in this case are identical to Case I:
Because the cumulative drone-ﬂow on the arc (0, i) is 0 < r < 1 then for customer i to be fully serviced
a remaining amount of ﬂow equal to 1 −r also needs to go into i. None of this ﬂow can come from the
depot because either it would mean that the cumulative drone ﬂow is not r but larger or the cumulative
truck ﬂow on the arc (0, i) would have a value in the interval ]0, 1−r] which contradicts the assumption
in Case II and is treated in the next case instead. Therefore at least one inter-customer arc (j, i) must
have ﬂow 0 < q ≤1 −r which serves customer i. Either this means that at least one of the two types
of cumulative vehicle ﬂow on this arc (j, i) is non-integer or the cumulative drone ﬂow is 1. In the
111
==PAGE:117==
latter case for i not to be over-serviced it has to be the case that some drones were launched at j while
others served j after being launched at the depot. The only way for the drones that are launched at j
to have arrived at j without the existence of an inter-customer arc with non-integer cumulative vehicle
ﬂow is if there is both non-integer drone and truck ﬂow on the arc (0, j) which makes it Case III and
not Case II.
Case III)
Finally we address the case where there exists an arc (0, i) with non-integer cumulative truck ﬂow,
0 < rT < 1 and non-integer cumulative drone ﬂow 0 < rD < 1. Due to ﬂow conservation then the
truck ﬂow equal to rT must also leave customer i.
Figure 29a: Assume ﬁrst that an amount of truck ﬂow 0 < q ≤rT leaves on an inter-customer arc
(i, j). In order for the arc (i, j) to not have non-integer cumulative truck ﬂow then the following must
also happen: Truck ﬂow equal to 1 −q enters customer i from somewhere that is not the depot and
leaves on arc (i, j) such that the cumulative truck ﬂow on arc (i, j) is q + (1 −q) = 1. This means
that the total ﬂow entering customer i to serve it is (1 −q) + rT + rD but we know that 0 < q ≤rT
so (1 −q) + rT + rD ≥1 + rD which since rD > 0 would make the solution infeasible by over-serving
customer i. Therefore, the only way for the truck ﬂow of rT to leave customer i, without violating our
assumption, is via the arc (i, 0).
Knowing the above we move on to the drone ﬂow. Since sorties that start and end in the depot are
forbidden as a basic assumption in the VRPD then all of the drone ﬂow has to leave i to go to other
customers. This means that there will be at least one arc (i, j) with a ﬂow of these drones on it and
the ﬂow will be 0 < q ≤rD < 1 as illustrated in Figure 29b.
Next we take a step back to see what other drone ﬂow could be present on arc (i, j) by considering the
important question that we have delayed until now:
What is the value of rT + rD?
Figure 29c: Let us ﬁrst assume that rD +rT < 1 i.e. that the ﬂow of drones and trucks from the depot
into i is insuﬃcient to fully serve customer i. None of the remaining ﬂow into i can stem from a truck
ﬂow because such a truck ﬂow would have to come from a customer and the cumulative truck ﬂow on
that inter-customer arc would then be non-integer as it would lie in the interval ]0, 1−rT −rD[ ⊆]0, 1[.
Therefore there has to be a total, additional, ﬂow of drones that service customer i and come from
customers into i with ﬂow value rD′ = 1 −rD −rT or customer i is not fully serviced. Therefore there
has to exist at least one inter-customer arc (k, i) with a ﬂow of these drones of 0 < q ≤rD′. Since this
is an inter-customer arc then the cumulative drone ﬂow is not allowed to be non-integer so there must
be an additional ﬂow of drones equal to 1 −q ≥1 −rD′ on the arc (k, i). These drones do not service
customer i as i is already fully serviced (recall that rD + rT + rD′ = 1). Instead these drones will have
to be retrieved by trucks at customer i. This means that at least 1 −rD′ = rD + rT drones need to be
retrieved at customer i. The total truck ﬂow into and out of i is rT and therefore at most rT drones
can be retrieved which leads to a contradiction since rD + rT > rT .
Figure 29d:
Since the above assumption lead to a contradiction then it must be the case that
rD + rT = 1.
Looking at the arc (i, j) where some of the drones that serviced i are ﬂowing we
need to ensure that the cumulative drone ﬂow on this arc is integer. Since the ﬂow 0 < q ≤rD < 1 is
not itself integer this requires an additional ﬂow of drones which did not service customer i. The only
source of such drones are the drones belonging to the trucks that serviced customer i since they can
potentially be launched from there. Assume that an amount of these newly launched drones equal to
1 −q also cross arc (i, j) then the cumulative drone ﬂow is integer. Note here that since at most rT
drones can be launched at customer i then it is necessary that 1 −q ≤rT which is the same as saying
q ≥1 −rT which by our assumption that rT + rD = 1 means that we have q ≥rD while at the same
time also satisfying q ≤rD so the only option is to have q = rD in which case 1 −q = rT .
At customer j the drones that serviced customer i all have to be retrieved by their trucks so there has
to be a ﬂow of trucks into and out of j with value q. Together with the ﬂow of 1 −q drones launched
112
==PAGE:118==
at customer i these trucks serve customer j fully so no other trucks can enter this customer. Since q
is non-integer then to not have inter-customer arcs with non-integer ﬂow the trucks must come from
the depot and go there straight after. The drones that were launched at i also have to ﬂy to the depot
next since their trucks have gone back to the depot so they cannot rendezvous with them anywhere
else. This only leaves the q remaining drone ﬂow unaccounted for. If the q remaining drones were to
leave j via inter-customer arcs then the cumulative drone ﬂow on these arcs would be non-integer and
this could not be remedied with additional drone ﬂow since there are no drones available at customer
j. To launch more drones at customer j would require an additional truck ﬂow into j which, as we
discussed previously, is impossible. They therefore have to be carried back to the depot with their
trucks.
So far no contradiction has been reached but the solution is made up of two unique truck-drone
tandems, that service the same set of customers, where one has weight rD and the other has weight rT .
The total cost of the tandem with weight rD is (here exploiting the symmetry of the cost matrices):
crD = 2 · cT
0,j + cD
0,i + cD
i,j
Meanwhile the other tandem route has a cost of:
crT = 2 · cT
0,i + cD
0,j + cD
i,j
In an optimal solution the route with the lowest cost would be chosen with weight 1 and the other
with weight 0. Here route "rD" would be chosen with weight 1 if crT > crD and weight 0 if crT < crD.
In either of these cases we do not have any arcs with non-integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow at all. Finally
in the very special case where crT = crD any solution where the two weights rD and rT sum to 1
is optimal and feasible. However, knowing that the algorithm used to solve the master problem is a
variant of Simplex and that Simplex returns corner point feasible solutions we know that the optimal
solution would either be rD = 1, rT = 0 or rD = 0, rT = 1 since any combination in between lies on
the line between two corner-points and are not themselves CPF.
113
==PAGE:119==
(a) If truck ﬂow left customer i via an inter-customer
arc (i, j) then the only way to avoid non-integer
cumulative truck ﬂow on this arc is to over-service
customer i which is infeasible.
(b) All the truck ﬂow rT entering customer i has to
return to the depot immediately after to avoid arcs
with non-integer cumulative truck ﬂow. Meanwhile,
the drone ﬂow is not allowed to return to the depot
so there exists at least one inter-customer arc (i, j)
with non-integer drone ﬂow on it.
(c) Assuming that rT + rD < 1 leads to a
contradiction because either there exists an
inter-customer arc into i with non-integer cumulative
arc ﬂow or more drones need to be retrieved at i
than there is truck ﬂow to do it.
(d) Assuming rT + rD = 1 means that it is possible
to construct one speciﬁc solution which does not
lead to contradictions, however this is not a corner
point feasible solution unless either rT or rD is 1 and
the other 0.
Figure 29: Illustrations for the proof of Case III.
B
Generating initial columns
B.1
Generating initial columns prior to branching
Before the addition of branching constraints any customer, i ∈C can always at least be visited by a
truck performing the tour Depot →i →Depot shown on Figure 30a since nothing can make such tours
for the grid sizes we consider. Therefore, after making a route visiting some customers it is always
possible to visit the remaining customers with more routes, in the worst case each route just visits one
customer. The only thing that could make our initial columns infeasible would be if more columns
visit the same customer.
To generate good routes for our initial columns we used our solver for the PP (heuristic label removal),
114
==PAGE:120==
returning only the best column, where we then choose the λ-values to avoid re-visiting as follows:
Algorithm 6: Generating initial columns pre-branching
1 c = 2

maxi∈C{cT
0,i} + 0.001

2 Initialise: λ0 = 0, λi = c ∀i ∈C, Unvisited = C and Ainit = [ ]
3 while Unvisited ̸= ∅do
4
Solve PP with current values of λ to obtain column a.
5
Add column a to Ainit
6
Remove customers visited by a from Unvisited and update their λi as follows:
λi = −c
7 Return Ainit
The overall goal with our initial column generator is to construct routes that visit as many customers
as possible. c is chosen large enough to ensure that even visiting a single customer with the truck
gives a column with negative reduced cost. We update the λi values of customers that have been
visited by previous routes to ensure that it is never beneﬁcial to visit them with another route, not
even if visiting them with a truck would allow for a drone to be launched that could visit a further oﬀ
un-visited customer.
B.1.1
Modiﬁcation for ng-relaxation
When ng-relaxation is used for the PP a modiﬁed code for generating the initial columns is used. In
the modiﬁed code, the Ni sets for all customers are set to include all customers such that ng-relaxation
is temporarily suspended while generating the initial columns. This ensures that the initial columns
are always elementary routes.
(a) Before branching any customer could always be
visited by a simple truck route as depicted here
assuming the arc time costs are not close to Tmax.
(b) Branching constraints can make it infeasible for
the truck to travel from the depot to customer i in
which case we attempt to pair it with a buddy j
from which a drone can serve the hard customer i
Figure 30: The simplest routes that can be repeated to visit customers when attempting to ﬁnd an
initial feasible solution.
B.2
Generating initial columns with branching
With the addition of branching constraints it may now be infeasible to visit a customer i ∈C with
a truck route depot →i →depot if for instance we have branched 0 on customer i or branched 1 on
a drone arc incident to i. This means that our previous strategy for generating initial columns can
115
==PAGE:121==
fail at ﬁnding an initial feasible solution. Consider for instance if the previously generated columns in
Ainit have visited all customers except i such that Unvisited = {i} but i is a customer which may not
be served by the truck. Then it is impossible to serve customer i with a new route without re-visiting
a customer who has already been served by a diﬀerent route.
How we combat this is to go trough multiple levels of initial column generators where the ﬁrst layers
prioritise generating columns of good quality but without a guarantee that they can ﬁnd a feasible
initial solution.
After attempting to generate columns in a layer we check whether the generated
columns, Ainit sum row-wise to 1. If they do it is possible to solve the RMP now and start column
generation. If not we call upon the next layer of initial column generation.
Layer 1: Long routes
In the ﬁrst layer of generating initial column we attempt to use the generator from algorithm 6 with
the modiﬁcation that customers that are harder to visit, are given a larger λi-value to motivate visiting
them as early in the process as possible. "Harder to visit" here covers customers that cannot be visited
by a truck from the depot and customers where the drone is only allowed to enter the customer from
few other customers due to branching on drone arcs.
Layer 2: The buddy pairing initial column generator
With this generator we give less freedom to how customers are allowed to be visited in order to avoid
infeasibility across otherwise feasible routes.
First we identify all the customers who cannot be visited by a truck from the depot. Then we iden-
tify from which other customers we are allowed to send a drone into each of these "hard customers"
and back to the depot, i.e. a solution as depicted in Figure 30b. With this information we solve a
set-packing problem where each "hard customer" is assigned up to one "buddy" which is a non-hard
customer that a drone could be launched from and go to the hard customer. Each non-hard customer
is allowed to be buddy for at most one hard customer. With the pairings between hard customers and
buddies the basic idea of our generator is similar to in layer 1 except the λ-values are chosen with more
details:
116
==PAGE:122==
Algorithm 7: "Buddy pairing" initial column generator
1 c =

maxi∈C{cT
0,i} + 0.001

2 From the set-packing problem solution:
3 Set of hard customers that are unvisited: H ⊆C
4 Set of non-hard customers that are unvisited NH ⊆C
5 Array, buddy, with an entry for each hard customer in H. If buddyi = −1 then i ∈H has no
buddy otherwise buddyi > 0 is an index of a non-hard customer.
6 Ainit = [ ]
7 while (H ∪NH) ̸= ∅do
8
if maxi∈H{buddyi} > 0 then
9
λ0 = 0, λi = 2c ∀i ∈C
10
Select a hard customer h which has buddy buddyh
11
Set λh = 4c, λbuddyh = 3c
12
Additionally set λi = −4c for all customers i that are buddies to the remaining hard
customers other than h.
13
For all hard customers i ̸= h which do not have a buddy, if buddyh is one of the customers
from which a drone may perform sortie buddyh 99K i 99K (n + 1) then set λi = 3c
14
else
15
λ0 = 0, λi = 2c ∀i ∈NH, λi = 4c ∀i ∈N
16
Solve PP heuristically with current values of λ to obtain column a.
17
Add column a to Ainit
18
Remove customers visited in a from H and NH
19 Return Ainit
The basic idea in this initial column generator is to select one hard customer h and make it beneﬁcial
for a tour to visit this hard customer and its buddy while making it very disadvantageous to visit any
of the customers who are buddies to other hard customers. Any other hard customers that could use
this customer as their missing buddy are given a slightly larger dual variable than customers that are
easy to visit. The goal with this generator is to avoid making the hard customers impossible to visit
by ensuring that we can always make the simple route that only visits the hard customer and their
buddy from Figure 30b.
Layer 3: Dummy solution
The two previous layers do not guarantee to ﬁnd a set of columns Ainit that make the RMP solvable
even if feasible solutions do exist. In the cases where neither of the previous layers succeed we instead
turn to a dummy solution which is a column with all ones, i.e. it visits all customers but has an
associated cost of:
cdummy = 1 + 2 ·
X
i∈C
cT
0,i
We chose this cost because the most expensive route there could exist for any instance would be to visit
each customer with a separate truck and the objective value of such a solution would be cdummy −1.
Any solution which visit more than one customer with the truck is cheaper due to the triangle inequality
and even cheaper if a drone is launched since the drone is only used if it is beneﬁcial, otherwise it could
simply be carried by the truck and never be launched.
When column generation terminates because no more improving columns are found we check whether
the optimal solution to the RMP has an objective value strictly less than cdummy in which case it is a
feasible solution, otherwise no feasible solutions exist.
The reason we only use the dummy solution as a last resort is that for regular VRP they can have a
bad inﬂuence on the dual variables, which could result in many iterations spend on generating poor
columns.
