E
Details on the events at a rendezvous point in our MIP
Recall that we used variables ai and di ∀i ∈V to keep track of time in our MILP from section 3.5. We
refer to the variables ai as the "availability time" at customer i. It is the time at which the customer
has been served and any potential drone recovering have been performed. Meanwhile, the variable di
is referred to as the "departure time". It is the time at which any potential drone launching has been
performed such that all vehicles are ready to leave the customer. If a drone is not launched at a node
i ∈V then ai = di.
Figure 32 illustrates on a time-scale the order of events at a combined customer where the drone is
recovered and launched again both in the case where the truck arrives ﬁrst (top image) and the case
where the drone arrives ﬁrst (bottom image). Included in the ﬁgure are the parameters tT , tD reﬂecting
travel time for the truck/drone as well as R, sT , L which are the recover time, service time for the truck
and launch time parameters.
(a) Truck arrives ﬁrst
(b) Drone arrives ﬁrst
Figure 32: Order of events at a combined customer where a drone is both recovered and launched
where either (a) The truck arrives ﬁrst or (b) The drone arrives ﬁrst. On the illustrations the two
times ai and di have been indicated as well as the parameters tT , tD, R, sT , L (the truck/drone travel
times, recover time, service time for truck and launch time).
When modelling the drone battery it is the time between ai and ak which we do not allow to exceed
the endurance if a drone performs sortie i 99K j 99K k.
When we made this modelling choice we noted that our model is then unable to fully reﬂect the
situation in Figure 32b. In real life one would assume the drones battery to not be drained in the
waiting time between it has been recovered and is launched again, however the way we model the
battery it is being drained all the way up to time ak no matter which order recovering and servicing is
done in. Our model thus always reﬂects that the order of events at a combined customer, i is:
1. When the truck arrives at i, i is served taking time sT
2. When any drone that needs to be recovered has arrived and i has been served the drone is
recovered taking time R. The time at this point is denoted ai
120
==PAGE:126==
3. Immediately after time ai a drone may be launched taking time L and the time at this point is
denoted di
An illustration of this is shown in Figure 33a where by the "battery’s perspective" we mean the above
discussed consequence on how events are treated.
(a) Event order in our model
(b) Event order in [Sacramento et al., 2019].
Figure 33: Order of events at a combined customer as handled by our model compared to in
[Sacramento et al., 2019].
In our performance reference paper [Sacramento et al., 2019] time is measured by variables tv
i where
i are the nodes and v indicates that it is the time upon which all vehicles (trucks and drones) have
arrived at the node and the drone has been recovered if necessary. The battery is constrained to last
from a time tv
i to time tv
k if the drone performs sortie i 99K j 99K k. Going back to the situation
in Figure 32b if a drone was recovered at customer i and subsequently launched to perform sortie
i 99K j 99K k then the time taken by the truck to service customer i would be included in the time that
the battery is drained. Thus in [Sacramento et al., 2019] the eﬀective order of events at a combined
customer i as treated by the battery constraints is:
1. As soon as both truck and drone have arrived at i the drone is recovered if necessary taking time
R. The time at this point is denoted tv
i .
2. After time tv
i the drone may be launched taking time L
3. After having ﬁnished interacting with the drone, the truck has to service customer i taking time
sT .
Ultimately how the battery is being constrained in our model and in [Sacramento et al., 2019] leads
to the same. If we consider a drone sortie i 99K j 99K k where the drone was launched in i, served
customer j alone and was recovered by the truck again in k the calculation for how much the battery
was drained is the same:
In Sacramento et. al.:
Starting at time tv
i when the drone is powered on, ﬁrst the drone is launched taking time L. Then
the drone ﬂies to node k via node j and services this node such that at the time it arrives at node
k it has spent time equal to L + tD
ij + sD + tD
jk. Meanwhile the truck ﬁrst has to service customer i
before leaving and then travel to node k meaning that at the time of arrival at k it has spent time
L + sT + tT
ik. Before the drone can be powered oﬀit has to be recovered and the recovery only starts
once both vehicles have arrived at k meaning that the total time spent between tv
i and tv
k is:
Tsac = L + max{tD
ij + sD + tD
jk , sT + tT
ik} + R
In our model:
Starting at time ai the drone ﬁrst has to be launched taking time L. Now both drone and truck can
leave. Upon arriving at node k the drone has spent time L + tD
ij + sD + tD
jk, while when the truck
arrives it has spent time L + tT
ik. Before the drone can be powered oﬀ, customer k needs to be served
121
==PAGE:127==
and the drone needs to be recovered. The service starts as soon as the truck arrives so the truck will
have spent time L + tT
ik + sT when it is ready to recover and therefore the total time between time ai
and ak is:
Tours = L + max{tD
ij + sD + tD
jk , sT + tT
ik} + R
In section 4.2 we experimented with a modiﬁed model where the constraints on the drone’s battery
allow the battery to not be drained during the time the truck spends serving a customer if the drone
can be recovered ﬁrst as in Figure 32b.
F
A more formal proof of integrality of solution when ensuring integer
cumulative vehicle ﬂow
This is a proof by contradiction. Assume that a CPF solution to the MP has the property that the
cumulative vehicle ﬂow as introduced in section 9.1 is integer for all arcs (i, j) ∈A but the weights of
the routes in the RMP solution, ψr, are not all integer. Speciﬁcally we will denote one of the routes
for which 0 < ψr < 1, by r′.
Since for each tandem route r ∈R it is a requirement that the truck and the drone both leave the
start-depot then there is guaranteed to exist at least one arc (i, j) ∈A which is traversed by the truck
from route r′, and by our assumption it will have a cumulative truck ﬂow of 1 even though ψr′ < 1.
Therefore, at least one other route’s truck also traverses this arc.
We will assume without loss of generality that there is one route, r∗, but the argument can be repeated
for any number of routes. Note that 0 < ψr∗< 1 and ψr′ + ψr∗= 1.
r∗has to deviate from r′ in some way or it would not be a CPF solution to choose route r′ with weight
0 < ψr′ < 1. Instead one of the identical routes would be chosen with weight 1 and all other identical
routes with weight 0 which would contradict the assumption.
Therefore there must exist a customer, i from which the two routes diverge. Denote the arcs used
by the routes (i, j′) and (i, j∗) for r′ and r∗respectively. It may be the truck of the two routes that
traverse these arcs or the drones. First we will assume that it is the trucks.
Since we assumed that the cumulative vehicle ﬂow on all arcs is integer then it must also be on these
two arcs. However, since 0 < ψr′ < 1 then the only way that the cumulative truck ﬂow on arc (i, j′) is
integer is if the truck from a third route uses this arc, but then customer i would be over-visited and
the solution would not be feasible. The same can be said about arc (i, j∗).
Thus we conclude that if the truck-paths diﬀer at all, then all our assumptions cannot hold true at
once, so the truck-paths must be identical.
This leads us to the case where the truck-paths are identical but the drone-paths are not:
We now consider the case where the two routes r′ and r∗diverge at i because the ﬁrst drone traverses
arc (i, j′) but the latter (i, j∗). Note that i may be part of the truck routes of r′ and r∗in which case
both drones are launched in i or i may be a customer which is served by the two drones by sorties that
started the same but end diﬀerently.
For the arc (i, j′) to have cumulative ﬂow 1, it has to be the case that the drone from a third route
also uses this arc. Recall that a drone can only use an arc by being either launched or recovered in
one end-point, and note that since ψr′ + ψr∗= 1 we know that no other trucks can be present in any
customer along the truck-path of these routes.
In the case that i is in the truck-path of r′ and r∗then the third drone would have to either also be
launched in i or have served i as the mid-point of a sortie. It being launched in i is impossible because
it would require a third truck to also be there. It serving i is impossible since the two trucks already
fully serve i.
122
==PAGE:128==
In the case that i is not in the truck-path the arguments above can be repeated but reversed. The
third drone would have to be either launched in i, meaning it would serve j′ or it would serve i and
be recovered in j′. Both of these are impossible for the same reasons mentioned above. Therefore arc
(i, j′) cannot have integer cumulative ﬂow, which is a contradiction. Similar arguments could be made
for arc (i, j∗).
Thus we conclude that ensuring integer cumulative vehicle ﬂow on all arcs is suﬃcient to ensure that
each route is chosen with integer weight in a CPF solution to the MP.
G
Experiments on dual stabilisation
G.1
Very ﬁrst attempt of tuning parameter values
In these experiments we solved all 12 instances of size 12 with 125 diﬀerent triples for the values of
(ε, αε, αλ) in the modiﬁed half-box dual stabilisation framework.
The values tested where all combinations of:
ε = [0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 0.9]
αε = [1.1, 1.5, 2.0, 3.0, 5.0]
αλ = [1.1, 1.5, 2.0, 3.0, 5.0]
Two diﬀerent versions of column generation were used to solve all instances with the 125 diﬀerent
parameter values. Both versions use modiﬁed half box dual stabilisation and both versions solve the
PP with two levels of label removal namely level 1 heuristic and exact. However, one version uses
ng-relaxation with Ni sets of size 5 while the other uses plain dynamic programming.
Heat-maps of the cumulative run times when solving all instances with each parameter triple are
shown in Figure 34 for the plain version and 35 for the version with ng-relaxation. Since the data is
4-dimensional we have only shown a subset of the results. Speciﬁcally we have identiﬁed the triple
which give the shortest cumulative run time and then we have shown 3 plots where each plot shows a
slice of how the run times change when we lock one parameter value and change the others.
For the plain version the best triple was (ε, αε, αλ) = (0.7, 1.1, 1.5) while for the version with ng-
relaxation the best triple was (0.1, 1.1, 1.5). The best triples have been indicated on the heat-map
"slices" with a green star.
123
==PAGE:129==
Figure 34: Dual stabilisation results for the plain version of column generation.
124
==PAGE:130==
Figure 35: Dual stabilisation results for the version of column generation with ng-relaxation.
From the bottom two heat-maps both in Figure 34 and in Figure 34 it seems that the value of αλ has
less of an impact on performance than the other two parameters. Meanwhile, looking at the bottom two
heat-maps still, there is not a monotonically increasing or decreasing performance in either direction
when moving away from the parameter value that gave the best results. The only clear conclusion is
that ε = 0.9 and αε = 5.0 are generally poor. Looking at the top heat-maps there seems to be a trend
of low αε giving good results but other than that is is hard to make any clear conclusions because the
125
==PAGE:131==
diﬀerence in cumulative run time from using one triple to another is very small. With larger instances
we could hope to see a more spread-out picture of the performance.
H
Experiments relating to implementation speciﬁcs
H.1
Impact of re-arranging checks in dominance
Below we experimented with changing the order in which we check the dominance conditions, such
that the intersections are done as late as possible, since they have shown to be time-consuming. We
report number of iterations and exact iterations to illustrate that changing this order does not change
the process, merely optimises it.
We choose to make this experiment using ng−relaxation with Ni sets of size 12, since this entails the
most intersections such that the diﬀerence is exacerbated.

H.2
Data-type for Sng and Ni
Previous proﬁling results of our column generation framework without dual stabilisation but with ng
relaxation showed that most of the time spend was spent on exact label removal (dominance) and in
particular the lines in which the intersection of a label’s Sng-set with another label’s Sng-set seemed
to take up a lot of time, this kind of intersection also takes place when propagating, in particular we
intersect a label’s Sng-set with a customer’s Ni-set.
127
==PAGE:133==
Motivated by these results we experimented with 3 diﬀerent data types for the label entry Sng and the
sets Ni. To illustrate how these data types diﬀer we have shown for each of them how the update of
Sng in the last iteration of Figure 11 would be performed, i.e. the update:
Sng = ({1, 2, 3} ∩{2, 3}) ∪{4}
Data structure for Sng, Ni
Sng update example
Sng = [1 1 1 0]. ∗[0 1 1 0]
BitArray
Sng[4] = 1
Result: Sng = [0 1 1 1]
Sng = ({2, 1, 3} ∩{3, 2}) = {2, 3}
BitSet
Sng = {2, 3} ∪{4}
Result: Sng = {4, 2, 3}
Sng = 7&6 = 6
Int64
Sng = 6∥8
Result: Sng = 14
Table 27 contains the results when solving all small instances with ng-relaxation that has Ni sets of
size 8, no dual stabilisation and 4 diﬀerent versions of the code where Sng and Ni are the three diﬀerent
data types presented above.
Note that the data type BitSet in Julia has no ordering as also attempted to illustrate in the table
above. This means that when propagating to all customers in the set 1, ..., n\Sng, the order in which
diﬀerent customers are attempted to propagate to is unpredictable (though consistent). This unpre-
dictability aﬀects the column generation process because when removing labels using either heuristic
or exact label removal the order in which two equally good partial solutions were generated makes the
diﬀerence between which label is kept and which is removed. This is particularly important for the
heuristic label removal as it only considers the value of partial solutions and not their opportunities so
two partial solutions visiting the same customers in reverse order are exactly identical in the eyes of
the heuristic label remover.
We can see the consequence of the altered process in the iteration column because all other experiments
go through the same process and number of iterations when solving an instance except for BitSet.
With BitSet Sorted we sort the customers by changing from the BitSet data type to a sorted array
before propagating, such that we can recreate the same column generation procedure as for the other
two data types to get a more fair comparison.

Table 27: Solving the same instances using 4 diﬀerent versions of the code with ng-relaxation to test
the best data format for Sng and Ni.
Looking at the results in Table 27 we see that the version of the code with sorted BitSets uses least
time overall and is even faster per iteration than the version of the code where the BitSets are not
sorted. This last observation can be explained by coincidence as the sorted code may have just been
lucky with the column generation process, but it also might be the case that the code runs faster per
iteration because customers which are propagated to are extracted from an array and not from a BitSet
and the former may be easier to access for the CPU.
All in all we conclude that BitSet seems to be the best data type for this purpose although not
immensely better than the alternatives. Additionally we decide to go with the sorted BitSet code as
there is less unpredictability with it and in these tests it was faster per iteration.
We use the same data type for the version of the code without ng-relaxation, but chose to decide
the data type based on the ng-relaxed version as more operations are performed on the Sng-sets and
therefore a better data type will have a larger impact here.
H.3
Testing an NG variant
In section 8.1 we introduced ng−route relaxation, and presented the changes that this would require
to our handling of the S sets. These changes are re-stated again below:
129
==PAGE:135==
(Sng, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
truck arc
======⇒
∀j /∈Sng
((Sng ∩Nj) ∪{iD} ∪{j}, ...)
(Sng, iT , iT , τ, θ, f)
combined arc
========⇒
∀j /∈Sng
((Sng ∩Nj) ∪{j}, ...)
(Sng, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
drone sortie
========⇒
∀j /∈Sng∪C
D
((Sng ∩NiT ) ∪{j}, ...)
This is slightly diﬀerent from how it is done in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020]. Since the authors are
the inventors of ng−route relaxation we decide to also test out the way that they do it. In particular
it pertains to the time at which intersection with the Ni set occurs. The change is presented below.
(Sng, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
truck arc
======⇒
∀j /∈Sng
(((Sng ∪{j}) ∩Nj) ∪{iD}, ...)
(Sng, iT , iT , τ, θ, f)
combined arc
========⇒
∀j /∈Sng
((Sng ∪{j}) ∩Nj, ...)
(Sng, iT , iD, τ, θ, f)
drone sortie
========⇒
∀j /∈Sng∪C
D
((Sng ∪{j}) ∩NiT , ...)
The only practical diﬀerence from our deﬁnitions to theirs is that we do not allow the customer just
served by a sortie to be forgotten while in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] it is allowed if j /∈NiT .
Pros and cons:
Our considerations for why we did not allow the route to forget the customer j just served by the
drone sortie is that we feel it goes against the notion, that non-elementary ng-routes will generally be
bad because they require a far oﬀcustomer to be visited in-between re-visits to the same customer.
Speciﬁcally, allowing the customer j to be forgotten immediately upon completing the sortie does not
require any other customers to be visited in the meantime, instead the propagation following immedi-
ately after could already return to j.
It seems clear, however that whether to allow or prohibit the customer j from being forgotten im-
mediately, presents the returning trade-oﬀof whether to prioritise decreasing computational com-
plexity or good LP bounds.
With the way we implemented ng-relaxation the Sng sets may more
easily contain a large number of customers, making dominance more restricted. While in the way of
[Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020], it is possible for the route to forget the customer that was just visited,
allowing more propagations already in the next generation. Thus the set of routes that can be gener-
ated by the ng-relaxation in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020] is larger than what can be generate with
our ng-relaxation. This leads to a larger solution space thus we expect that it could lead to lower
objective values i.e. poorer LP bounds.
Empirical results from where we ran two versions of column generation with either our version of ng-
relaxation or ng-relaxation as implemented with the way Sng is updated in [Roberti and Ruthmair, 2020]
are shown below:

We see that, indeed, there are a couple instances in which the objective value is lower for this variant.

since the goal is to implement Branch and Bound, where poorer LP bounds is potentially detrimental,
we decide to stick with our original version.
I
Experiments to improve bi-directional labelling computational per-
formance
I.1
Order of merges in bi-directional labelling
As another attempt to optimise we experimented with merging labels in diﬀerent orders when doing
bi-directional labelling. We did this because it enables us to make a stricter check regarding whether
each a label has potential to become one of the ones we keep. Below we tried 4 diﬀerent orders. Note
that the instances we run this experiment on are not the usual ones of size 14. In particular the ones
with grid-size 5 have been exchanged for ones of grid size 40.
The numbers refer to the diﬀerent orders:
Order 0:
1. Glue merge with two non-ﬁnished half routes.
2. Glue merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
3. Sortie merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
4. Glue merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
5. Combined arc merge (requires two ﬁnished half-routes).
6. Sortie merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
Order 1:
1. Sortie merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
2. Sortie merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
3. Glue merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
4. Combined arc merge (requires two ﬁnished half-routes).
5. Glue merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
6. Glue merge with two non-ﬁnished half routes.
Order 2:
1. Glue merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
2. Glue merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
3. Glue merge with two non-ﬁnished half routes.
4. Combined arc merge (requires two ﬁnished half-routes).
5. Sortie merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
132
==PAGE:138==
6. Sortie merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
Order 3:
1. Combined arc merge (requires two ﬁnished half-routes).
2. Glue merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
3. Glue merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
4. Glue merge with two non-ﬁnished half routes.
5. Sortie merge with two ﬁnished half routes.
6. Sortie merge with one non-ﬁnished and one ﬁnished half route.
Merge order:

Table 29: Attempts to re-organise the order in which merges are performed once propagations are

I.2
Dominating backwards labels with the forwards labels in bi-directional la-
belling
Below we show the results that made bi-directional labelling an improvement. Namely we needed to
also put the backwards labels through the dominance procedure using the forwards labels to dominate
133
==PAGE:139==
the backwards labels. As mentioned the forwards labels can dominate the backwards labels but not
vice versa. The three right columns refer to three diﬀerent ways of doing this and the reference refers
to when we only dominated backwards labels using backwards labels.
In particular the three ways are:
1. Dominate the backwards labels using the forwards labels and then the backwards labels sepa-
rately.
2. Dominate the backwards labels using the backwards labels and then the forwards labels sepa-
rately.
3. Make one list of all the labels and use this to dominate the backwards labels.

Table 30: Comparing three diﬀerent ways of dominating backwards labels using forwards labels, with
a reference in which backwards labels are only dominated using other backwards labels.
Note that since all the three ways of dominating result in the same exact label being left we have
only include the process results once. The blue row is because the fwbw had a diﬀerent process but
same result in the end
From table 30 we conclude that order 1 and order 2 are essentially identical, while order 3 is noticeably
worse. We choose order 1 since it feels more intuitive to us.
I.3
Sorting half-route labels before merging
We experimented with sorting the labels before merging when doing bi-directional labelling. In par-
ticular we sorted each list of labels, with the same iT , iD, by their reduced cost. We did this because
it enabled us to make a stricter check regarding whether this label even has potential to become the
best label. If it does not we skip it and the rest of the list, since no further label (which all have larger
reduced costs) could then have potential.

Table 31: Comparing with and without sorting of labels before merging them. Sorting is done for
each iT , iD-list.
From table 31 we conclude that sorting the labels is worthwhile and thus it is incorporated in the
standard procedure. Note that the run-times look very diﬀerent from other results presented on bi-
directional labelling. This is because these experiments were run on code that did not include the
procedure of dominating the backwards labels as described in the section above.
Since this only
reduces the number of labels and does not change any algorithmic procedure we conclude that the
results from this section holds regardless.
J
First attempt at tuning the number of columns from PP
Below in Table 32 are our initial attempts at tuning the number of columns to return from the PP.
In these initial attempts the number of labels kept in the 1st level heuristic was unchanged (5). We
135
==PAGE:141==
expect that this can explain why returning 5 or 6 columns does not reduce the overall run time despite
decreasing the number of iterations since choosing 5 or more columns when there are only 5 labels pr
generation to choose from, makes it very likely that the columns returned are closely related and thus
adds complexity to the RMP without being useful as they cannot be chosen in a solution at the same
time.
